• General Reasoning Guide Pre-Alpha v0.4.0
    Last updated: July 7th

    • Guide guide

      • Intro

        • This guide is based on classical logic.
          • If you're looking for a guide on reasoning that explicitly includes non-classical logic, you'll have to look elsewhere.
        • FAQs

      • Meanings

        • Color meanings

          • COLORLESS

          • ^ Descriptions or largely plain text mostly.

          • RED

          • ^ Psychology / Limitation / Detriment / Counterargument
            • Limitations don't necessarily lower the importance of a topic. Occasionally, they can even raise the importance, especially when it's important to understand the topic to better understand its limitations.
            • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=kKQAr5M3XUuysAL4VVWIGYza

          • ORANGE

          • ^ Practical / Informal stuff that doesn't fit in the other categories (aside from "other").

          • YELLOW

          • ^ Semantics / Meaning / Link

          • GREEN

          • ^ Induction / Benefit / Supporting argument
            • In supporting/countering argument chains, green/red only indicates the argument's relationship with its parent, not the argument's "side" at the broadest level.

          • BLUE

          • ^ Deduction / Structure / Formal

          • PURPLE

          • ^ Category / Other / Example

        • Structure meanings

          • Meta

            • Overview

              • Definition

                • There are a few common categories in many items in the guide, like "definition" and "importance". These typically have smaller text and are like the overview of the category.

              • Where / appearance

                • Most "overview" sections can be found at the top, but the related section is always at the bottom.

            • Main sections

              • Definition

                • There are items specific to the item you're under, which usually have bigger titles.

                  • Like this

                  • Or this

                  • But still sometimes this

                • These usually have most of the content.

              • Where / appearance

                • They usually come after the top overview sections.

              • Order

                • These are typically in descending order of importance and/or intended reading order.
                  • So it's typically best to read from top to bottom of the main sections if you will read them.

            • Misc

              • Oftentimes (as referenced in color meanings) red and green items will represent arguments for/against (or benefits/detriments of) their parent.
                • Example:
                • color meanings clearly states that they can be benefits/detriments and [supporting arguments] or counterarguments.
                • It would be quite confusing to use the same color to mean very different things, and green is used for induction and red is used for psychology. So color meanings is likely wrong.
                  • Dynalist only has 6 colors, so it is an unfortunate compromise.

              • Non-hidden details

                • Sometimes items will have details that you don't need to expand to see.
                • ^ Example:
                • ^ This is an example.
                • These will always be denoted using the ^ symbol.
            • The notes (small, grey text) under most items here explain what the kinds of items mean throughout the rest of the guide.
            • Inside of each item with a note is info about this topic (structure meanings) that fits within the type of parent item it is in.
              • This acts as both examples of how these kinds of items typically work, and data about this topic.

          • Definition (specific)
            The definition(s) of/[relevant to] the topic. Often the word(s) in the parentheses (if there are any) will give a rough idea of the kind of definition being used.

            • Explanation of how the general reasoning guide is organized and what different parts of sections mean.

            • Synonym(s)
              Other term(s) that mean the same thing.

            • Distinction(s)
              Important distinction(s) between this concept and (a) similar one(s).

          • Importance / Limitations
            Reasons for why this topic is important, and what limitations/drawbacks of the topic to keep in mind. Will typically be in roughly descending order of importance for the particular topic.

            • Impactful
              Reasons for why this topic is likely impactful to one's values when it does come up.

              • Getting a better understanding of what the different sections mean can help with navigation.
              • Getting a better understanding of what the different sections mean can help with reading comprehension (of this specific guide).
              • The general reasoning guide is still probably a bit confusing to follow even with this little tutorial.

            • Common
              Reasons for why this topic is frequently relevant.

              • Most of this guide follows this format.
              • Almost no reasoning guides follow formats like this.

            • Difficult
              Reasons for why this topic is frequently difficult, and thus reading the section is likely worthwhile.

              • The structure is unique.
              • Some of the terms are misleading.
                • "Definition(s)" can also include synonym(s).
                • "Definitions" includes multiple definitions, sometimes both recommended and unrecommended definitions.
                  • This is partially to explain why to avoid certain definitions.
                  • This is partially to avoid the potential for conflations.
                    • This is achieved by showing the difference between definitions that might otherwise be conflated.

            • Improvable
              Reasons for why this topic is likely practically and substantially improvable.

              • You probably haven't read all of "structure meanings" yet.

            • Accurate
              Reasons for why this topic is accurate, if it's about a claim.

          • Nuances
            The substantial nuances of the topic.

          • Reducible
            How the topic is reducible (at least to a noteworthy degree) to a more central/important subject.

          • How to view it
            A description of how to look at the topic in general.

            • An optional tutorial to understand the structure of the general reasoning guide better.

          • Items

            • We call every bit of text paired with a bullet point an "item".

          • Sections

            • Almost every colored item with children (that isn't an argument for/against its parent or an example) is a section.
            • These are the big categories present in topics, or the topics themselves, or the categories of topics, etc.

            • Main sections

              • Sections which contain other section(s).

          • [Square brackets]

            • Square brackets are used to specify the "order of operations" of the sentence. You can think of them as being similar to (parentheses) in math.

            • Examples

          • Related
            Topics (usually linked) that are related (typically closely), but usually not very important to immediately visit.

            • Node order

              • Typically (at a high level) the node order is roughly sorted in descending order of importance.

                • Definition

                  • In a set of sibling nodes (without considering parents or children) more important nodes will tend to be higher up while less important nodes will tend to be lower down.

                • Example

                  • High importance

                  • Medium importance

                  • Low importance

              • Oftentimes the node order is instead roughly sorted in order of what should be read sooner/later.

                • Example

                  • Ideally read first

                  • Ideally read third
                    Keyword: roughly

                  • Ideally read second
                    Keyword: roughly

                  • Ideally read fourth

            • Specific definitions

          • Source
            Sources, if less fitting to place under definition.

        • Less important meanings

          • You can skip these.

          • Note meanings

            • General

              • Notes are often used to covey little bits of information that seem useful

            • Definitions

              • "Here" means that the definition is specific to the topic it's used in, and doesn't apply to things beyond that.

            • Importance/limitations notes

              • Likelihood: The item supports/contests the parent's likelihood.
              • Relevance: The item supports/contests the parent's relevance/impact.
              • Addressed: This item has been addressed, at the end of the current reasoning it seems to be inadequately supported to make much of a difference.

            • Reasoning level terms

              • You'll notice a lot of notes like "~advanced", these are vague labels meant to make it easier for me to create the guide guide and/or create versions of the guide which include/exclude more complex/easily misunderstood parts in the future.

          • Unique/niche definitions

            • Unusual definitions that are used here

          • Text size meanings


            • Note
              • Note meanings
            • Normal text
              • Normal text is typically used for the main content of the guide. You'll find it when you dig deep enough in most categories.

            • Headings

              • Headings are typically used for categories.
              • Heading size typically doesn't mean much, it's often arbitrary for the sake of making text look/wrap nicer.
              • But usually the standard categories present in many places throughout the guide are given the smallest heading (heading 3), while categories specific to the parent category are given the largest heading (heading 1).
              • Links are one of the more common exceptions to this, since the text in links doesn't wrap. They are often given heading 3 even if they are specific to the parent category.
              • Sometimes relative importance will be used to make important categories seem bigger and unimportant categories seem smaller.
                • This is common for categories of main categories.
                  • Categories of main categories are usually colored purple.

              • Examples

                • Heading 3

                • Heading 2

                • Heading 1

      • Tips

        • Reading tips

          • Read it like a very general service manual, not a book

            • Reading it like a generally useful service manual

              • It saves time

            • Reading it like a very specialized service manual

              • This is likely to cause you not to read much of it, which is likely to cause you to miss a lot of very generally important things.

            • Reading it like a book

              • You're unlikely to retain the vast majority of the info.
                • Unlike many similar guides and learning resources in general, this one intentionally tries to minimize repetition.
                  • Repetition helps a lot with being able to retain info.
                    Relevance
                • People tend to feel like they will retain way more info from books than they actually do.
                • There are memory techniques to retain a lot more info than one otherwise would.
                  • Most people have not learned to use these techniques very well.
              • Likely to involve reading a lot of largely useless info.
                • Much of the guide is meant to be skipped by the vast majority of people, just intended to clarify things for the minority who needs it.
                  Likelihood
                  • We are all members of those minorities in some cases, but usually not most cases.
                • This would likely make the experience more boring.
                  Relevance, maybe link to boredom under motivation or something.
                • The time could instead be spent practicing and/or re-reading the more important things.
                  Links to practice and re-reading

            • Related

              • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=hcPLgV8Us5L8ci7jwCugXrQw

              • Deciding what to skip

              • Why non-linear format

          • Deciding what to skip

            • Already read/visited

              • Rereading parts of sections in the context of other sections.
                • It can be helpful for getting a better grasp on how they are connected.
              • Rereading sections to better remember them.
              • Rereading sections to better understand them.

            • Will read/visit later

              • Maybe

                • Typically you should be careful about skipping things with the intention of reading them later, since you might forget.

              • Interesting / useful

                • If you're in a section that seems very interesting / useful to you, you'll probably want to at least check roughly what the things you're planning on skipping look like, in case it's not in the reading guide and you forget to come back to it.

              • Notes

                • Feel free to take notes about what sections you would like to come back to.

              • Related

                • "Related" sections are used largely to say "unless you want to learn this topic thoroughly, it's ok to skip for now".
                  So they should generally be things that you'll either already have read, read in the future, or rightfully completely skip.
                • When trying to learn a topic thoroughly, it's often useful to think about how related topics connect (to build an intuition of how the topic applies in various areas and/or what it can be boiled down to), and thus it can be useful to follow the links.

              • Link-only items

                • When an item has only link(s), it should be read as though all the links were in a "related" category.

            • Potentially permanent skip

              • Unless your chosen reading order suggests to skip it, it's generally best to read almost all main sections (linked sections are the main exception, more on that below).

              • Links

                • Definition

                  • Permanently skipping linked sections.

                • In "related"
                  Or other categories if there are other categories for links.

                  • Most links will be put here.
                  • These are typically fine to skip, but for more important sections it's often worthwhile to first check if there's anything that seems worth checking out.

                • Main links

                  • Typically if you're going to do this, you should at least check its "importance" section (if it has one) and make sure you find the reasons insufficiently compelling to be worth considering further.

                    • It's easy

                      • When the topic is unimportant, it's typically easy to tell based on its "importance" section or lack thereof.
                      • Easy to return to where you were via back/forward page
                      • "Importance" sections are typically not very long.

                    • It's important

                      • People very often overestimate their knowledge/abilities in many of these areas and have permanent and large reasoning weaknesses as a result.
                      • Many of the areas are much more important than they seem.

            • Category-specific

              • Definitions

                • Generally if the definition is unusual or confusing, there will be a word in parentheses next to it.
                  • The word is used to describe why you might not know the definition being used even if you initially think that you do.
                • ^ Example:

                • Definition (unusual)

                • If there isn't a word like this and it's just like normal and you think you understand it, it's probably fine to skip.

              • Importance

                • If you think the topic is:

                • Very important

                  • You can generally skip over the importance section.

                • Somewhat or pretty important

                  • It's likely still worth taking a look if it's a big section, because there's likely sufficient evidence there to suggest that the topic is more important than you think.

                • Not very important, or unimportant

                  • You almost certainly want to check out the importance section, as it likely has things you haven't considered about how important it is.

              • Limitations

                • Limitations are usually important to read even if you already feel like you understand the limitations.
                • ^ There are often more than you would expect or unique angles we present for them.

              • Related

                • These sections can usually be skipped without missing too much.
                  • They mostly contain links to other sections or (relatively) less important things.
                • They are intended for people who really want to get a deep grasp of the topic.
                  • The links (and common accompanying explanations) help intuitively grasp important relations with other topics.

          • Thinking of applications

            • Definition

              • Thinking of different practical ways in which a topic or idea can apply to important things.

              • Example

            • Importance

              • Many of these topics are likely applicable to many different things that are important to you.
              • Understanding how ideas connect to each other.
                Link (create if needed)
                • Useful for getting a better understanding of what reduces to what.
                  Move to linked section
              • Understanding how ideas appear in practice.

            • Typical types of applicability

              • To you

                • Importance

                  • This can help you fix your reasoning flaws.
                  • ^ This can help you identify your reasoning flaws.
                    • You're sure to have some reasoning flaws.
                    • Your reasoning flaws are likely to be initially unintuitive and hard to see for you.
                      • Otherwise you probably would have fixed them by now.
                      • We all have some things that are less intuitive and harder to see for us.

              • To someone(s) you know

              • To a system

              • To someone(s) you don't know

          • Zoom > Expansion

            • It's easy to expand too much.
              • Expanding a lot can easily make people feel overwhelmed at the amount of info displayed at once.
                • Especially when expanding the more dense sections.
                  • "Dense" as in both intellectual dense and lots of items having many children.
                • Especially for high-level sections
                  • "High-level" as in high in the hierarchy of items, not necessarily intellectually dense.
                  • Expanding a high-level section using ctrl+shift+. will typically result in expanding a lot more at once.
              • If you expand too much at once (by using ctrl+shift+.) at a high level section, it can cause significant lag.
                • This is not an issue on mobile, only PC.
            • You can typically zoom in/out instead of expanding/collapsing.
              • It tends to make it easier to focus on particular things.

        • Software/dynalist tips

          • Basic

            • Expanding/collapsing

              • You can click/tap the + to the right of each collapsed item to expand it, and likewise click/tap the - to the right of each expanded item.
              • You can use ctrl + shift + . or double-click on the +/- to expand/collapse the item and all their sub-items.

                • Importance / Limitations

                  • This is often extremely useful for low-level sections where you don't want to click/tap to read every little sentence.
                  • It's not recommended to use this for higher-level sections. For more info: Zoom > expansion
                  • There is unfortunately not a mobile equivalent as far as I'm aware.

                • Multiple items at once

                  • Can do this with multiple items at once by selecting items by clicking and dragging over them, then using ctrl + shift + .

            • Back/forward page

              • Definition

                • Your web browser's back /forward page buttons.

              • Importance

                • Very handy for quickly going back to where you were, especially after clicking/tapping link tips

            • Link tips

              • Back/forward page
                • If you want to go to a linked section of the guide and then return to where you were, you can use the back page button to easily return.
              • Knowing location
                • Navigating links can be quite disorienting.
              • Font size
                • Link text cannot wrap around on mobile [...]

            • Knowing location

              • Definition

                • Figuring out where you are in this document.

              • Importance

                • Especially useful after using links
                  • They can be quite disorienting.

              • Method

                • At the top, you should see something like "General Reasoning Guide [...] > Guide guide > Tips > Software tips > Basic >"
                • This displays the location you're currently zoomed into.
                  • You can expand sections without zooming into them, so if you do that, you won't be able to use this method alone to see exactly where you're at.
                    • This is part of the reason I recommend zooming in instead of expanding most of the time.
                      • Zoom > expansion
                • Specifically, you're in "Method" inside of "Knowing location" inside of "Basic" inside of "Software tips" inside of "Tips" inside of "Guide guide" inside of "General Reasoning Guide".

            • Font size
              Recommended for mobile

              • Definition: Text size.

              • Finding the setting

                • 1. Tap the three dots in the top-right corner.
                • 2. Tap "Settings"
                • 3. Scroll down to the "Font options" section.
                  • Should be blue text.
                • 4. Tap on "Font size"

              • What font size to choose

                • Small
                  Recommended (mobile)

                  • Mobile: readable links

                    • Link text cannot wrap-around on mobile, meaning that it's often hard to see the names of the linked sections with a larger font sizes.

                  • Less scrolling

                    • Especially on mobile.
                    • More can be on screen at once, so you don't have to scroll around as much.

                  • Harder to read most text

                    • Especially on mobile.
                    • Small text may be harder on eyesight and make things generally harder to read.

                  • Overwhelming

                    • Especially on desktop.
                    • There can be more on screen at once, which can feel more overwhelming.
                    • With less list density, this is not so bad.

                • Medium
                  Recommended (desktop)

                • Large

                  • Easier to read most text

                    • Especially on mobile.
                    • Large text is easier on eyesight and easier to read.

                  • Less overwhelming

                    • There's less on screen at once, so it's less overwhelming.
                    • It may be better to achieve this with less list density
                      • It may feel less overwhelming to see larger padding between items, rather than larger items.
                      • Link text cannot wrap around on mobile [...]

                  • Mobile: unreadable links

                    • Link text cannot wrap around on mobile [...]

                  • More scrolling

                    • Especially on mobile.
                    • Less can be on screen at once, so you have to scroll around a lot more.

                  • Deletion

                    • You can delete sections that seem unimportant to you.

                    • Risky

                      • While there are undoubtedly going to be sections that are pointless to you, it's likely that some sections will seem unimportant to you despite being pretty important to you.
                      • Be careful to check the deletion candidate's "importance" item to be safe.

              • Method

                • Using the left menu, you can create a copy of this doc/file.
                  Make this much less vague.

            • List density
              Recommended for mobile

              • Definition

                • The setting that determines the density (compactness) of the list of items in Dynalist.
                • In other words: it controls how large the areas between the items are.

              • Finding the setting

                • 1. Tap the three dots in the top-right corner.
                • 2. Tap "Settings"
                • 3. Find the "Appearance" section at the top.
                  • Should be blue text.
                • 4. Tap on "List density"

              • What list density to choose

                • Comfortable

                  • Less overwhelming

                    • There's less on screen at once, so it's less overwhelming.

                  • More scrolling

                    • Especially on mobile.
                    • Less can be on screen at once, so you have to scroll around a lot more.

                • Cozy
                  Recommended (desktop)

                • Compact
                  Recommended (mobile)

                  • Less scrolling

                    • Especially on mobile.
                    • More can be on screen at once, so you don't have to scroll around as much.

                  • More overwhelming

                    • There's more on screen at once, so it's more overwhelming.

      • Reading order

        • Meta

          • Colors

            • Based on average importance.
            • Only applies to everything inside "Reading order".
              • Except this "colors" section of course, since it is just showing the colors that will be used.

            • Nuances

              • Subjects may be more or less important to you specifically.
              • Impacted somewhat by the reading order selected, since things will be more/less important based on how you're approaching the whole guide.
            • Absurdly important
            • Extremely important
            • Very important
            • Pretty important
            • Somewhat important
            • Pretty unimportant

            • !!! READ THIS !!!

              • This color scheme does NOT apply to sections which are outside of the "Reading order" section but merely linked inside.
                • Eg. The colors in https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=CAUurOYjAYcX8-6gqZgbc468 mean something else

        • Methods

          • Rainbow repetitions

            • Definition

              • Starting by only reading the most important sections, then re-reading with less important sections again and again until everything is read.

              • Explicit

                • Starting with just reading the things in the red sections (without the things inside the things in the red sections unless they are also red), then re-reading everything inside the red sections and also orange sections (same nuance as above but for orange now), then everything in red, orange and yellow sections, etc.

            • Importance

              • Good for better remembering the more important material and really internalizing what you learn.

            • Nuances

              • It may be better to start by reading red and orange sections, since there probably won't be much directly inside the red sections.
              • For the final re-read, it may be best not to read the purple sections unless you really think they would be useful.

        • General

          • You should generally read from the top main section to the bottom one.

        • Read this before following an order

          • Remember, if you click a link to a section outside of "Reading order", you probably shouldn't (unless otherwise instructed like "read everything here") expand or zoom any further and should just go back a page to return once you finish reading what is immediately visible.

            • An order that starts with axioms and primarily follows the emergence/reasoning that leads to the kinds of reasoning processes we typically do.

            • Useful for learning act-based reasoning.
            • Useful for understanding why certain reasoning processes are used.

      • Misc

        • Objections

          • There are many unsupported claims.
            • 1. This guide is unfinished.
            • 2. The reasoning that is transparent is usually well-done, indicating that the less-transparent reasoning is likely usually well-done as well. So there is some indirect support for the claims via that fact.
          • This doesn't account for non-classical logic.
            • True, we consider non-classical logics to be absurd and/or redundant.
              • If you're looking for a reasoning guide based on non-classical logic [...]
          • This guide is missing a lot of very important ideas.

            • Likelihood

              • Much of this knowledge is a form of "soft" reasoning which people are almost always very bad at.
              • In general, more important ideas are easier to find.
                • P1. In general, the more important knowledge (such as reasoning tricks) is, the more generally causally impactful it has to be.
                  • P1. Importance in this context refers to wide usefulness.
                  • P2. In general, the more widely useful knowledge is, the more generally causally impactful it has to be (to be more widely useful).
                • P2. And typically the more generally causally impactful something is, the easier it is to discover it.
                  • Because there would be more opportunities to discover it, since it is more likely to influence you more often and in more impactful (and thus obvious) ways.

            • Impact if true

              • This guide is very unfinished, over time it will be filled with a lot more important ideas.
              • Even if true, it can still be quite helpful to use this guide alongside other resources.

      • FAQs

        • What is special about this guide?
          • Comprehensive material
          • Efficient format
          • Minimal questionable shortcuts
            • In many guides, things like fallacies will be explained with oversimplified descriptions and proclamations about how to treat them, which can cause people to make severe mistakes.
              • Mistake example: there's a common notion that an "appeal to authority" is always a fallacy, when in fact if the authority is sufficiently relevant, it is not a fallacy and can actually be an important piece of evidence to consider.
            • This guide attempts to be very careful with its definitions and its coverage of the topics, to avoid simplifications like that.
        • Why is the format non-linear?
          • It allows people to easily learn specific things more deeply
            • Very useful if they have an idea of some of their weaknesses.
            • It allows us to make the guide essentially as big as we want, without worrying about people having to read too much to read it or to read particular sections.
              Likelihood
              • Because it allows us to easily skip/skim less important parts
                Likelihood
          • It allows people to easily skip/skim less important parts

            • At a broad level

              • Eg. Skipping propositional logic
                • If you're already very familiar with it, it's unlikely you'll gain much from reading that section.
              • Complemented by the interlinked nature of the guide.
                • It typically allows one to skip a broad category without skipping important sub-components of that category.
                • ^ Because you can almost always find links to those very important sub-components in the other broad categories.

            • At a detailed level

              • Eg. Skipping an example
                • Often useful if you already understand the concept well enough.
                • Often possible if you know that non-link purple items are almost always examples.
            • It saves time if you already know what you're skipping.
              Relevance
          • It helps form useful associations
            • By showing people how various things are connected.
            • ^ Especially in areas that people have decided to learn more thoroughly, since the reading order instructs them to investigate those connections.
              Link
          • We attempt to mitigate its detriments
            • We believe this guide guide (especially reading tips) should be able to teach people how to mitigate the detriments of this format.
          • It allows for a better reading order
            • It better allows us to adjust the reading order, since with a linear format it would typically take a lot more work to adjust the order.
            • ^ A lot of the required knowledge to read a bunch of paragraphs in a given section would be dependent on understanding a section elsewhere, such that it would be difficult to know what we can move without breaking stuff.
          • It makes it easier to include more links
            • Since we're already creating lots of links to make the non-linear format work decently well, we have the practice/knowledge to easily create a lot of links for things like definitions/related sections.
              Likelihood
            • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=dvuNaCPXkDDm-gIrfq6DJDLG
              Relevance
              • Example: It allows people to more easily view relevant definitions.
                Likelihood

          • Related

            • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=NdorHkkOSy1uTnsEsONr8RfF
        • What does "reasoning" mean?
          • Technically, our definition is typically just forming inferences from premises
          • However the guide is specifically trying to teach how to reason well, which might be defined more along the lines of epistemic rationality.
          • There are also a lot of different ways to improve one's ability to form inferences from premises. So to clarify: This guide is aiming to become a standalone, comprehensive, general resource for all major aspects of reasoning. It covers topics relevant to all experience levels, and all domains where reasoning plays a substantial role.
          • Learn more about our definition here.
        • Who is this guide for?
          • Primarily for people who are willing to take the time to properly learn general reasoning from the ground up, and who want a practical guide with all the necessary information to learn it.
          • I want to bring it to everyone who's interested in reasoning, and ideally (though probably not) it (or something like it) could become recognized and become part of standard education to be taught to a ton of people, even those who aren't as interested in reasoning for its own sake. But until I finish enough of the material and the reading order and stuff (which will likely take a long time, if I ever finish it), I'm mainly hoping to distribute it to people who are already (relatively) very good at reasoning, but who could still improve a lot with the guide.
        • What is the plan for this guide?
          • My plan is to add all of the most important knowledge about reasoning I can find/think of, refine it and organize it well, and then create a reading order that should eventually be able to take people through the sections in an order that can bring them from no knowledge to (if they are willing to go through all the important parts) being capable of much more advanced and consistent reasoning than basically anyone else currently is.
        • Why isn't X in this guide?

          • Broad

            • The guide is very unfinished. However:
            • There are a few criteria (in roughly descending order of importance) I use to judge whether to add something to the guide:
            • ^ How impactful is it for reasoning abilities?
            • ^ How easy is the information to derive from other information?
              • Eg. If it's very easy to derive, I trust that once the reader has properly learned the important sections, they will almost certainly do fine on the topic without a dedicated section.
                • And this guide is designed for people willing to properly learn the important sections.
                • ^ Primarily for people willing to take the time to learn general reasoning from the ground up
            • ^ How well-known is it?
              • If it's already very well-known, it might not even be worth mentioning.
            • ^ How well has it been taught elsewhere?
              • For many important and relevant topics (eg. math), there are likely tons of resources far better than what I would make.
              • So in such cases, I will typically link to a place where it is better taught and perhaps explain relevant things left out of their explanation, like how it [relates to / can be used in] reasoning.
            • ^ How time consuming is it to add?

          • Specific

            • Why isn't there a section on morality?
              • I was very tempted on many occasions to make section(s) dedicated to morality, but in all cases I felt that what I had in mind could either:
              • ^ Be applied in other areas (and thus that morality should be confined to examples).
              • ^ Be reduced to / solved by other things, such that making a section on specifically morality or a moral issue would be unworthwhile.
                • While I think the perfect guide might have a section dedicated to morality, I think it would be difficult to write it in a useful way without making it very contentious and large, and for now I think the benefit in this guide would not be nearly as great as refining other sections which are more fundamental to reasoning.
          • If you feel that I've missed something, feel free to contact me on discord. My username is:
          • ferbalot
        • Why so few sources?
          • The guide is unfinished, over time many more sources will be added.
          • Often I write sections from scratch, so I have no sources I can easily give.
          • Some of the topics would not benefit from sources.
            • Eg. Cases in which all the important material from a source was already put into the guide and the sources are just less organized/efficient, with no other useful content.
          • Sometimes the sources are actually present, just in the definition item rather than a source item.

          • Related

            • There are many unsupported claims
        • Why is X in this guide?

          • This guide is broad

            • The guide covers a lot of topics that are relevant to reasoning but often excluded from other reasoning guides.

            • Examples

              • Semantics
              • Axioms
          • Why is Semantics taught?
            • A huge aspect of cooperative reasoning is effective communication, and that heavily involves being careful about both making and interpreting vague/clear definitions, making good decisions about how long to think/talk about a given problem/point, seeing where people are over-relying on conflations/vague arguments that are ultimately held up with an overreliance on intuition, etc.
          • Why are Axioms taught?
            • Few guides get into the fundamental axioms that all reasoning (or at least all reasoning in the guide) is supposed to be based upon, and people often disagree or have confusion about axioms.
          • Why are so many abstract things included?
            • In general I think most guides tend to focus too much on immediately obvious reasoning mistakes, rather than the more fundamental errors that are causing people to consistently make such mistakes. We are often taught to recognize and memorize specific fallacies or biases, but not taught about how they arise, what they're built on or how to effectively combat them.
          • Why include the "reducible" category?
            • To explain how subjects/ideas reduce to other subjects/ideas.
            • ^ This helps better understand how the subjects/ideas fundamentally work.
            • ^ This allows people to change gears in a discussion when a certain level of abstraction is not working well enough.
              • Example: If people are relying on simplified principle like "competition drives innovation" and "extrinsic motivation reduces intrinsic motivation" and arguing for two competing ideas using these principles, it would likely be beneficial to make it clear what the reasoning underlying those peoples' trust in those principles is, and to investigate the strength of the arguments when considering that reasoning, whether the principles should be applied in the relevant scenario, etc.
          • Why include the "common" importance category?
            • The reason that there is often a "common" category under importance is that if something is common, it is more likely to be relevant to your life in some way.
            • ^ There are of course still many exceptions, but I thought it was worth including the category anyway.
        • Why aren't degrees of relevance/likelihood quantified?
          • It would take more effort to quantify them.
          • It would make the format even more alien/confusing to people just getting into it.
          • We believe that it's usually not very improtant to quantify the degree.
            • We typically trust our unquantified estimates a decent amount and
            • try to clarify the consequences of those estimates in the "how to think of it" sections.
          • Despite these reasons, we plan to consider quantifying relevance/likelihood in the future.

    • Reasoning

      • Definition

        • Forming inference(s) from premise(s).

        • Distinction

          • Rationality vs emotions/intuitions
            Source: https://www.readthesequences.com/Biases-An-Introduction

            • In a Hollywood movie, being “rational” usually means that you’re a stern, hyperintellectual stoic. Think Spock from Star Trek, who “rationally” suppresses his emotions, “rationally” refuses to rely on intuitions or impulses, and is easily dumbfounded and outmaneuvered upon encountering an erratic or “irrational” opponent.
            • There’s a completely different notion of “rationality” studied by mathematicians, psychologists, and social scientists. Roughly, it’s the idea of doing the best you can with what you’ve got. A rational person, no matter how out of their depth they are, forms the best beliefs they can with the evidence they’ve got. A rational person, no matter how terrible a situation they’re stuck in, makes the best choices they can to improve their odds of success.
            • Real-world rationality isn’t about ignoring your emotions and intuitions. For a human, rationality often means becoming more self-aware about your feelings, so you can factor them into your decisions.
            • Rationality can even be about knowing when not to overthink things. There are some problems where conscious deliberation serves us better, and others where snap judgments serve us better.
              • When selecting a poster to put on their wall, or predicting the outcome of a basketball game, experimental subjects have been found to perform worse if they carefully analyzed their reasons.
              • ^ Timothy D. Wilson et al., “Introspecting About Reasons Can Reduce Post-choice Satisfaction,” Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin 19 (1993): 331–331.
              • ^ Jamin Brett Halberstadt and Gary M. Levine, “Effects of Reasons Analysis on the Accuracy of Predicting Basketball Games,” Journal of Applied Social Psychology 29, no. 3 (1999): 517–530.
            • Psychologists who work on dual process theories distinguish the brain’s “System 1” processes (fast, implicit, associative, automatic cognition) from its “System 2” processes (slow, explicit, intellectual, controlled cognition).5 The stereotype is for rationalists to rely entirely on System 2, disregarding their feelings and impulses. Looking past the stereotype, someone who is actually being rational—actually achieving their goals, actually mitigating the harm from their cognitive biases—would rely heavily on System-1 habits and intuitions where they’re reliable.
            • Unfortunately, System 1 on its own seems to be a terrible guide to “when should I trust System 1?” Our untrained intuitions don’t tell us when we ought to stop relying on them.
              • Being biased and being unbiased feel the same.
              • ^ Timothy D. Wilson, David B. Centerbar, and Nancy Brekke, “Mental Contamination and the Debiasing Problem,” in Heuristics and Biases: The Psychology of Intuitive Judgment, ed. Thomas Gilovich, Dale Griffin, and Daniel Kahneman (Cambridge University Press, 2002).
            • On the other hand, as behavioral economist Dan Ariely notes: we’re predictably irrational. We screw up in the same ways, again and again, syste­ma­tically.
            • If we can’t use our gut to figure out when we’re succumbing to a cognitive bias, we may still be able to use the sciences of mind.

        • Reasoning vs similar things

          • Definition (different)

            • Reasoning: The general ability to find important truths and avoid important falsehoods.
            • Why is this different?
              • Because we're referring to the ability of reasoning rather than the process of reasoning.

          • "Winning"

            • In most important cases, your ability to "win" is not very indicative of your reasoning abilites.
              • Most important debate "winning" is decided quite irrationally.
                • Most people are bad at a large portion of important reasoning
                • "Winning" is decided by things like what people generally think the conclusion was.

          • Persuasion

            • In most important cases, how well you can convince people of your points is not very indicative of your reasoning abilities.
              • Most people are (in important matters) persuaded to a substantial degree by factors that don't have to do with reasoning.
                • Most people are bad at a large portion of important reasoning.
                  • Most people are bad at "soft" reasoning
                    Broken link
                  • "Soft" reasoning makes up a large portion of important reasoning

            • Articulation

          • Debating abilities

            • Ability to shut down opponents
              • In most important cases, how well you can shut down other(s) is not very indicative of your reasoning abilities.
                • In most kinds of important cases, people often aren't shut down by good reasoning and often are shut down by bad reasoning.
                  • Most people are bad at a large portion of important reasoning
            • Ability to leave opponents flustered
              • In most important cases, how well you can leave other(s) flustered is not very indicative of your reasoning abilities.
                • In most kinds of important cases, people often aren't shut down by good reasoning and often are shut down by bad reasoning.
                  • Most people are bad at a large portion of important reasoning

            • Persuasion

            • "Winning"

          • Articulation

            • In most important cases, your ability to articulate your points is not very indicative of your reasoning abilities.
              • In most kinds of important cases, articulation has to do more with practice, vocabulary, knowledge of the subject, etc.

          • Calculation speed
            Clarify what "irreducible calculation speed" means, and what calculation speed overall means.

            • Definition

            • Calculation speed has a decent effect on reasoning.
            • Different people don't really have much of a difference between irreducible calculation speeds.
              • The apparent differences are typically due to other things.

                • Ability to focus

                  • Focus has a significant effect on apparent irreducible calculation speed.
                    • More focus = more efficient usage of irreducible calculation speed.
                  • Different people have different amounts of mental stamina
                  • People have varying degrees of ability to focus.
                    • ADHD is notorious for its tendency to make focusing much harder.

                • Memory

                  • Short-term

                    • Better short-term memory makes it significantly easier to reason about remotely complicated things.
                      • Forgetting important things mid-discussion often forces a person to pause and try to recall those important things before they can continue, making it seem like their irreducible calculation speed is slower.

                  • Long-term

                    • Better long-term memory often makes it significantly easier to reason about things that you're familiar with, since you can utilize much more reasoning that you've done in the past.

                • Well-trained system 1 thinking / Good heuristics

                  • When it is well-trained, it performs common reasoning processes very quickly and leaves our conscious reasoning to calculate higher-level things.
                    • This is a kind of typical pattern-recognition.
                      • So not the kind of calculation we typically imagine.
                      • It might still be regarded as calculation.

                • Knowledge

                  • General

                    • General knowledge makes us much more able to have and use more information for calculation in many situations.
                      • This often saves a lot of time that would otherwise be used researching things.

                  • Specific

                    • More specific knowledge relevant to the matter one is calculating saves time.
                      • This often saves a lot of time [...]

                • Debating abilities

                  • debating abilities have more to do with things that don't involve as much calculation.
                    • The above link shows this.

                • Articulation

                  • Vocabulary

                    • A kind of knowledge so not really calculation.
                    • Allows for more accurate and easy expression, which makes articulation easier.
                  • System 1 thinking that's well-trained for articulation.
            • In many important topics, the average amount of close mindedness makes virtually any realistic calculation speed insufficient to reason well.

      • Axioms

        • Definition

          • Foundational beliefs which lack epistemic justification or are justified circularly.
            • Foundational: Plays a significant role in the relevant epistemology(s).
          • Usually: Justifies a ton of other beliefs (often indirectly).
          • Usually: Commonly held.
          • Examples: Specific axioms

        • Importance / Limitations

          • Common

            • Unless we accept non-axiomatic circular reasoning, everyone holds some axiom(s).

              • Unless we accept circular reasoning, axiom(s) must underlie all reasoning.

          • Impactful

            • Knowing axioms ≠ reasonable: Even if you understand axioms very well, it is still very possible to be very unreasonable.
              • For example via heavy heuristic usage
                • Eg. If one uses very simplistic heuristics to determine when they should stop using said heuristics, and the heuristics promote using heuristics in virtually all cases, it may be infeasible to get them to think at a lower level to properly check their reasoning.
              • For example via close-minded close-mindedness
                • If one is too overconfident, they may consider it a waste of time to check their reasoning at a lower level, resulting in it never (or very rarely) being checked, preventing them from improving even if they have a good understanding of axioms.
            • Axioms allow us to better avoid overly heavy heuristic usage
              • Understanding axioms and how they build up to higher-level reasoning allows us to default to a deeper level of reasoning when there is sufficient evidence of our heuristics being unreliable for the relevant case(s).
                • Unless we accept circular reasoning, axiom(s) must underlie all reasoning.

                  • Gödel's incompleteness theorems

            • Understanding axioms allows one to shift to slower but more reliable reasoning.
              • Understanding axioms and how they build up to higher levels of reasoning allows us to use a lower level of reasoning when a higher one is brought into question.

                • Lower/higher level definition
                  Move all of this to definitions section.

                  • Lower/higher level of abstraction from pure logic.
                  • ^ Low-level reasoning is reasoning done close to the level of pure logic.
                    • Example: Symbolic logic
                      Create and link section
                    • ^ Definition: Symbolic logic is a branch of formal logic that uses mathematical symbols, variables, and operators to represent logical expressions and relationships explicitly.
                  • ^ High-level reasoning is reasoning that's highly abstracted/emergent from the level of pure logic.
                    • It's entirely reducible to pure logic.
                    • It has many short-cuts and functions that make it far easier to work with for everyday matters where precision is not super important.
                    • It tends to be far more ambiguous than low-level reasoning.
                      • <Consequences of misunderstandings>
                  • ^ Pure logic is ...
                • This allows for more resolutions of disagreements.

                  • Cases where people agree (at least technically) about the relevant axioms

                    • Understanding axioms and how they build up to higher levels of reasoning frequently allows for being able to systematically reason through a disagreement with someone when it would not otherwise be possible. So long as all the most impactful information can be shared.
                      • For a lot of the more complicated and important topics, it is not realistic to share all the most impactful information.
                        • There is too much impactful information to share.
                          • Due to emergent simplicity, it is usually realistic.
                            Arguments continued under the importance / limitations of the 'emergent simplicity' section.
                        • A lot of the impactful information comes from things like personal experience, which cannot be adequately shared.
                          • Generally, there is a low likelihood that one would be lying about their personal experience in a topic like this. So usually personal experiences can be shared to an adequate degree.
                      • The relevant axioms are generally not agreed upon.
                        • Example: Cases where people consciously disagree (at least technically) about the relevant axioms

                  • Cases where people consciously disagree (at least technically) about the relevant axioms

                    • Often when people consciously disagree about axioms, they still subconsciously disagree. So it is still theoretically possible to resolve the disagreement by showing them how they subconsciously rely on the same axioms.
                      • This is usually infeasible due to how common a high level of close-mindedness is.
                    • Being able to use a lower level of reasoning can often be very helpful even in cases where people even subconsciously disagree on the axioms.
                      • Even if they don't agree on the specific axioms, they probably agree on most if not all of the relevant principles built not more then a few levels above those axioms, and those principles are part of how axioms build up to typical reasoning.
                        • This applies even when the lower level(s) of reasoning are not axiomatic.
                • This applies even when the lower level(s) of reasoning are above axioms.
                  • Understanding axioms gives us a better intuition for how reasoning builds up in general, even at a high-level.
                    I'm a little skeptical of this, unsure.
              • This applies even when the supposed axioms in question are not the axioms one really accepts.
                Likelihood
                • Even if these axioms are not accepted, the ones that are accepted will probably be accepted in such a way that they support all the main common reasoning principles in deduction and induction (and in turn virtually everything else).
                  Likelihood | Ideally support this.

        • Specific axioms

          • The laws of thought

            • Definition

              • The set of three laws: identity, contradiction and excluded middle.

              • Synonym

                • Typically synonymous with the "laws of logic".

            • Importance

              • Laws that are generally regarded as underlying all reasoning.

            • Identity

              • Definition

                • All well-defined propositions are equivalent to themselves.

              • Importance

                • Common

                  • Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.

              • Intuition pull

                • All reasoning relies on propositions being equal to themselves, otherwise they could be different from themselves at any point.

                • Anti-circularity specific

                  • Any attempt to justify the law of identity must implicitly rely on its propositions being equal to themselves, thus dooming the attempts to be circular at best.

            • Contradiction

              • Definition

                • No well-defined propositions can be both true and untrue.

              • Importance

                • Common

                  • Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.

              • Intuition pull

                • The principle of explosion

                  • Definition

                    • The law according to which any statement can be proven from a contradiction.
                    • The implication is typically that accepting any contradiction is absurd.
                    • Wikipedia

                  • Importance

                    • This principle doesn't apply to paraconsistent logic
                      • This guide is based on classical logic, not paraconsistent logic.

                • Anti-circularity specific

                  • Any attempt to justify the law of contradiction must implicitly rely on its propositions not entailing any contradictions, thus dooming the attempts to be circular at best.

            • Excluded middle

              • Definition

                • All well-defined propositions must either be true or false.

              • Importance

                • Common

                  • Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.

              • Intuition pull

                • Given the other laws of thought and modus ponens, it appears to be possible to derive the law of excluded middle and unnecessary to accept it as an axiom.
                  Link or move proof from elsewhere
                • However if we do not accept one of: [identity, contradiction, modus ponens works], then we can either accept it as an axiom, or reject it and potentially face some issues.
                • ^ Without the law of excluded middle, we need a method to separate which well-defined propositions can be neither true nor false, and which can't.
                  • If we don't have this method, then:
                  • For any well-defined proposition, we could say "how do we know that this is true or false? It could be neither."
                  • This would allow all well-defined propositions to escape the perview of reasoning, leaving us with no means to reason about anything.
                • However if we come up with a method, then it seems like we have other issues.
                  • It seems unclear how to justify such a method without using itself (because of the aforementioned problem of any well-defined propositions possibly being neither true nor false, including the method), so it seems like we must use circular reasoning to justify it.
                  • However circular reasoning tends to pose issues.
                    Read the circular reasoning (circularity) section for more details. Just click the "circular reasoning" link above to visit it.

              • Alternative

                • Negation as failure

                  • Definition

                    • Synonym: NAF

          • Modus ponens works
            Likely replace this

            • Definition

              • If [if x then y] and x, then y.

            • Importance

              • Common

                • Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.

            • Intuition pull

              • Without accepting that modus ponens works (or something similar) as an axiom, it seems we are unable to engage in logical inference.
                • If only the other axioms listed here are presupposed, then it seems like we are unable to justify modus ponens without using modus ponens.
                  • Using modus ponens to justify modus ponens would involve circular reasoning.
                    • If we accept a type of logic that allows for circular reasoning, it is unclear why it should be allowed in this case but not in other cases that one would (ostensibly) not accept.

            • Isn't modus ponens an inference rule rather than an axiom?

              • Modus ponens is an inference rule, however the claim that it works is a proposition that can be treated largely like any other, including being considered an axiom.

          • Principle of induction

            • Definition

              • The principle that a higher number of cases of association will increase the probability of a fresh association.
                Clarify to what degree the probability increases and what exactly this means.

              • Examples
                Provide non-event based examples if applicable.

                • We experience the sun rising many times, therefore we are more likely to experience the sun rising again then we would otherwise be.

              • Wikipedia)

            • Importance / Limitations

            • Supports probabilistic reasoning

            • Intuition pull

              • Anti-circularity specific

                • The problem of induction

                  • Definition

                    • The problem that there is no non-circular way to justify inductive inferences

                  • Wikipedia

            • Alternative

              • Sometimes people presuppose the principle of uniformity and derive the principle of induction, rather than the other way around.

          • Semi-reliability of mental faculties

            • Definition

              • Your own conscious reasoning mental faculties are semi-reliable.

              • Distinction

                • Different from the idea that everyone's conscious reasoning mental faculties are semi-reliable, this is just about your own conscious reasoning mental faculties.

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Common

                • Often considered to underlie everything except other axioms.

              • Impactful

                • Non-reasoning mental faculties can still be less than semi-reliable.

                  • Example

                    • System 1 thinking

                • Reasoning mental faculties can still be unreliable to a substantial extent.
                  • We can still often be wrong for reasons we never considered.

            • Intuition pull

              • Any attempt to justify the semi-reliability of our mental faculties must implicitly rely on our reasoning being semi-reliable, which implicitly relies on the semi-reliability of the mechanism(s) responsible for that reasoning, which are by definition our mental faculties. Thus dooming the attempts to be circular at best.
              • ^ Thus we would have to use circular reasoning to justify this, and circular reasoning tends to lead to issues.
                See linked section for details.

              • Anti-circularity specific

          • Cogito
            Needs a lot more work.

            • Definitions

              • There are multiple subtly different variations/interpretations of the cogito, but they're all roughly based on the idea of "I think, therefore I am".

              • Synonyms

                • Cogito, ergo sum
                • I think, therefore I am

            • Importance

              • Common

                • One of the most common beliefs considered to be at the foundation of one's epistemology.

              • Impactful

                • At least in argument form (as it is often understood), it appears to be misleading about how axioms work.

            • Full argument version

              • Definition

                • The axiom that the argument: "I think, therefore I am" is sound.
                • "I think" is a premise, "I am" is the conclusion.

              • Related

                • Just-conclusion version of the cogito

            • Just-conclusion version

              • Definition

                • The axiom that the argument: "I think, therefore I am" has a true conclusion.
                • "I think" is a premise, "I am" is the conclusion.

              • Importance

                • Impactful

                  • This seems to be misleading about how axioms work.
                    • The argument really appears to be based on premises that are not normally deep down accepted as axioms.

              • Possible implied premises

                • If something thinks, then it must exist.

                  • Why?
                  • ^ It is self-evident.
                    Create a definition for "self-evident" and link it here.
                    • As intuitive as it seems, it doesn't seem like it's truly self-evident.
                    • For example the statement below has the same structure:
                    • "If x does not exist, then x must exist."
                    • And yet would not generally be considered true for obvious reasons.
                    • So why is "if something thinks, then it must exist" true, when the above example is not?
                    • It seems like there must be some kind of answer here, and that this answer would justify the "if thinks then exists" statement.
                    • If this is the case, it's difficult to see how it's self-evident, seeing as its evident-ness comes from something outside of itself.

                • Modus ponens works

        • Axiom systems

          • Definition

            • Collections of axioms.

          • How to select an axiom system

            • Typically, people select an axiom system based on the following principles:

            • Minimal axioms

              • No unnecessary axioms if it can be helped.

              • Why isn't this an axiom?

                • Definition

                  • Why don't we treat this essentially like an axiom?
                  • Eg. "If it can be justified, then it is not necessarily true".

            • Matches irreducible intuitions

              • Definition

                • The axiomatic system appears to match our most fundamental reasoning intuitions.

                • Fundamental reasoning intuitions (definition)

                  • What we would refuse to stop believing regardless of the evidence presented to us.
                    At least unless that evidence convinced us for non-evidential reasons, such as causing an emotional reaction that irrationally changes our mind. This doesn't account for that.

                • Example

              • Why accept this?

                • It doesn't seem justified to trust our intuitions (even our most irreducible ones).
                  • We should likely not trust our intuitive distrust of this principle.
                  • ^ To be intuitively opposed to trusting our most irreducible intuitions is likely essentially equivalent to being intuitively opposed to a stronger intuition.
                    • P1. These intuition of distrust must (to the extent that it is justified by any sort of reasoning) be based on some kind of reasoning that is ultimately justified by our axioms.
                    • P2. Our axioms are in turn likely partially derived from our irreducible intuitions.
                • Essentially all reasoning necessarily relies on irreducible intuitions.
                  Justify

              • Why isn't this an axiom?

                • Definition

                  • Why don't we treat this essentially like an axiom?
                  • Eg. "What matches our irreducible intuitions must necessarily be true".
                • Our temptation to make this an axiom appears to come from our subconscious desire to go "my axioms must match my most irreducible intuitions, therefore axioms like the law of contradiction must necessarily be true."
