        • The causal source of accepting axioms

          • Definition

            • The causal reason for us accepting our axioms.

            • Distinction

          • Importance

          • Coincidentally accurate axioms

            • Definition

              • The position that we coincidentally don't hold any wrong axioms.

            • Importance

              • Accuracy

                • In order to better survive and reproduce, evolution would have been best off selecting for the (at least deep down) acceptance of axioms that match reality.
                  • This feels like circular reasoning.
                    Addressed
                    • It feels circular because of using axioms to investigate the causal source of axioms.
                    • To be a form of circular reasoning, it would have to be using axioms to justify axioms, or using axioms' causal source to investigate their causal source. Neither is being done here.
                  • There could be axioms that were not relevant to our survival/reproduction.
                    Addressed
                    • Simpler/broader axioms would likely apply everywhere in the universe in substantial way(s).
                      • Occam's razor supports the idea that all axioms are simple/broad.

          • Transcendental-esque access

            • Definition

              • Some sort of direct, implicit, perfect access to truth.
              • Not indirect/imperfect and probably not coincidental.

            • Importance

              • Common

                • Accepting something like this in terms of trusting strong enough intuitions is pretty common.

              • Impactful

              • Accuracy

        • Accepting axioms with uncertainty

          • Definition

            • Accepting axioms in a manner that is uncertain.

            • Distinction

              • This is different from considering our conception of what axioms we hold to be uncertain.
              • ^ It's possible to think that we could be wrong about what axioms form the foundation of our reasoning, while thinking that the axioms themselves cannot be wrong.

          • Importance / Limitations

            • Common

              • It's common for people to attempt to accept axioms in an uncertain way.

            • Impactful

              • It isn't possible for us to be consistent in believing that we hold axiom X while also believing that axiom X is uncertain.
                • P1. Uncertainty about an axiom requires the possibility of affecting some currently present supporting evidence of that axiom.
                  • P1. Uncertainty requires the possibility of being wrong.
                    • In order to be uncertain about an axiom, we must consider it possible for that axiom to be wrong.
                    • Example: If there's an 80% chance of being right, then there's necessarily a 20% chance of being wrong.
                      Link relevant induction section about probabilities adding up to 100%.
                  • P2. The possibility of an axiom being wrong requires the possibility of affecting some currently present supporting evidence of that axiom.
                    • P1. The possibility of an axiom being wrong requires the possibility of some evidence existing which indicates that the axiom is wrong.
                      • Distinction: This evidence/indication does not necessarily have to be practically or even theoretically accessible.
                      • In order for a well-defined proposition to be wrong, there must be some logic that makes it wrong.
                        Likelihood
                    • P2. In order for some evidence to indicate that an axiom is wrong, the evidence must influence some information we previously had that was supporting the axiom.
                      • This is because counter-evidence always functions via deductive or inductive reasoning, which both always have this property.
                        Likelihood

                        • Example

                • P2. It isn't possible to affect some currently present supporting evidence of an axiom.
                  • Axioms are (by this guide's definition) necessarily unsupported.
                • Accepting axioms with uncertainty frequently results in confusion and mistakes, especially in important areas.
                  Relevance
                  • Example: When feeling cornered with logic about religion or lackthereof, people will often resort to challenging their own implicitly accepted axioms with "we can't be sure about that".

        • The ways in which these can be axioms

          • All of these can be assumed as applying in all cases or in at least most cases, or in at least most cases that have been observed, etc.

      • Standard deduction

        • Definitions

          • Deductive reasoning is the process of drawing valid inferences

            • Example

              • The inference from the premises "all men are mortal" and "Socrates is a man" to the conclusion "Socrates is mortal" is deductively valid.

          • Alternative definition

            • One approach defines deduction in terms of the intentions of the author: they have to intend for the premises to offer deductive support to the conclusion.
              • With the help of this modification, it is possible to distinguish valid from invalid deductive reasoning: it is invalid if the author's belief about the deductive support is false, but even invalid deductive reasoning is a form of deductive reasoning.

        • Importance

          • Common

            • Most reasoning involves some standard deduction.

          • Impactful

            • Better understanding the processes underlying deduction allows for more systematic reasoning which allows for better decision-making during big decisions

        • Limitations

          • It's very easy to error in a very impactful way when using deductive reasoning.
            • There are a lot of common and easy-to-make mistakes and fallacies surrounding standard deduction.
            • Analogy: Just as it's easy for one small mistake in a mathematical solution to ruin the entire process.
          • There are more specific limitations listed in other "limitations" items in this section.

        • How to view it

          • Standard deductive reasoning (when applicable and properly used, which like most reasoning is often difficult) is extremely strong and reliable, so where available, it tends to be preferable to using just inductive reasoning.
          • However combining both inductive and standard deductive reasoning can be even better in many situations.

        • Structure

          • Definition

            • The standard deduction topics that have to do with logical structure.

          • Importance

            • Understanding and following structured deductive reasoning helps reason in a more systematic way.
              • Unstructured reasoning typically leads to lots of/major blunders and mistakes.

                • Examples

          • Propositions
            Needs much more work.

            • Definitions

              • Propositions are typically characterized in terms of three interlocking roles:

              • Meanings of declarative sentences

                • Definition

                  • The ideas or semantic contents expressed by declarative sentences.
                  • The information content of sentences and cross-linguistic sameness.

                  • Distinction

                    • Propositions are not identical to individual sentences and do not belong to any particular language.

              • Contents or objects of propositional attitudes

                • Distinction

                  • Propositions are not identical to individual beliefs or desires since the same proposition can underlie many individual mental states.

                • Examples

                  • Such as when someone believes that the sky is blue.
                  • If Leila believes that the train will be delayed, then she has a mental state, called a propositional attitude, directed at the proposition that the train will be delayed.

                • Propositional attitudes

                  • Types

                    • Beliefs

                    • Desires

                    • Hopes

                    • Fears

                  • Linking psychological attitudes

                    • Propositional attitudes are typically expressed through that-clauses to link a psychological attitude to a proposition.
                      • "She believes that it will rain."
                  • The contents of propositional attitudes are shareable.
                    • Different persons can have the same beliefs or fears.
                    • Both Leila and Diego fear that the train will be delayed.

              • Bearers of truth values

                • Each proposition is a function which takes a claim and returns either true or false.

                • Primary truth-bearers

                  • Non-proposition truth-bearers are true or false in a derivative sense by being about true or false propositions.
                    • Declarative sentences
                    • Beliefs
                  • Propositions are not the only entities that have truth values.

                    • Examples

                      • Declarative sentences
                      • Beliefs

                • Bearers of modal properties

                  • A proposition can be possible, impossible, or necessary, depending on whether it is logically compatible with coherent scenarios, or in some sense conceivable or contradictory.

                  • Modal types

                • True vs false

              • Synonyms

                • Statement

                  • Sometimes used synonymously.
                  • However, the word "statement" is ambiguous since it can also refer to declarative sentences themselves rather than their meanings.

                • Judgement

                  • The term proposition overlaps with the term judgment, with one difference being that judgments are more closely associated with mental processes that affirm or deny the truth of a content.

              • Sub-definitions

                • Non-mental/abstract entities

                • General mental entities

              • Distinction

                • Unlike sentences, propositions are not linguistic expressions.
                  • The English sentence "Snow is white" and the German "Schnee ist weiß" denote the same proposition.

              • Formal definition

                • Formal propositions are often modeled as functions which map a possible world to a truth value.
                  • The proposition that the sky is blue can be modeled as a function which would return the truth value T if given the actual world as input, but would return F if given some alternate world where the sky is green.

                • Alternative definitions

                  • Structured propositions view

              • Further clarification

                • Propositions are the objects denoted by declarative sentences.
                  • "The sky is blue" expresses the proposition that the sky is blue.
              • Wikipedia

            • Types

              • True vs false

                • The truth value of a proposition depends on its accuracy / correspondence with reality.

                • True

                  • A proposition which is in accordance with reality.

                • False

                  • A proposition which is not in accordance with reality.

              • Compound propositions

                • Definition

                • Conditional propositions

                  • Conditional propositions are compound propositions since they have components that are themselves propositions.

                • Conjunctive propositions

                  • Conjunctive propositions claim that all their component statements are true, typically expressed as an "and" sentence, such as "the tree is green and the sky is blue".

                • Disjunctive propositions

                  • Definition

                    • Propositions which assert that one of their component statements is true, typically expressed as an "or" sentence, as in "it is windy or it is rainy".

                  • Inclusive disjunctive propositions

                    • At least one but possibly both component statements are true.

                  • Exclusive disjunctive propositions

                    • Exactly one component statement is true and the other is false.

              • Analytic vs synthetic

                • The difference depends on why they are true.

                • Analytic

                  • The truth of analytic propositions is determined only by the meanings of concepts, independent of the actual state of the world.

                  • Example

                    • The proposition "all bachelors are unmarried" is analytically true because the concept "bachelor" already includes the meaning of "unmarried".

                • Synthetic

                  • The truth of synthetic propositions, such as "snow is white", depends on the state of the world.

              • A priori vs a posteriori

                • The difference depends on the source of their knowledge.

                • A priori

                  • Propositions which can be known through pure reasoning alone.

                  • Example

                    • "2+2=4"

                • A posteriori

                  • Propositions which describe empirical facts knowable through sensory experience.

                  • Example

                    • "The sun is shining."

              • Modal types

                • Definition

                  • Modal propositions express what is possible, necessary, or impossible.

                  • Examples

                    • "It is possible that I will win the lottery."
                    • "It is impossible to travel faster than light".

                  • Distinction

                    • Rather than asserting how the world is, they describe how it could or could not have been.
                • A proposition is necessarily true if it is impossible that it is false.

                • Alethic modality

                  • Concerns what is possible or necessary relative to the laws of nature, metaphysics, or logic.

                • Epistemic modality

                  • Concerns what may or must be the case relative to someone's knowledge or evidence.

                  • Example

                    • "The butler cannot be the killer."
                • Normative propositions express what ought to be the case, like "you should not drink and drive". They include permissions, requirements, and prohibitions. Moral propositions are normative propositions that assert moral principles or judgments, such as "you should keep promises". Normative propositions contrast with descriptive propositions, which express what is rather than what ought to be. The schools of cognitivism and non-cognitivism debate the existence of normative propositions. Non-cognitivism argues that normative sentences are neither true nor false and do not express propositions, for example, because they convey emotions rather than propositions. A gappy proposition, also called an incomplete or unfilled proposition, is a statement whose subject matter is not properly specified, which results in an incomplete meaning. This can happen when the proposition involves an empty name, which does not refer to any real entity, such as the name Pegasus. Given the difficulties in assigning truth values to gappy propositions, philosophers debate whether they qualify as propositions in the strict sense. Alternative proposals suggest that they are another type of meaning content. Temporal propositions, another type, are statements that refer to specific times, such as "the Berlin Wall fell in 1989". Propositions are also classified by the domain or field of inquiry to which they belong, such as mathematical, scientific, metaphysical, and theological propositions.

              • Categorical vs conditional

                • Categorical propositions

                  • Definition

                    • Categorical propositions assert how things are, independently of other statements or assumptions.

                • Conditional propositions

                  • Definition

                    • Conditional propositions link two simpler propositions, typically expressed as an "if-then" sentence.

                    • Synonym

                      • Hypothetical proposition
                  • They hold that if the if-statement is true, then the then-statement must be true.
                    • If it rains, then the ground gets wet.

              • Typically linguistic
                Check if the other types belong here.

                • General vs singular

                  • How to view it

                    • Usually a purely linguistic distinction.

                  • General

                    • General propositions are about a general set of entities.

                    • Universal vs existential

                      • Universal

                        • Definition

                          • Propositions which assert that something is the case for all entities in a domain.

                          • Example

                            • "All humans are mortal."

                      • Existential

                        • Definition

                          • Propositions which state that something is the case for at least one entity in a domain.

                          • Example

                            • "Some humans are left-handed."

                  • Singular

                    • Singular propositions are about one specific entity.

                    • Example

                      • "Socrates is wise."

                • Affirmative vs negative

                  • How to view it

                    • Often a purely linguistic distinction.
                      • Usually, the same information can be expressed with and without negations, as in "he is not happy" and "he is sad".

                  • Affirmative

                    • Affirmative propositions state that something is the case.

                    • Example

                      • The tree is green.

                  • Negative

                    • Negative propositions deny that something is the case.

                    • Example

                      • The tree is not green.
                  • If an affirmative proposition is true, then the corresponding negative proposition is false, and vice versa.

              • Misc

                • Fictional

                  • Definition

                    • Propositions about non-existing entities.

                    • Example

                      • "Santa Claus has a beard."

            • Well-defined propositions
              Reconcile with Well-defined proposition

              • Definition

                • Propositions that are defined in a perfectly unambiguous way.

                  • Unambiguous

                  • Infeasibility

                    • In reality this is typically infeasible to achieve, but we can get close enough by minimizing the probability of impactful ambiguities from arising.

                      • Clear semantics

          • Antecedent and consequent

            • Definitions

              • Antecedent

                • The first half of a conditional proposition, whenever the if-clause precedes the then-clause.

                • Synonyms

                  • Condition

                    • When the antecedent is used for a IF-THEN (material conditional)

                  • Protasis

                    • Used synonymously in some contexts.

              • Consequent

                • the second half of a conditional proposition. In the standard form of such a proposition, it is the part that follows "then".
                • Synonyms
                  • Consequence

            • Related

              • IF-THEN (material conditional)

                • The only kind of conditional proposition where the sides cannot be swapped.

              • Conclusion

            • Basic connectives

                  • Outputs the logical negation of the input.

                  • Example

                    • I am human. -> I am not Human.
                  • Symbols
                    • ¬
                    • -

                • Double negation

                  • Definition

                    • "The tree is not not green."
                  • A proposition with a double negation is logically equivalent to a proposition without either negation.

                  • Symbols

                • Definition

                  • Distinction

                    • More specific than the colloquial definition of or, in that it never means https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=n7OMAZ2eLgm2YIZTBh-L2db8

            • Secondary connectives

              • IF-THEN (material conditional)

                • Definition

                  • Synonym: conditional.
                  • Antecedent
                  • Consequent
                  • Symbols
                    • →
                    • ->
                    • =>

                  • Distinction

                    • Different from colloquial if-then, in that there is no requirement for a causal or epistemic correlation between the antecedent and consequent.

                • Importance

                  • Impactful

                    • Unintuitive

                      • Non-colloquial

                    • Very useful in logic in general

                  • Common

                • Vacuous truth

                  • Definition

                    • A true proposition that has the form "if P then Q" with a false antecedent.
                      • The antecedent is P in this case.
                    • https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vacuous_truth

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                      • Unintuitive

                        • https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paradoxes_of_material_implication

                    • Common

                  • The only way a vacuous truth can be false is if the antecedent is true and the consequent is false.
                    • But what if the consequent would be false if the antecedent was true, but the antecedent is false for now? It feels like the overall statement should be false, since colloquially "if P then Q" is false in this case.

              • IF-AND-ONLY-IF (material biconditional)

                • Definition

                  • It can be understood as literally "if and only if", or "sufficient and necessary". But a more colloquial definition might simply treat it as synonymous with "equals". If the antecedent's truth value is equal to the consequent, then this connective 'returns' true (i.e. the whole statement is true). Otherwise, if the antecedent and consequent have different truth values, then it returns false.
                  • Why "if and only if"?
                    • A nice intuitive way to think of this is to split a biconditional into "if" and "only if".
                    • "If" (in this context) just describes an ordinary conditional, so you can think of this part as describing the right side of the double-headed arrow ↔.
                    • "Only if" (in this context) means that the only way for the consequent to be true is if the antecedent is true. This implies that if the consequent is true, the antecedent must be true, so you can think of this part as also describing an ordinary conditional, but in the other direction.
                      Unimportant note: Technically it seems we could also derive "if" from "only if" as well, so the entire name only needs to be "only if", the "if and" is kinda redundant. But I think describing "if" and "only if" this way is still a nice way to intuit and memorize what "if and only if" means.
                  • Synonym: biconditional.
                  • Symbols
                    • ↔
                    • <->
                    • <=>

                • Related

                  • Informal representation of (bi)-conditionals

              • XOR

              • https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boolean_algebra#Secondary_operations

          • Validity and soundness

            • Validity

              • Definition

                • Validity (in this context) describes whether or not it is possible for the conclusion to be false while the premises are true. If an argument is valid and has only true premises, that means that the conclusion *must* be true, it is logically proven.

              • Limitations

                • We cannot ever know for certain that an argument is valid.
                  • Limitations of mental faculties
                • An invalid argument can still have a true conclusion, it just cannot justify that true conclusion.
                  • Examples
                    • Horses and zebras
                      • Argument
                        • Horses exist
                        • Therefore zebras exist
                      • Why it's invalid
                        • It's true that horses exist (the premise is sound).
                        • It's true that zebras exist (the conclusion is true).
                        • But it isn't logically impossible for horses to exist without zebras existing (the conclusion doesn't logically follow from the premise, so the argument is still invalid).
                  • Limitations
                    • Sometimes in informal discussions, people will intend something different from what they technically said. So maybe the intended interpretation was not deductive or involved a different premise at one point or something.
                • It's possible for a deductively invalid argument to work as a good inductive argument.
                  • Examples
                    • Rising sun
                      • Argument
                        • The sun has risen every morning so far.
                        • Therefore the sun will likely rise in the next morning.
                          • * As long as there isn't sufficient evidence to the contrary.
                      • Explanation
                        • This argument is invalid, the premise does not prove the conclusion, but it works fine as an inductive argument.
                • If term(s) are used in multiple different senses but the logic requires that the terms describe the same thing, this is called conflation and makes the logic invalid*.
                  • This is because logic only applies to well-defined propositions and therefore only those can be valid.
                  • * Technically it is possible for the logic to be valid if the two different usages are treated as distinct.

              • Related

                • Standard form

                  • A form of argument that makes it relatively easy to check the validity of the argument.

            • Soundness

              • Definitions

                • Soundness (in this context) typically refers to whether or not an argument is both valid and containing no false premises. If it meets both of these conditions, then it is a sound argument.
                • Main alternative definition
                  • Occasionally "soundness" will be defined to require *only* that the premises be true, and not that the argument is valid.
                  • This definition is often more useful than the main one if it doesn't cause confusion.
                    • Because it's often useful to describe only the truthfulness of the premises, and it's easy to say "valid and sound" in place of using the more common definition.
                • Which definition to use
                  • To avoid confusion, you can also use "valid and sound" in place of the former definition, and "sound premise(s)" in place of the latter definition.

              • Limitations

                • An argument with unsound premises can still have a true conclusion.
                  • It just cannot deductively prove the true conclusion.
                • A valid argument can still have unsound premises.
                  • If the premises are not solidly established, then the conclusion cannot be solidly established via the argument either. The logic being valid doesn't mean that the premises are true, it can only tell us the logical consequences of the premises.
                  • The conclusion can still follow from the premise(s) while the premise(s) are false.
                    • Example
                      • Premise 1: 2 + 2 = 5
                      • Premise 2: John and Mary both gave me two apples each.
                      • Conclusion: I received 5 apples from John and Mary.
                  • The conclusion cannot be established from mere validity, the premises also have to be sound.
                • We cannot ever know for certain that an argument is sound.
                  • Limitations of mental faculties
                • Oftentimes what was technically stated is unsound but what was meant is sound.

          • Formal deduction

            • Formal true and false

              • In and out of correspondence with reality, respectively.
              • Synonyms: Truth simpliciter, absolute truth

            • Importance

              • A very important process to use for formal reasoning.
                • Importance of formal reasoning
                  Relevance

            • Truth tables

              • Definition

            • Formal contradictions

              • Definition

                • When multiple well-defined propositions entail (directly or indirectly) that one proposition is both true and false.

                • Distinction

                  • This is different from a colloquial definition of "contradiction" which only requires that two things be very unlikely to both be true.
                  • This is different from a colloquial definition of "contradiction" which is synonymous with "paradox".

              • Importance

                • Recognizing them can be hard sometimes because of vague definitions
                • Recognizing them can often be hard because of how indirect they can be.

                  • Examples

                  • Sat solver
                    Link

            • Proofs

              • Definition

                • A deductive proof is a logical argument that establishes the truth of a conclusion based on the truth of one or more premises, using established rules of inference and previously accepted axioms or theorems.

                • Example

                  • "There are no married bachelors".
                  • ^ This statement can be deductively proven given the definitions of "married" and "bachelor".

              • Specific proof methods

                • Proof by contradiction
                  Confirm that this is the name and stuff

                  • Definition

                  • Importance

                • Proof by contrapositive

            • Natural deduction

              • Definition

                • In logic and proof theory

                • ^ A kind of proof calculus in which logical reasoning is expressed by inference rules closely related to the "natural" way of reasoning.
                • Specifically: A syntactic proof system which assumes inference rules as primitives.
                  Link Inference rules, create a definition for primitive and maybe section/definition for syntactic proof system.

                • Distinction

                  • This contrasts with Hilbert-style systems, which instead use axioms as much as possible to express the logical laws of deductive reasoning.

              • Undischarged assumptions

                • Definition

                  • In natural deduction

                  • ^ A premise or hypothesis in a proof that remains (at least at some relevant point(s)) active and unremoved.
                  • If a proof contains undischarged assumptions, it establishes that the conclusion follows from those assumptions, rather than being a standalone truth derived solely from axioms.

                  • Example

                • Importance / Limitations

                  • Common

                  • Impactful

                    • These assumptions are the necessary conditions on which the final conclusion depends;
                    • If they are not discharged (removed) by inference rules like Implication Introduction or Reductio Ad Absurdum, the conclusion is only true relative to those assumptions, not universally.

                • Related

                  • Vacuous discharge

                    • Definition

                      • The process of removing an assumption even if it was not used in the parent derivation.

                    • Importance

                      • Impactful

                        • Permissible in classical logic.

              • Deductive consequence (⊢)
                I don't think this is specific to natural deduction, should probably be conbined with material implication or whatever?

                • Definition

                  • Synonyms

                    • Syntactic consequence (⊢)
                    • Material implication (->)

                  • Distinction

                    • Semantic consequence (⊨)
                      Create section and link

                • Inference bar

                  • A line separating a conclusion from premises in a proof.
                  • Typically: Placed under premises and over a conclusion.

                  • Synonyms

                    • Inference line
                    • Therefore (∴)

                • Therefore (∴)
                  Maybe

                • Comma (,)

                  • In a deductive consequence

                  • ^ A separation between premises.

              • Styles

                • Fitch-style

                  • Definition

                    • A style which uses vertical lines instead of numbers to keep track of undischarged assumptions.
                    • It is a style characterized by:
                    • ^ Its linear presentation of the proof, instead of presentation as a tree.
                    • ^ Subordinate proofs, where assumptions could be opened within a subderivation and discharged later.

                • Suppes-Lemmon-style

                  • Standard form
                    Standard form may be a subset, superset or alias to "Lemmon-style".

                    • Definition

                      • 1. An argument in standard form puts the premises first, and the conclusion last.
                      • 2. The premises and conclusion are labelled, so they can be referred to easily.
                      • 3. An inference bar is placed between the premises and the conclusion.
                        "Therefore" should get its own section probably.
                        • The inference bar indicates that the conclusion is intended to follow from the premises above it.
                        • It's essentially synonymous with the "therefore" symbol (∴).

                      • Example

                        • Informal:

                        • ^ There’s no need to stop using fossil fuels unless climate change is real. But climate change is fake. So there’s no need to stop using fossil fuels.

                        • Standard form:

                        • ^ P1) There’s no need to stop using fossil fuels unless climate change is real.
                        • ^ P2) Climate change is fake
                        • ^ \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_
                        • ^ C) There’s no need to stop using fossil fuels.
                        • Note that the ‘So’ that indicates the conclusion has been left out of the standard form.
                          • That’s because its role is to indicate where the conclusion is.
                          • It is not part of the content of the statement the arguer is trying to get you to believe.
                        • Because there are multiple premises, we number them.
                          • It does not matter what order you put the premises in, in relation to one another.
                        • In standard form, the premises must occur above the conclusion they are intended to support.

                    • Importance / limitations

                      • Impactful

                        • Standard form makes it much easier to assess the validity of arguments.
                          • The standard form of an argument is the stripped-down version of the argument that has the pure reasoning involved, without any of the unnecessary rhetoric.
                            • This allows for the argument to be assessed on its merits as an argument alone.
                        • Creating standard form arguments tends to take longer.
                          • We tend to be much more accustomed to dealing with informal arguments than formal ones.
                        • Translating informal arguments to standard arguments usually takes a bit.
                          • We tend to be much more accustomed to informal arguments than formal ones

                      • Importance of informal arguments

                    • Translating informal to standard form

                      • The statements occurring in standard form are often rephrased so that they express their content in a straightforward way.

                        • In ordinary language, people sometimes use questions or commands to communicate statements. When we put the argument into standard form, we rephrase them as statements.

                        • Example

                          • Suppose someone says:
                          • University education was free for our parents’ generation. Shouldn’t it be free for us?
                          • This is an argument: the person is trying to persuade you of something, and they are giving a reason. The reason is “University education was free for our parents’ generation”. “Shouldn’t it free for us?” has the form of a question, but it’s being asked rhetorically. The person who gave the argument is not asking a sincere question: they are using the question to make a point. The statement the arguer intends is “University education should be free for us”. When we put the argument into standard form, each premise and conclusion is rephrased so that it is expressed as a statement.
                          • P1) University education was free for our parents’ generation.
                          • \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_
                          • C) University education should be free for us.
                          • Rhetorical questions are one common way of making statements in ordinary language. Also common is expressing statements as commands. So, someone might say:
                          • University education was free for our parents’ generation. Make it free for us!
                          • Once again, a correct standard form rendition of the argument will rephrase the command as a statement, resulting in the same formal argument shown above.

                      • Each statement should be complete in itself.

                        • Importance

                          • Each statement is expected to be assessable on its own, so that both the Validity and Soundness can be assessed in a systematic manner.

                        • Example

                          • Consider the following argument:
                          • Barack Obama was a great president. He was always dignified and composed.
                          • The sentence “He was always dignified and composed” is incomplete, because we don’t know, when considering the sentence by itself, who is referred to by “he”. To make the statement complete, all we need to do is replace the “he” with the person it stands for. This is clear from the context. So, in standard form:
                          • P1) Barack Obama was always dignified and composed.
                          • \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_
                          • C) Barack Obama was a great president.

                      • Omitting rhetoric

                        • Importance

                          • Common

                            • It is often the case that putting an argument into standard form will require omitting a lot of what was said.

                        • Useful questions to ask during this process

                          • What is the person trying to persuade me of?
                          • What reasons do they give to support that?

                        • Example

                          • Informal:

                          • ^ Vegans are a bunch of puppy-kissing tree-huggers. They seem to think everybody should be vegan just because they are. Everyone is entitled to their own opinion. Besides, they give lousy reasons for being vegan: just as many animals are hurt through harvesting of plants as die in animal farming.
                          • The conclusion is not explicitly stated here, but the main reason is clear.

                          • Formal:

                          • ^ P1) Just as many animals are hurt through harvesting of plants as die in animal farming.
                          • \_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_\_
                          • ^ C) It is not the case that everyone should be vegan.
                          • You may think something is missing from the above argument. You are right. In chapter 3 we will look at how to make such standard form reconstructions more complete. Before we do so, we need to look at one important aspect of what makes a good argument: validity.
                            Yoink more from https://oercollective.caul.edu.au/howtothinkcritically/front-matter/introduction/ to resolve this part.

                      • https://oercollective.caul.edu.au/howtothinkcritically/chapter/standard-form/

          • Rules

            • Importance

              • Crucial for performing formal deduction.
                • Importance of performing formal deduction.
                  Relevance

            • Replacement rules

              • Definition

                • Rules that apply in both directions. Either side can be replaced with the other.

                • Distinction

                  • Some of these rules come in pairs, split with a "|". These pairs cannot be swapped. Only expressions on either side of a ↔ may be swapped.

              • Contraposition (Contra)
                P → Q ↔ -Q → -P

                • Synonym: Transposition

              • Double negation (-E / DN / -- Elim)
                --P ↔ P

                • Synonyms: double negation elimination, negation elimination

                • Double negation introduction
                  P ∴ --P

              • Demorgan's laws (DM / DeM)
                -(P ^ Q) ↔ -P v -Q | -(P v Q) ↔ -P ^ -Q

                • Negation of conjunction
                  -(P ^ Q) ↔ -P v -Q

                • Negation of disjunction
                  -(P v Q) ↔ -P ^ -Q

              • Distribution (Dist)
                (P v Q) ^ R ↔ (P ^ R) v (Q ^ R) | (P ^ Q) v R ↔ (P v R) ^ (Q v R)

              • Conjunction introduction (^I)
                P, Q ↔ P ^ Q

              • Exportation (Exp)
                (P^Q) → R ↔ P → (Q→R)

                • Reducible

              • Material equivalance (Equiv)
                (P ↔ Q) ↔ [(P → Q) ^ (Q → P)] | (P ↔ Q) ↔ (P ^ Q) v (-P ^ -Q)

                • Logical connectives

              • Material implication (Impl)
                P → Q ↔ -P v Q | -(P → Q) ↔ P ^ -Q

                • Reducible to: The definitions of material conditional (→ ), disjunction (v) and conjunction (^).
                  See this entry's note for more details on how this might be wrong and an alternative way to look at it.

              • Commutative (Com)
                P ^ Q ↔ Q ^ P | P v Q ↔ Q v P

                • Reducible to: The definitions of conjunction (^) and disjunction (v), respectively.
                  I think. But if not, then maybe the principle of induction or similar. Like we've done this operation without issue so many times, therefore likely reliable. Or it can be expressed in truth tables and truth tables have been shown to be reliable via induction, etc.

              • Associative (Assoc)
                (P ^ Q) ^ R ↔ P ^ (Q ^ R) | (P v Q) v R ↔ P v (Q v R)

                • Reducible to: Commutative

            • Inference rules
              Add more from classically valid [...] section of https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Propositional_logic

              • Definition

              • Modus tollens (MT)
                P→Q, -Q ∴ -P

                • Reducible

                  • Maybe reducible to doing
                  • Contraposition to get -Q → -P
                  • and then modus ponens to get -P

              • Disjunction introduction (vI / Add)
                P ∴ P v Q

              • Constructive dilemma
                P→Q, R→S, P v R ∴ Q v S

                • Modus ponens applied to both possibilities of the OR.

              • Destructive dilemma
                P→Q, R→S, -Q v -S ∴ -P v -R

                • Modus tollens applied to both possibilities of the OR.
                  There should probably just be a rule for being able to account for both possibilities of the OR, and then a reducible section can be created here and for the constructive dilemma.

              • Disjunctive syllogism (DS / MTP)
                P v Q, -P ∴ Q

                • Synonym: modus tollendo ponens

              • Disjunction elimination (vE)
                P v R, P→Q, R→Q ∴ Q

                • Synonyms: or elimination, proof by cases, case analysis

              • Conjunction elimination (^E / Simp)
                P ^ Q ∴ P

              • Hypothetical syllogism
                P→Q, Q→R ∴ P→R

                • Reducible

                  • Subproof?

              • Modus ponens (→E / MP)
                P→Q, P ∴ Q

                • Synonym: Conditional elimination

              • Absorption
                P→Q ∴ P→(P ^ Q)

                • Reducible to: Modus ponens and conjunction introduction
                  Need to show that conditional proof is reducible too or use an alternative method.

                  • 1. P→Q (P)
                    Absorption premise
                  • 2. | P (Hyp)
                  • 3. | Q (2,3 modus ponens)
                  • 4. | P ^ Q (4 conjunction introduction)
                  • 5. P→(P ^ Q) (2-4 CP)

              • Premise (P / Supp)
                P

                • Synonyms: supposition

              • Assumption (Hyp / Assump / ACP)
                P

                • A temporary premise used to begin a subproof.
                • ACP as in "assumption for conditional proof".

              • Reiteration (R)
                P ∴ P

                • Reducible to: Law of identity

            • Other techniques

              • Indirect proof (-I / IP)
                (Q ^ -Q) is proven using assumption P ∴ -P

                • Synonym: Negation introduction
                • Reducible to: Law of contradiction

              • Conditional proof (→I / CP)
                Q is proven using assumption P ∴ P → Q

                • The process whereby a sub-proof is provided, and a conditional is proven from it.
                • Synonym: Conditional introduction

                • Example

                  • 1. | (P → Q) ∧ P (hyp)
                  • 2. | P → Q (1 ^E)
                  • 3. | P (1 ^E)
                  • 4. | Q (2,3 MP)
                  • C. ((P → Q) ∧ P) → Q (1-4 CP)

            • Meta

              • The colors for the grandchildren of Rules (aside from definition, importance, meta and related sections) are just used for easier navigation/memorization, and not intended to be interpreted in the usual way. Their meanings are roughly as follows:
              • Red: Introduces/eliminates a negation (-).
              • Orange: Introduces/eliminates either a disjunction (v) or conjunction (^).
              • Yellow: Introduces/eliminates a disjunction (v).
              • Green: Introduces/eliminates a conjunction (^).
              • Blue: Introduces/eliminates a conditional (→) or biconditional (↔).
              • Purple: Non-traditional rules or simple tautologies.
              • Within each color category, typically "introduction"-esque rules/equivalences/etc. will come first, then "elimination" ones.

        • Theorems

          • Definition

            • A statement that can be proven with no undischarged assumptions (i.e., all assumptions are discharged).

          • Importance

            • Common

            • Impactful

          • The law of excluded middle

            • Definition

              • All well-defined propositions must either be true or false.

            • Reducible

              • The definition of well-defined proposition.
                This feels wrong, because it feels like "well-defined proposition" should be defined more in terms of lacking ambiguity than having a definitive true/false state. The latter seems more like a necessary consequence than a definition to me. But given the definition I put together based on similar terms, this seems right, so I'll leave it for now.
              • Assuming the above doesn't work, there is also this natural deduction proof:
              • 1. | -(P v -P) (assumption)
              • 2. | | P (assumption)
              • 3. | | (P v -P) (putting 2 in an 'or')
              • 4. | | False (contradiction with 1)
              • 5. | -P (proof by contradiction, 2-4)
              • 6. | (P v -P) (putting 5 in an 'or')
              • 7. | False (contradiction with 1)
              • 8. --(P v -P) (proof by contradiction, 1-7)
              • 9. P v -P (double negation, 8)

      • Probabilistic reasoning

        • Definition

          • A variety of methods of reasoning in which the conclusion of an argument is supported at best with some degree of probability*.
            • Probability: Almost always credence, but very occasionally propensity depending on the context.

          • Distinct from standard deduction

            • In probabilistic reasoning, the conclusion does not [follow with certainty when the argument is valid and sound] like it does in deduction.
              • This doesn't mean that there isn't any uncertainty involved when using standard deduction.
                • We cannot ever know for certain that an argument is valid
                • We cannot ever know for certain that an argument is sound
              • And the reasoning justifying the probabilistic inference rules can be reduced to standard deduction, so ultimately probabilistic reasoning is still reducible to deduction.

                • Reducible

            • Eg. Mathematical induction
              • Kind of deceptively named, since it's actually a form of standard deduction and not induction as defined here.

        • Importance / Limitations

          • Common

            • A massive portion of reasoning is probabilistic.

          • Impactful

            • Probabilistic reasoning can never justify literal absolute certainty.
            • Typically if all the main easily accounted for factors are taken into account, probabilistic reasoning cannot justify a very high or low probability.

          • Improvable

            • There's a lot of very impactful and typically improperly or inadequately taught aspects of probabilistic reasoning that are taught better here.
              Will be*, once the guide is much more complete.

          • Difficult

            • Probabilistic biases

        • How to view it

          • Probabilistic reasoning should typically be thought of as the weaker, less reliable but often more widely available sibling to standard deductive reasoning.
            • While standard deductive reasoning is by no means rarely applicable, there are a vast amount of important situations in which exclusively standard deduction cannot take you all the way to a useful conclusion, but alongside probabilistic reasoning, can.
            • Inductive reasoning typically can't justify a very low/high probability for much
          • Probabilistic reasoning is reducible to deduction, it just isn't regarded as a part of standard deduction.

        • Reducible

          • It's just a useful higher level of abstraction, so we don't have to deal with so many deductive steps all the time.
          • All probabilistic reasoning is just the deductive application of some axioms, with the uncertainty coming from the principle of induction (or similar) and the semi-reliability of mental faculties. (link)

          • Known associations

            • Using the uncertain definition of "known".
              • Know (probability)

        • Induction
          Add stuff from https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inductive_reasoning

          • Definition

            • the process of inferring some general principle from a body of knowledge, where the principle does not necessarily follow from the body of knowledge.
              • The body of knowledge might give us very good reason to accept the principle, but never ensures it for certain.

            • Example: White/black swans

              • If all swans that a person has observed so far are white, they may infer a universal categorical proposition of the form "All swans are white". They have good reason to believe the conclusion from the premise because it is the best explanation for their observations, but the truth of the conclusion is not guaranteed. Indeed, it turns out that some swans are black.

          • Importance / Limitations

            • Common

            • Impactful

            • Improvable

              • Most people tend to apply a lot of specific and selective forms of induction, but tend discount many broad and abstract forms without good reason.

                • Bias towards the specific/"grounded"

        • Fundamentals

          • Important definitions: credence and plausibility

          • Probabilistic axioms

            • Definition

              • Fundamental rules about probability.

              • Distinction

                • Not necessarily unsupported like some definitions of "axioms".

            • The probability of all events are non-negative real numbers

            • Assumption of unit measure

              • The probability [that at least one of the elementary events in the entire sample space will occur] is 1.

            • σ-additivity

              • https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sigma-additive_set_function

          • Evidence

            • Definitions

              • Here defined as information that epistemically supports a proposition.
                • This includes all information that epistemically opposes propositions, since for any proposition there is a negation.
                  • If X opposes Y, then X supports the proposition "Y is false".
              • Often defined more narrowly, like information that epistemically supports a proposition by a quite substantial amount.

          • Principle of indifference

            • Definition

              • The principle that in the absence of any relevant evidence, agents should distribute their credence (or "degrees of belief") equally among all the possible outcomes under consideration.

              • Synonym

                • Principle of insufficient reason

            • Importance / limitations

              • Impactful

                • The principle of indifference tends to work well.
                • A uniform distribution typically requires fewer assumptions than other distributions.

                  • Occam's razor
                    Relevance

                • Only works for cases where we have no information to favor any particular outcome.
                  • Only works for cases where the possibility spaces of each of the considered possibilities are the same size.

                    • Occam's razor

            • Reducible
              Ideally be more specific here.

              • A proof by contradiction using fundamental axioms of probability.
              • If we are to assign a greater probability than 1/possibilities without any evidence, it would seem to result in a contradiction with said axioms.

            • Advanced usage

              • Breaking down [more complex cases where this principle cannot be applied] into multiple cases, in which some can have the principle applied.

          • 50% default
            Intermediate

            • Definition

              • The concept that every well-defined proposition should be assigned 50% credence in the absence of any (other) information.

            • Importance

              • Can be very useful for systematically deriving probabilities, as it encourages accounting for every relevant factor explicitly.
              • If not all substantial intuitive factors are explicitly accounted for, the result of starting from the beginning may be worse than if we hadn't bothered.
                • It would typically still be more accurate, even in these cases.
                  Impact
                  • Our intuition is very unreliable.
                    Link
                • It's usually not too difficult to account for all substantial intuitive factors.
                  Likelihood
                  • Our minds are typically (almost definitionally) quite ready to offer intuitive factors when we look for them.

            • Reducible
              This isn't quite complete, but probably close enough for a while.

              • Premise 1: 50% of well-defined propositions are true, and 50% are false.
                • Premise 1: 50% of well-defined propositions are the inverse of the other 50% of well-defined propositions.
                  • All well-defined propositions have exactly one inverse.
                  • Premise 2: A well-defined proposition must either be true or false (i.e. the law of excluded middle)
                • Premise 2: A well-defined proposition must either be true or false. (i.e. https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=V99dOKyaHrazTWcoUt-ly_eC)
                • Premise 3: The inverse of a true well-defined proposition is a false well-defined proposition and vise versa. (definition of inverse)
              • Premise 2: Principle of indifference
              • Conclusion: Well-defined propositions have (without accounting for any other information) a 50% chance of being true and a 50% chance of being false.

            • Objections

              • What about cases where it's quite obvious that something has <50% likelihood, even when we're running blind?
                • Example: we have no information about Russell's teapot, but we all obviously believe that there is no such teapot.
                  • It seems like when considering the likelihood of Russell's teapot, our assignment of a low probability is based on evidence.
                  • For example it seems like the main reason is abductive. It would simply be much simpler to suppose that no such teapot existed, because supposing that it is there seems to require a greater number of assumptions.
                    Link to relevant abduction section(s) maybe?
                  • Russell's Teapot is typically used to support the concept of a burden of proof, which is often a misleading heuristic, see <section to be linked> for more info on why.

              • p(a) = 1 - p(not a)

          • Zipf distribution of evidence weight

            • Definition

              • The distribution of weight for different pieces of evidence for a given hypothesis tends to follow Zipf's law.

            • Importance

                • The authors of this guide have consistently observed this.
                  Likelihood
                • Zipf's law) supports this.
                  Likelihood
                • Possible to resolve virtually any disagreement with reasonable people thanks in part to this.
                  Relevance
                  • It drastically limits the median amount of possible relevant irriducible disagreements that have to be explored to resolve the initial disagreement.
                    Likelihood | Maybe move elsewhere in "zipf distribution of evidence weight" importance and link here? | Link definition to "irreducible disagreement".

        • Abduction

          • Definition

            • Abduction is a form of logical inference that seeks the simplest and most likely conclusion from a set of observations.
              Look into whether "simplest" is defined specifically or broadly.

            • Compared to other forms of probabilistic reasoning

              • While induction draws general conclusions that apply to many situations, abductive conclusions are confined to the particular observations in question.
                Link induction

            • Synonyms

              • Abductive reasoning
              • Abductive inference
              • Retroduction

            • Wikipedia

            • Examples

              • Two friends frequently go jogging together. They have a major argument, and therefore a falling out. However, the next week, you see them jogging together again, and assume they made up.
                • Source: https://rationalwiki.org/wiki/Reasoning
              • Observing a billiard game and seeing the 'eight' ball moving towards us, and abducing that the cue struck the ball.
                • Source: https://rationalwiki.org/wiki/Reasoning

          • How to view it

            • An often helpful form of probabilistic reasoning.

          • Explanation / hypothesis
            Probably incorporate "explanation" terminology in other sections where reasonable?

            • Definition

              • A claim (or set of claims) intended to explain/account for some data.

            • Comparing explanations

          • Prior probability

            • Definition

              • The credence that should be given to a hypothesis before considering the main evidence.

                • Main evidence: The main evidence that would typically* be considered.
                  • * Except when the evidence that would typically be considered is prior evidence.
              • Context: Otherwise known as simply "prior" in the right kind of probabilistic context.

            • Importance

              • Impactful: Prior probabilies are typically one of the most impactful unknowns.
                • It's important to account for unknowns.
                  Relevance
              • Improvable: Obtaining the prior probability of a claim just involves looking at the ratio of scenarios under which it could be true vs couldn't be true, which is usually pretty easy to estimate.

            • Typical prior evidence

              • Usually priors use occam's razor and nothing else.
                Check if this is right.

            • All prior evidence

              • Occam's razor

          • Occam's Razor

            • Definitions

              • The problem-solving principle that recommends searching for explanations constructed with the smallest possible set of assumptions.

              • Synonyms

                • The principle of parsimony.
                • The law of parsimony.

              • Other relevant definitions (for other terms)
                Move to definitions and link here

                • Broadness/simplicity and specificity/complexity
                  Probably move to definitions but retain a copy of the definitions here.
                  • How few and many (respectively) logically irreducible suppositions have to be made.
                  • Logically irreducible supposition
                    • A supposition that is unable to be logically reduced any further.
                    • Logically irreducible
                      • "Logically irreducible" is here defined as something like "cannot be reduced to multiple smaller elements of formal logic".
                      • This is because without this "floor" on complexity, it's very unclear how to properly count the number of assumptions. And this floor is grounded in the nature of the recommended axioms.
                        Relevance
                    • Supposition
                      • "Supposition" is here defined as "proposition that is not epistemically justified by anything aside from simplicity."
                    • Note that this is a different concept from metaphysical irreducibility.
                  • Usually the terminology used is "complexity" and "simplicity", but the definitions used in the context of Occam's razor translate roughly to measures of specificity/broadness.
                • Possibility
                  • In this context: events or series' of events that are subjectively possible.
                    • "subjectively possible" being defined similarly to how "subjective probability" is defined.
                      • Credence
                • Possibility space
                  • Possibility

              • Distinction

                • Different from "The simplest answer is the correct one.", since this is not always the case.

              • Examples

                • Russel's Teapot

                • Horse vs zebra

                  • You hear hoofbeats outside, is it a horse or a zebra? Unless you live in the African savannah, it's very unlikely to be a zebra. We'd need more assumptions to get there - a zebra was imported to a local zoo, it escaped captivity, and now it's running amok. Whereas a horse would typically require fewer assumptions. That doesn't mean that it cannot be a zebra, it just means that the prior probability for the horse hypothesis appears to be higher.

                • Left out pickles

              • For more info, visit here: Wikipedia

            • Importance

              • Common

                • Even when it's not directly mentioned by name, you'll see it frequently pop up in terms like "what, you think things just happened to end up that way?"

              • Impactful

                • Reliable

                  • Reducible to solid principles that can be applied systematically.
                    Likelihood

                  • Feels unreliable
                    Likelihood

                    • This is likely in part caused by the bias towards the specific/"grounded"
                      Relevance
                      • Occam's razor typically doesn't feel specific/solid/grounded, it feels abstract and vague.
                        Likelihood
                      • Occam's razor can be specific/grounded, it just isn't typically used in a proper systematic manner.
                        Relevance
                        • This guide teaches how to use it in a proper systematic manner.
                          Relevance

                • Useful if reliable

                  • Generally powerful

                    • Specificity can often provide a very strong case against hypotheses that seem otherwise plausible.
                      Relevance
                    • ^ When you add properties to a hypothesis, the probabilities of the properties initially get multiplied together, quickly resulting in a much lower prior probability.
                      Likelihood

                  • Rejecting the absurd

                    • Occam's razor is one of the most common tools we use to reject absurd hypotheses.
                      Needs to be justified

                    • Examples

                  • In the absence of other evidence

                    • Occam's razor provides us with epistemic justification to place a lower probability on even things that lack any evidence for/against it other than complexity.

            • Reducible
              Make this much more clear.

              • 50% default

                • Via the principle of indifference, we can conclude that logically irreducible assumptions should by default be considered ~50% likely. Thus the more logically irreducible assumptions are involved in a hypothesis, the more unlikely it tends to be.

              • Induction

                • Occam's razor tends to work well, so via induction we can say that it will likely continue to work well.

              • "Complexity" translates to possibility space size

                • Definitions

                  • Possibility
                  • Possiblity space
                • The set of possibilities where a given specific possibility occurs is typically smaller than the set of possibilities where a given broader possibility occurs.
                  • Because specificity/broadness tends to refer to the amount of components required for something to be the case, i.e. the number of set divisions specified.

                • Example

                  • If there's a ball in a box and that ball could be any of the 7 colors of the rainbow and you have pretty much no reason to think that it's more likely to be any particular color, then you should place an equal probability on all colors, meaning they all have a 1/7 chance of being the color of the ball.
                  • The hypothesis "the ball is not red" is less specific than the hypothesis "the ball is red". The latter is more specific, so the set of possibilities where the ball is red is smaller (1) than the set of possibilities where the ball is not red (6).

            • Common razor heuristics
              Current WIP

              • Definition

                • Common heuristics derived from Occam's Razor.

              • Importance / Limitations

                • Impactful

                  • They are semi-unreliable

                • Improvable

                  • Your reasoning has likely been influenced at least a bit by bad ones.
                    • They are commonly used and internalized in some form or another.
                      Likelihood
                    • These heuristics are largely pretty unreliable.
                      Likelihood
                      • reliability of "first prioritize simplicity"
                        Likelihood
                      • Reliability of "equally/similarly" heuristic
                        Likelihood

              • Only applies to hypotheses that explain observations equally/similarly well

                • Definition

                  • Occam's Razor does not apply when one theory explains observations better.

                  • Example

                    • Quantum mechanics is more complicated than Newtonian mechanics, but it explains certain observations better, so Occam's razor doesn't apply.
                      • It's not quite as obvious as one might expect that QM is more complex than Newtonian physics.
                        • Most of the complexity of QM that we're familiar with is emergent from much simpler rules.
                          Likelihood
                        • Much of QM is made much less comprehensible by the fact that it involves a kind of probabilistic process that's very unfamiliar to us.
                          Likelihood
                      • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=rcyeuef9zWsNYMnUImsDLk7a

                • Importance / Limitations

                  • Impactful

                    • Occam's Razor should often apply at least somewhat in cases where relative explanatory power has more impact on the posterior probability than relative prior probability.
                      Link explanatory power and prior probability
                      • The prior probability often still has enough of an impact on the posterior that it's worth taking into account.
                        Likelihood
                      • ^ Even a difference of 1 assumption means one side has at least twice the relative prior probability as it had before.
                        Likelihood | Not sure about this
                    • It's very rare that we reason about two hypotheses for more than a few moments and justifiably find that they explain the relevant observations exactly equally well.
                      Should likely support
                      • This doesn't address the "similarly well" interpretation of the heuristic.
                        Relevance

              • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=Rr2KTtxTD333wxnIG5iUuJS3

          • Bayesian inference
            ~~Adept

            • Definition

              • Bayesian inference is a specific method for calculating a probability of a hypothesis given prior evidence and new information.
              • The hypothesis is sometimes referred to as an unobserved variable.
                I think
              • Inverse probability is an old term for the probability distribution of an unobserved variable.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Very generalizable

                  • Bayesian inference can be generalized to many different kinds of problems, many of which involve unintuitive correct answers and intuitive incorrect answers, so it is a powerful way to overcome our intuitive limitations when it comes to probabilities.
                    Split

              • Improvable

                • We make it easy to use

                  • We provide calculators and teach how to use them.

            • Calculators

              • You can use this Bayesian calculator to more intuitively grasp how it works and more easily perform Bayesian reasoning.
                There are likely better calculators out there so if anyone finds them, please let me know.
                • The way to use it is:
                • 1. Assign the prior probability (the probability of a given hypothesis before accounting for whatever new information you want to account for) by dragging the black dot near the bottom to the appropriate place (eg. 0.1 = 10%).
                • 2. Adjust the hypothesis' probability given the new info (without accounting for the prior) by dragging the yellow dot on the left to the appropriate place.
                • 3. Adjust the probability of the hypothesis being false given the new information (without accounting for the prior) by dragging the blue dot on the right to the appropriate place.
                • After this, the bar at the bottom will tell you how probable the outcome is.
                • You can drag the black circle to that spot and repeat the process if you have another piece of new info that you would like to calculate on top of all the other info as well.

                • Another definition of "probability" that can be used in bayesian inference.
