        • General tips
          Fix this, move some into inductive biases

          • Calculating the inverse: Frequently when trying to find the probability of an event, it is most easy to calculate the probability of the event *not* occurring and then deriving the probability of it occurring using the law of complement.
            "Inverse" might cause some confusion here, due to inverse probability.
          • Multiple events of a certain probability cannot simply have their probabilities added together unless the events are disjoint (mutually exclusive).
            Make sections for disjoint and conjoint(?) events, and how to calculate probabilities as a result. And create a section for the inductive bias involved in trying to add probabilities together when they are not mutually exclusive.
            • Otherwise, rolling a 6 sided dice 6 times would guarantee a 100% chance of rolling a 6 once.
          • Compounding uncertainty: In almost all cases, all else being equal, the more steps that are involved in any kind of reasoning, the more uncertainty carries into the conclusion.
            Likely link this in many places.
            • This is because all propositions (including most inference rules) aside from axioms rely on:
              Make a section for inference rules, probably under Axioms.
            • ^ The semi-reliability of mental faculties (which, due it to being "semi-reliability", always has some uncertainty).

              • Example:
                Move or link this example in Deduction (broad).

              • When making deductive arguments, we often like to say "so long as the premises are true and the argument is valid, the conclusion is certain!"
              • ^ In itself, this is true. However the implication is often something more like "so long as we have a high confidence in the premises and a high confidence in the validity of the argument, we can know for certain that the conclusion is true!", which is obviously quite questionable.
                • It is very possible (and common) for people to accidentally create invalid arguments or rely on premises that really seem true at first glance, but on closer inspection are plausibly false. Deduction provides "certainty" only in an idealistic sense, or often a "high probability" sense. Practically it only provides a stronger inductive argument (eg. "you suppose that your mental faculties are semi-reliable and you can't see a problem with this deductive argument, so you would agree that it's probably true, right?").
            • ^ And/or the principle of induction (or similar alternatives). Which also has some uncertainty, due to the fact that we (ostensibly) do not make infinite observations.

          • Common probabilistic errors
            Maybe just move the stuff in here into the parent category.

            • Definition

              • Common probabilistic errors or areas where probabilistic errors often arise.

            • False cause

            • Lacking evidence ≠ false

              • The failure to consistently account for this.

          • Prioritize more likely hypotheses

            • Definition

              • The strategy of prioritizing hypotheses that initially appear to be more likely.

              • Examples

                • Left out pickles

                  • A man goes into his kitchen and sees a jar of pickles left out on the counter. He knows it wasn't him, which left two possibilities that their mind initially came up with:
                  • 1. A burglar broke in, stole several other items, and also ate a pickle. He left the jar out to taunt the owner.
                  • 2. The man's wife had a pickle and then forgot to put away the jar.
                  • The man could have totally checked their locks, made sure their valuables were still in the right place, etc. Instead he just yelled "Hey, did you leave this pickle jar out?" and got the simpler answer right away. Starting with the simpler solution (fewer assumptions than my burglar story) got them to the right answer efficiently.

            • Importance

              • Common

              • Impactful

                • In general, this strategy saves time.
                  • Once the correct hypothesis is found, it typically means that no more analysis is necessary. Thus prioritizing the more likely hypotheses typically results in more quickly finishing the analysis.
                    Likelihood

            • First prioritize simplicity

              • Definition

                • When trying to explain an observation, you should go with the simplest explanation first.

                • Example

                  • Forgetting Avengers

                    • You notice that the name of an old film wasn't you remembered it being. Maybe you notice a poster for the first Avengers movie and see it's called "Avengers Assemble". That can't be right, you think, you're sure it was just called The Avengers.
                    • Two explanations occur to you:
                    • 1. you misremembered it
                    • 2. you come from another universe where it was called The Avengers and you somehow jumped dimensions
                    • The second one requires more assumptions, namely the assumptions inherent in other universes existing and the possibility of travelling between them. The first assumption doesn't require any new assumptions on top of how you already understand reality, so you go with that one.
                    • But then you gather new evidence--another poster where it was called The Avengers. So what now--your first theory now doesn't work, so what do you do? Immediately adopt the second theory?
                    • No, because someone suggests a different theory. The film was released under different titles in different regions, and you saw a poster made for the UK. This isn't as simple as that first theory, but it's still simpler than the multiverse theory, so you change to that theory. And in this case that is the actual answer.
                    • So, it doesn't mean "the simplest explanation is always true", just that it's usually an easier process of arriving at the truth if you start at the simplest answer and work up
                    • The important part is that if you have two theories that explain observations similarly well then you should assume the simpler is true.
                    • Source: A Reddit comment

              • Importance / Limitations

                • Impactful

                  • Complexity = difficulty to investigate
                    Plausibly important enough to become its own section or something, and then become linked here?

                    • The more different variables or assumptions have to add up to get to a solution (i.e. the more complex the hypothesis), the more difficult it is to investigate.
                  • The more complex the hypothesis, the less likely it is to occur in general.

                  • Decently accurate

                    • Usually, there is [an obvious enough difference in simplicity] to make it quick and easy enough to estimate prior probabilities well enough to make them worth thinking of first.
                      Very unsure about this, because you can switch back and forth between considerating simplicity and other factors at first, and the effect of starting on one vs the other would be so small in that case, that other factors likely play a bigger role in which should be started with.
                      • Still isn't always the case.
                        Relevance

              • How to view it (crucial to move stuff to importance / organize)
                Likely move some of this to Occam's razor how to view it section and link it here. | Organize this better | Move a bunch of this to importance.

                • Simplicity and other factors both typically offer evidence.
                • Neither are inherently stronger than the other.
                  • In some cases simplicity is stronger, in some cases it isn't.
                    Likelihood | Link examples for both kinds of cases.
                • Getting the posterior is just a matter of combining these probabilities, which can ultimately be represented by numbers.
                • The combination method should not care which number comes first. So it shouldn't intrinsically matter whether the number comes from the simplicity or other evidence.
                • So prioritizing one kind of evidence can only be justified when it's typically stronger, typically easier to get a relatively decent estimate for quickly, or something similar.
                • And even then, it's questionable whether it's better to more quickly get into other considerations, or whether one should try to estimate both simplicity and other factors, and/or then more deeply consider both simplicity and other factors for each hypothesis.
                • Considerations for simplicity should definitely not end at prioritization of which hypotheses to consider in what order, because it's often unclear when a hypothesis should be discarded (and we are often irrationally reluctant to discard a hypothesis), and there are often unobvious considerations to have about simplicity that have a substantial impact on the prior probability (and thus also the posterior probability).

          • Emergent simplicity

            • Definition

              • The supposed phenomenon whereby for a simple question about a complex topic, the importance of the relevant components is rarely uniform, resulting in a decent guess being derivable from a much smaller set of the factors/interactions than might be expected.

        • Probabilistic biases
          Probably move to cognitive biases and link here? Also update backlinks if so.

          • Statistical bias

            • Definition

              • A bias that occurs when one's sample is unrepresentative of a corresponding population one is intending to make an inference about.

              • Example

                • Scenario: An urn contains seventy white balls and thirty red ones (the population). You pick a number of balls out (a sample), and try to infer the number of white balls vs red balls (using induction).
                • Bias: In this case, the white balls happen to be heavier, meaning that your sample (the balls that you happen pick when using the process of reaching into the urn to fetch them) is likely to be biased (unrepresentative of the population).

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • When your method of learning about the world is biased, learning more may not help. Acquiring more data can even consistently worsen a biased prediction.

        • Misc

          • Analogical reasoning

            • Definition

              • Reasoning involving a comparison of two systems in relation to their similarity. It starts from information about one system and infers information about another system based on the resemblance between the two systems.
                Make this more colloquial.
              • Wikipedia

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Common

                • People often use analogical reasoning.

              • Impactful

                • Analogical reasoning tends to be relatively weak

                  • The differences between the target idea and the allegedly analogous idea often hold important details that change whether or not the analogy works.
                  • Analogies are often used when there is a disagreement or potential disagreement. Propositions where there is relatively high (potential) disagreement tend to have more reasons for believing that particular details make them true/false.
                    Split

                  • Weak analogy

                • Analogical reasoning tends to be relatively weak

                • People often overestimate analogical reasoning

              • Difficult / Improvable

                • People often overestimate analogical reasoning

                  • Analogical reasoning tends to be relatively weak

                  • Weak analogy

          • Convergent futures

            • Definition

              • Future events which many, most or all possible timelines between now and then will lead to.
                • Possible timelines defined in terms of subjective possibility: things that are possible according to our best guesses.

              • Example

                • Ice cube melting
                  • It is incredibly difficult or near impossible to predict where exactly all the atoms of water will go during the ice cube's melting, but it is often easy to predict that the ice will completely melt despite that.

            • Importance

          • Avoiding double-counting

            • Definition

              • Avoiding counting evidence's impact twice.

            • Importance

              • Difficulty

                • Double-counting is an easy mistake to make, due to how it can often be hard to tell when we're using two long lines of evidence that are incompatible with each other but both support a conclusion we're trying to defend.

          • Feedback

            • Definition

            • Importance

              • We typically need accurate, consistent feedback in other to form the right heuristics and avoid forming the wrong ones.

            • Reasoning feedback

              • Definition (specific)

                • Here: The feedback you get for how well you performed at reasoning and how you could improve.

              • Importance

                • Typically practicing reasoning on soft science topics does not help us reason much better because we either get no feedback or unclear/misleading feedback (such as our emotions, how right/wrong we feel). So we have no good system to reinforce good habits and prune bad ones.
                • However if we treat what others say seriously, even when we really feel like it's wrong, there is a good way to use what they say as effective feedback. However we must be incredibly careful with this, because it's very easy to either take what they say too seriously or not seriously enough (usually the latter, at least in practice).
                • That is one of the reasons we place such importance on steelmanning (in the way it will be described). Without this, it's very difficult to get good feedback. Unfortunately this can become a self-perpetuating cycle, where you can't get good feedback to improve how to get good feedback. As such, we will develop a system for practicing and getting clear feedback without needing these techniques.

                • Helpful for clear communication

                  • By consistently understanding topics using concepts that apply to many different things, you'll have more very-generally-applicable-knowledge.

                • Problem types

                  • Recognizing assumptions

                    • Definition

                      • Tests involving checking if a supposed assumption is actually an assumption of a given statement.
                        • Intentionally vague, I'm not sure what interpretation of "assumption" would be best to use for the test.
                        • Obviously should be clarified if/when the test is made.

                    • Implementation note

                      • We'll have to be very careful when making this test, as it would likely often be very easy to mistakenly assume that something is an assumption of something else when it isn't.
                        • We might be best off using very abstract or toyish scenarios, or just writing assumptions which are definitely necessary for the statement.

                    • Types

                      • Premises

                        • Definition

                          • Checking if something is necessarily a premise of something else.
                        • I haven't thought about this much.

                      • <99% credence

                  • Recognizing (in)validity

                    • Definition

                      • Tests involving checking if conclusions necessarily follow from sets of premises (the sets of premises may take the form of natural language statements).

                    • Importance

                      • Practice sections on deduction seem like they could be quite beneficial for thinking of exceptions and nuances, which would be beneficial for criticalness and indirectly open mindedness.

                    • Specific problems

                      • Ferb's wager

                  • Making good inferences

                    • Definition

                      • Problems involving addressing degrees of likelihood/certainly for a conclusion given a statement, assuming that the statement is definitely true.

                      • Distinction

                        • This doesn't necessarily mean assuming that our interpretation of the statement is definitely true.

                      • Example

                        • 5-step system

                    • Types

                      • 5-step system

                        • Natural language interpretation
                          • True
                          • Probably true
                          • Not enough information
                          • Probably false
                          • False
                        • A credence interpretation
                          • c ≥ 19/20 (true)
                          • 3/5 < c < 19/20 (probably true)
                          • 2/5 ≤ c ≤ 3/5 (unsure)
                          • 1/20 < c < 2/5 (probably false)
                          • c ≤ 1/20 (false)

                          • Implementation note

                            • 5/95% might be better as 1/99% or even 0/100%, especially if we tell the test taker to select answers as if they're assuming that their interpretations are definitely (100% credence) correct.

                    • Common inductive errors

                      • We should abuse these in the problems.

                  • Judging arguments

                    • Definition

                      • Judging how weak/strong arguments are.
                        • This definition is vague and should be improved for the test if nothing else.

                  • Judging interpretations

                    • Definition

                      • A test comprised of statements and interpretations of sections of those statements, where one must answer whether the interpretations correctly interpret that section of the statement (at least when taking into account the context of the other parts of the statement).

                  • Translating formal to informal

                    • Implementation note

                      • To make an automatic system to test for this, we can use multiple choice questions.

                  • Translating informal to formal

                • Implementation types

                  • Definition

                    • Ways in which problems can be implemented.

                  • Types used

                    • Definition

                      • Which types are used during the test.

                    • 1-type

                      • Only one type can appear, so you can practice just that type.

                    • All-type

                      • Any type can appear, so you have to pay attention to all the important details that you can.

                  • Memory (optional)

                    • Definition

                      • Problems are presented and you have to remember important details about them and answer later.

                    • The way to view this

                      • Plausibly important for getting people to focus on important details about statements (especially like validity/invalidity).

                    • Pause-based

                      • Definition

                        • X seconds to read the statement, Y seconds waiting, then Z seconds to read the proposed conclusion/interpretation/etc. of the statement and answer.

                      • Times

                        • 60-5-60

                    • Juggling

                      • Definition

                        • Back and forth between reading and answering, and you have to remember important details about the problems.

                        • Example

                          • A¹,?²,B¹,A²,C¹,B²,D¹,C²

            • Skills require practice

              • Definition

                • Complicated precise behaviors typically require habits to perform consistently.

              • Importance

                • People typically don't properly internalize what they read about reasoning and implement it into their epistemic habits, because they don't practice it enough to form those habits.
                • Merely knowing how to reason well doesn't do much until you form the habits necessary to consistently apply that knowledge.
                • Common misconceptions

                • Mastery

                  • In virtually all areas* we can properly test, it seems that our subconscious typically takes a lot of https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=9SVyks7moIibu2HXE-cK1qWi to properly master topics.
                    • * minus those that are (near-)trivially simple.

              • Reasoning

                • You can learn all the theory you want about how to play the piano, but if you don't practice it, you won't be able to perform all the precise movements, even for simpler songs (unless you've practiced being able to do so).
                • Likewise this applies for mental activities that require lots of complicated precise actions.
                • You can learn all the info you want about how to play chess well, but if you don't practice it, you won't be able to act it out consistently.

              • Primary sources

                • Definition

                  • Immediate, first-hand accounts of a topic, from people who had a direct connection with it.

                  • Example
                    Give an example based on reasoning/experiment rather than direct experience, to clarify that potential ambiguity.

                • Importance

              • Credibility

          • Proactive learning

            • Post-failure review

          • Post-failure review

            • Definition

              • Reviewing the causes and trying to come up with solutions to prevent future repeats, after a failure.

            • Importance

              • Generally an incredibly underrated technique to apply to many problems that are often deemed (consciously or subconsciously) unsolvable.

            • Limitations

              • Freak accidents
                ~~Intermediate

                • Definition (specific)

                  • Freak accidents that are very unlikely to ever happen again.

                  • Distinction

                    • This is different from freak accidents that are likely to happen again. Eg. A very unlikely event that causes a plane crash should likely be accounted for even if it's very unlikely, because there are so many planes flying constantly that the chances of a repeat are usually decently high.

                • Nuance

                  • While the specific accident might be so unlikely, it may be part of a category of possible failures which has a small set of solutions required to prevent most of the possible failures. In this case, while the specific failure may not be worth focusing on for its own sake, it may be worth focusing on to better understand the category and how to prevent similar failures from occurring.
                • These are sometimes so unlikely to happen again that it's probably not worth trying to prevent repeats.

              • Unimpactful failures

                • Definition (specific)

                  • Failures that are so unimpactful compared to the cost to solve them, that they aren't worth solving for their own sake.

                • Nuance

                  • While the specific accident might be so unimpactful, it may be part of a category of possible failures which has a small set of solutions required to prevent most of the possible failures. In this case, while the specific failure may not be worth focusing on for its own sake, it may be worth focusing on to better understand the category and how to prevent similar failures from occurring.
                • These are sometimes so unimpactful that it's probably not worth trying to prevent repeats.

            • Finding the causes

              • Impact

                • Definition (specific)

                  • Causes that have the greatest impact on the potential for similar future failures.

                • Limitation

                  • Cost

                    • High impact causes may not be worth considering if the cost is too high.

              • Cost

                • Definition

                  • Causes that have a low cost to prevent.

                • Limitation

                  • Impact

                    • Uncostly-to-prevent causes may not be worth considering if they have low impact.

                • Direct

                  • Definition

                    • The upfront obvious cost(s).

                    • Example

                      • The financial cost.

                • Indirect

                  • Risks

                  • Other negative consequences

            • Finding solution(s)

              • Solution(s) to the specific problem

              • Solution(s) to similar problem(s)

              • Contingencies

                • ^ ^ One's ability to explain something tends to be highly correlated with their intuitive understanding of the topic.
                • ^ ^ ^ A good intuitive understanding tends to be preceded by understanding that something in terms of more familiar things. And more familiar things tend to be understood by more people.

            • Common misconceptions

              • Definition

                • Common misconceptions about learning.

              • We are sponges

                • Definition

                  • The feeling/idea that we are information sponges who merely need to hear things (often only once) in order to fully incorporate them into our behavior.

                • Importance

                  • Even when we are consciously aware that this is wrong, we often still feel as though it is right.

                    • Example

                      • When we listen/read a ton more material than we can possibly internalize at once, because it makes us feel like we're making more progress than we would otherwise feel like we're making.

                • Related

                  • Skills require practice

              • Little practice = mastery

                • Definition

                  • The feeling/idea that we only need to practice a little bit in order to become masters.

                • Importance

                • Common causes

                  • The feeling/belief that proactive practice can speed up mastery by more than it can.
                  • The feeling/belief that we are sponges (at least largely).

                • Related

                  • Skills require practice

              • Mastery is unattainable

                • Definition

                  • The feeling/idea that we could never be really fantastic at something(s).

                • Importance

                  • Usually we underestimate how much we can improve with the right approach.

                • Nuance

                  • Sometimes this is (at least practically) true.

          • Bad habits

            • Definition

              • Habits that you would rather not have.

            • Importance

                • The vast majority of our bigger mistakes are typically partially caused by bad habits.

              • Common

                • Bad habits influence a massive amount of behavior for virtually everyone.

            • Removing bad habits
              Possibly somewhat combine/replace with changing habits section

              • Importance / Limitations

                • Impactful

                  • Requires finding them

                  • Requires training

              • How to view it

                • Something we should virtually always be trying to do.

              • 0. Prepare to remove the bad habit

                • These are optional practices in no particular order:

                • Find a habit to replace it

                  • Importance: This often makes it feel more natural and easier, since you can focus on doing the alternative thing(s) rather than the thing you would have otherwise done.

                • Change motivators

                  • Definition

                    • Motivator (in this context): Factor that motivates us to perform an act [we have a bad habit or want to have a good habit] to perform.

                  • Bad motivator changes

                    • Definition

                      • Good/bad (in this context): Things we think would lead to better or worse habits, respectively.
                        • Distinction: Different from ethically/axiologically good/bad.
                          • It's possible for it to be best (especially temporarily) to keep a bad habit or remove a good habit for various reasons.

                    • Remove bad motivators

                    • Don't add bad motivators

                      • Example: You identify a bad habit to eat unhealthy food, and to make it easier to avoid, you stop buying unhealthy food.

                  • Good motivator changes

                    • Definition

                    • Limitation

                      • Typically reduces intrinsic motivation for the good behavior

                    • Add good motivators

                      • Example: Get someone to hold you accountable.
                        • Importance: Can often be very effective.
                        • Limitation: Such people often pressure us in a way that can cause bigger issues.
                        • Limitation: Such people often assume that certain things will help you, when they won't. This can cause issues.

                    • Don't remove good motivators

              • 1. Learn to recognize when you're doing it

                • 1.1 Learn to recognize when you're in situations where you will likely do it.
                • 1.2 Learn to ask yourself questions to check if you are doing it.

              • 2. When you realize that you're doing it, avoid following the bad habit

                • 2.1 And instead do what you want to learn to form a different habit to do, if there is one.

              • 3. Regularly practice the habits that you want to replace the bad ones with

                • 3.1 Find good replacement habits.
                • 3.2 Form a plan to make yourself practice the good replacement habits well and consistently.
                  • 3.2.1 Principles for practicing habits well.
                  • 3.2.2 Principles for practicing habits consistently.
              • Source: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Undoing_(psychology)

        • Intellectual humility

          • Definition

            • General ability to avoid being overconfident.

          • Importance

            • Impactful

              • Extremely important for avoiding close-minded tendencies.
              • ^ Close-minded tendencies are one of the largest contributors to irrationality ever.
                Support
            • The importance of learning about close-mindedness

          • Open-mindedness (broad)

            • Definition (broad)

              • Skill to change your mind when it is most reasonable to.

              • Distinction

                • Distinct from one's agreeableness
                  • Although this is typically correlated.
                • Distinct from the degree to which one is accepting of others in general.
                  • Although this is still plausibly correlated.

            • Importance / limitations

              • Common

                • Close mindedness is very common.
                  • If it weren't, people wouldn't disagree so much on topics with a lot of "Soft" reasoning
                  • Examples of close mindedness being very common

              • Impactful

                • For those who use the right strategies, it's typically much more impactful than criticalness because of critical piggybacking
                • For those who use the wrong strategies, it's very bad.
                  • Examples of lacking epistemic humility being extremely bad for reasoning
                    Move this to broader section about how people tend to be irrational.
                    • Three out of three Verizon employees believe that $0.002 = ¢0.002
                • The usefulness of open-mindedness is very dependent on knowledge and criticalness.

                  • Criticalness

                    • With insufficient criticalness (and/or insufficient Open-mindedness (broad)), it's going to be very difficult to properly filter the information you recieve and determine what makes the most sense.

                  • Knowledge

                    • If you don't have the important info in the first place, the ability to change your mind in accordance with it won't do much good.

              • Improvable

                • Open mindedness is determined by mental habits, and mental habits can almost always be changed.

              • Difficult

                • Difficulty of measuring

            • How to view it

              • Reasoning abilities are mostly determined by open mindedness, so it is one of the most important things to get right.

                • "Soft" reasoning makes up a large portion of important reasoning.

            • Causes of close-mindedness

              • Emotions from open/close mindedness

                • Confidence

                  • High confidence

                    • It almost always feels cool and satisfying to be confident, so long as it doesn't feel misguided.
                    • It feels like you're the strong, powerful, capable one who firmly knows the truth.

                  • Low confidence

                    • Less respect

                      • Being less confident typically makes you appear weaker, less reliable, less cool and less attractive.

                    • Less trust

                      • People trust confidence way too much, so having less confidence means way less trust, even when the opposite makes the most sense.
                        Link to cognitive bias on this in future.

                • Being "right"/"wrong"

                  • Being "right"

                    • It feels cool and satisfying to feel right and like you have the truth.
                    • You're the capable one who's correct, who's aligned with reality, who knows best.

                  • Being "wrong"

                    • Embarrassment

                      • Often, accepting that you were wrong means embarrassment, especially if it was because of a pretty obvious error.

                    • Lack of recognition

                      • Unfortunately people don't typically appreciate or compliment people for recognizing their own errors, despite how important and rare it is to do.

              • Close-minded close-mindedness

                • Definition

                  • Being close-minded about how close-minded they themselves are or could be.

                • Importance

                  • Common

                  • Impactful

                    • It makes it much more difficult to become more open-minded.
                      • If one can't recognize their own close mindedness (or the plausibility of it), they have much less reason and motivation to proactively improve.
                    • At the very least, it is important to seriously consider the possibility that you are slightly close-minded in the particular area you are discussing in the vast majority of cases.
                      • Even if there's only a 0.1% chance that you're somewhat close-minded about the topic, that's still enough to justify taking it seriously because the cost of taking it seriously is so low and the reward of fixing it is potentially very high.

                • How to view it

                  • One of the most important areas to be open-minded about is the possibility of your own close-mindedness.

                • Defensiveness about cm cm

                  • Definition

                    • Getting defensive about the idea that we could be close-minded.

                  • Importance

                    • Common

                      • Often when someone points out our close-mindedness, they do so in a way that is or seems accusatory, causing us to feel defensive.

                    • Impactful

                      • When defensive about it, it's often very hard to properly reflect on the possibility that we could be close-minded on the topic when others point it out, despite how important it is.

                  • Countermeasures

                    • Taking a break before considering it.
                    • Analyzing your own discussions

              • Related

                • Unsystematic reasoning

                  • Unsystematic reasoning makes it much harder to realize when we're wrong

                • Lacking self-awareness

                • Heavy heuristic usage

            • Strategies for open-mindedness

              • Acknowledging mistakes

                • Intellectual humility

              • Other-shoe review

            • Measuring open-mindedness

              • Importance

                • Impactful

                  • Knowing yourself

                  • Creating reasonable people
                    ~~Advanced

                    • It's very difficult to improve open mindedness, so finding open minded people to start with is very useful for creating very reasonable people.

                • Difficult

                  • It's typically very difficult to accurately determine how open-minded people (including yourself) are.

              • How to view

                • Something that typically shouldn't be attempted on your own.

            • Definition

              • Confidence in your reasoning abilities, judgement, etc.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Generally you can avoid most of the detriments of low criticalness by having a relatively low epistemic confidence.

              • Definition (overconfidence for short)

                • (A propensity towards) overconfidence in one's own reasoning abilities.
                  • Overconfidence: an irrationally high credence.
                • "Overconfidence" is in this section used as a shorthand for this specific kind of overconfidence.

              • Importance

                • Common: A massive portion of people are overconfident in this way about many topics.
                  • Often even when they appear on the surface to have a lot of epistemic humility, they have just formed surface-level agreeable habits.

                • Irrationality: Overconfidence makes one far less rational.

                  • Overconfidence usually makes one far more close-minded.

                    • Overconfidence usually greatly reduces the effort that one would put into considering how they could be wrong.

          • The knife's edge of humility
            Link/combine with emotional/epistemic self-confidence.

            • Definition

              • The issues that epistemic humility can cause.

            • Doubting yourself

              • Doubting yourself epistemically is crucial for good reasoning.
              • Doubting yourself emotionally often leads to insecurity, anxiety, self-hate, and lots of other unhealthy things.

            • Believing in yourself

              • Epistemically believing in yourself tends to lead to frequently assuming you're right and failing to consistently give the possibility that you're wrong adequate consideration.
              • Emotionally believing in yourself is extremely useful for pushing past hard obstacles and bouncing back from bad feelings.

            • The correlation between epistemic and emotional habits

              • Long-term, the habits we form for how to emotionally view things tends to heavily skew how we epistemically view things in a similar way.

                • Examples

                  • If one is emotionally optimistic then they'll likely be epistemically optimistic.

          • Related

            • Measuring epistemic humility

            • Goals of reasoning

        • Cognitive biases

          • Definition

            • Systematic pattern of deviation from rational judgement.

            • Distinction

              • When calling people biased, we are often using "biased" in a different way:

              • Unfair

                • One does not have to be "unfair" in any traditional sense to have some kind of systematic pattern of deviation from rational judgement.
                • ^ Eg. We have evolved to simplify our sensory data in a way that is more efficient to us given our cognitive limitations, resulting in us being consistently trickable with optical illusions.

              • Preferenced

                • One can have an emotional preference for something (that is specific to themselves) without any irrational judgement involved.

              • Particularly biased relative to what we're accustomed to.

                • One can be just as biased (or even far less) than what we are used to, while still having some deviation from rational judgement.
                • ^ Optical illusions

          • Importance / Limitations

            • Common

              • Cognitive biases are incredibly common.
                Support

            • Impactful

              • Cause big reasoning obstacles: Cognitive biases of various sorts are involved in all the biggest obstacles to good reasoning.
              • Stacking effects: The effects of multiple cognitive biases can stack on each other, creating exponentially more irrational judgements.
              • Can't substitute systematic reasoning: Learning to avoid specific cognitive biases can't be a complete substitute for systematic reasoning
                • If it's treated as a complete substitute, there is a significant risk of substantial Uncriticalness due to still relying on heavy heuristic usage

            • Improvable

              • Bias variety indicates solution variety: People vary in how strongly they exhibit different biases, so there should be a host of ways to influence how biased we are.
              • Learning helps avoid: Learning about specific cognitive biases helps develop methods for recognizing and combating many specific cognitive biases.
              • Bias blind spot: Due to the bias blind spot, studying biases can in fact make you more vulnerable to overconfidence and confirmation bias.
                • Because it can help you see the influence of cognitive biases all around you—without seeing the influence in yourself.
                  • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=0Twe0jxOkPS7EBmXHOxaQnBm
                • Richard F. West, Russell J. Meserve, and Keith E. Stanovich, “Cognitive Sophistication Does Not Attenuate the Bias Blind Spot,” Journal of Personality and Social Psychology 103, no. 3 (2012): 506.

          • Common cause

            • Cognitive biases arise when the corners cut with heuristics result in a relatively consistent and discrete mistake.

          • Bias blind spot

            • Definition

              • A special blind spot towards our own biases, which applies even when we can correctly identify the biases of others.

            • Importance

              • Common

                • In a survey of 76 people waiting in airports, individuals rated themselves much less susceptible to cognitive biases on average than a typical person in the airport. In particular, people think of themselves as unusually unbiased when the bias is socially undesirable or has difficult-to-notice consequences.
                  • Emily Pronin, Daniel Y. Lin, and Lee Ross, “The Bias Blind Spot: Perceptions of Bias in Self versus Others,” Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin 28, no. 3 (2002): 369–381.
                • Other studies find that people with personal ties to an issue see those ties as enhancing their insight and objectivity; but when they see other people exhibiting the same ties, they infer that those people are overly attached and biased.

              • Impactful

                • The bias blind spot, unlike many biases, is especially severe among people who are especially intelligent, thoughtful, and open-minded.
                  • Not to be confused with people who think they’re unusually intelligent, thoughtful, etc. because of the illusory superiority bias.
                  • Richard F. West, Russell J. Meserve, and Keith E. Stanovich, “Cognitive Sophistication Does Not Attenuate the Bias Blind Spot,” Journal of Personality and Social Psychology 103, no. 3 (2012): 506.
                • Studying biases can make you more overconfident and susceptible to confirmation bias.
                • Learning about biases can make us more overconfident
                  • Therefore learning how this works, how to recognize it, etc. is incredibly important.
                    Relevance

              • Difficult

                • Learning about biases can make us more overconfident

                  • Bias blind spot would ostensibly apply to itself, so we must be very careful with how we go about learning to avoid it.

            • Causes

              • We’re especially loath to think of our views as inaccurate compared to the views of others.
              • We fail to detect any “biased-feeling thoughts” when we introspect, and so draw the conclusion that we must just be more objective than everyone else.
                • Joyce Ehrlinger, Thomas Gilovich, and Lee Ross, “Peering Into the Bias Blind Spot: People’s Assessments of Bias in Themselves and Others,” Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin 31, no. 5 (2005): 680–692.
              • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=02ErcrGfQIBieY8kw6Fke-Nc

            • Source

              • https://www.readthesequences.com/Biases-An-Introduction

          • Base rate neglect

            • Definition

              • The fallacy where we ground our judgements in how intuitively "normal" a combinatino of attributes is, neglecting how common each attribute is in the population at large.

              • Example

                • Is it more likely that Steve is a shy librarian, or that he’s a shy salesperson? Most people answer this kind of question by thinking about whether “shy” matches their stereotypes of those professions. They fail to take into consideration how much more common salespeople are than librarians—seventy-five times as common, in the United States.
              • Wikipedia

            • Importance

              • Improvable

                • There are effective countermeasures

            • Reducible

              • Representativeness heuristic

            • Countermeasures

              • Thinking of probabilities as frequencies of objects or events.
                • https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HZGCoVF3YvM shows a visual technique that can help with this.

          • Confirmation bias

            • Definition

              • Giving more weight to evidence for confirming what we already believe (in itself).

              • Distinction

                • The "in itself" is very important here. without that, we might be tempted to object: "We ought to give more weight to evidence that confirms what we believe, as it fits in better with our model of reality in that case, doesn't it?"
                • ^ Confirmation bias occurs when it is [the mere fact that it confirms are beliefs] that makes us give it more weight, and not any indirect factors such as the abductive weight provided by [it fitting into our model of reality well].

              • Example and source

            • Importance

              • Common

                • Wason selection task
                  • A very small number of people (~4-10%) are able to complete the task successfully, when it is presented ordinarily.
                    • "ordinarily": (i.e. Without prior knowledge about its difficulty, and with abstract elements.)
                  • We tend to be able to perform well at the task when it is presented in a social context.
                    • Whether this is the result of instinctual or learned processes, either way it seems obviously dependent on heuristics which are not ordinarily active in more abstract variations.

              • Impactful

                • It easily tricks people into denying important logical consequences of new information.

              • Improvable

                • We can effectively practice systematic methods of reasoning that account for and largely avoid most forms of biased thinking.
                  Needs support.
                • We can learn much more consistent means of accounting for [past reasoning that may have been forgotten].

              • Difficult

                • Merely knowing about a bias is rarely enough to protect you from it.

                  • In a study of bias blindness, experimental subjects predicted that if they learned a painting was the work of a famous artist, they’d have a harder time neutrally assessing the quality of the painting. And, indeed, subjects who were told a painting’s author and were asked to evaluate its quality exhibited the very bias they had predicted, relative to a control group. When asked afterward, however, the very same subjects claimed that their assessments of the paintings had been objective and unaffected by the bias—in all groups!
                  • ^ Katherine Hansen et al., “People Claim Objectivity After Knowingly Using Biased Strategies,” Personality and Social Psychology Bulletin 40, no. 6 (2014): 691–699.
                  • ^ Similarly, Pronin writes of gender bias blindness.
                    • In one study, participants considered a male and a female candidate for a police-chief job and then assessed whether being “streetwise” or “formally educated” was more important for the job. The result was that participants favored whichever background they were told the male candidate possessed (e.g., if told he was “streetwise,” they viewed that as more important). Participants were completely blind to this gender bias; indeed, the more objective they believed they had been, the more bias they actually showed.
                    • ^ Eric Luis Uhlmann and Geoffrey L. Cohen, “‘I think it, therefore it’s true’: Effects of Self-perceived Objectivity on Hiring Discrimination,” Organizational Behavior and Human Decision Processes 104, no. 2 (2007): 207–223.

                  • Source for this section

                    • https://www.readthesequences.com/Biases-An-Introduction#footnote3

            • Reducible

            • Indicators

              • Definition

                • Indications that you may be under the influence of cognitive bias(es).
              • Someone is claiming that you are being close-minded or failing to take into account an obvious fact.
              • Rejecting a proposition that you likely do not fully understand.

            • Suggested countermeasures

              • Systematic reasoning

                • Learning (and practicing) to reason systematically (at least for important decisions).
                • This minimizes the room for all biases (confirmation bias included) to creep in.

              • Reduce over-reliance on intuition
                Only visit this now if you're heavily focusing on learning countermeasures.

              • Recognition

                • Practice recognizing when confirmation bias is likely occurring.
                • We are typically very bad (and overconfident) at recognizing when a bias is affecting us.
                  • So this method should likely not be relied on, at least on its own.
                  • Bias blind spot

              • Considering alternatives

                • Practice considering alternative possibilities that feel wrong in situations where you lack very solid conscious reasoning to justify your feelings.

              • Disproving

                • Create the habit of (for non-trivial problems) considering whether it would be easier to solve the problem via trying to disprove what you're ultimately trying to prove.

                • Software debugging

                  • To think of your ideas and beliefs as software you're actively trying to find problems with, rather than things to be defended.
                  • If they should be defended given the information you'll have, you should naturally realize this without needing emotionally assume that they are true.
                  • It's easy to get into habits that make you feel like you're doing this, without actually properly doing it.
                    • So there should be some feedback mechanism to practice this, if you intend to use this method.

              • ["Blind" techniques](https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=CLmXQEO4-lPT5O56n2jbZsSC
                Only visit this now if you're heavily focusing on learning countermeasures.

              • Related

            • Potential evolutionary explanations

              • It tended to be more efficient for multiple people with competing perspectives to focus on reasoning for their own perspective rather than for multiple perspectives, and then to use that reasoning to competitively try to influence the overall tribe. In the absence of biases specific to the topic in question, the crowd would tend to overall come to an agreement on the better justified position this way, with the "researchers" often only needing to do enough work to represent the best reasons they could give for their own perspective.
              • The past importance of making rapid decisions in uncertain situations.
              • Confirmation bias implicitly accounts for (in some sense) past reasoning that may have been forgotten, since we ostensibly arrived at our initial belief through reasoning that we may or may not remember.
              • We evolved to act reasonable more than be reasonable.
                Create/link

            • Myside bias
              Synonym of confirmation bias? I recall it being different

              • Definition

                • A bias toward searching and interpreting evidence in a manner that tends to favor the hypothesis we want to be true.

          • Groupthink
            PC ~Beginner, link in framing/etc., deal with duplicate in "environment" or sociology category.

            • Definition

              • The bias to let the social dynamics of a group situation override the most reasonable choice.

              • Synonym

                • Informational social influence

              • Example and source

            • Importance

              • It's common

                • It's often used in advertising.
              • It can lead us to deny important logical consequences of the available information in favor of the socially accepted answer.

                • This is a cognitive bias that plagues many people

                  • Dissent can be uncomfortable and dangerous to one's social standing, and so often the most confident or first voice will determine group decisions.

              • We can learn much more consistent means of accounting for the reasoning that others have performed, such as gaining an in-depth understanding of why they hold different opinions.

            • Reducible

              • Partially reducible to anchoring/framing effects.
                • Social norms are a heavy anchor/frame.
                  • Overton Window

            • Indicators

              • Your belief initially differs from the relevant social group and the subject of the beliefs is heavily reliant on "soft" reasoning.
                Fix link
              • The reasoning that other(s) provide(s) seems flawed.

            • In-group favoritism
              PC Link stuff from importance in in-group vs out-group or something.

              • Definition

                • The propensity for people, under certain conditions, to prefer and have affinity for one's in-group over the out-group, or anyone viewed as outside the in-group.

              • Importance

                • Common and impactful

                  • How we perceive the actions of others is frequemtly and substantially affected by in-group favoritism.
                    • People may perceive the same action very differently depending on whether the action was executed by a member of the same group or a member of a different group.
                    • People tend to evaluate actions of their own group or team members much more favorably than those of outgroup members.
                    • This phenomenon was demonstrated in an empirical study conducted by Molenberghs and colleagues in 2013.
                      • In the study, participants were arbitrarily divided into two teams where they watched videos of individuals of competing teams and individuals from their own team perform hand actions. Participants were then asked to judge the speed of the hand movements.
                      • On average participants judged members of their own teams to be faster, although the hand movements were the exact same speed across the board.
                    • Similarly, Hastorf and Cantril conducted a pioneering study in 1954, where students of both Princeton and Dartmouth viewed a contentious football game between their two teams.
                      • Although they had watched the same motion picture of the game, their versions of what transpired were so starkly different it appeared as though they had watched two totally different games.

                • Common

                  • This can be expressed in one's evaluation of others, linking, allocation of resources, and many other ways.

                • Improvable

                  • Elements of favoritism are flexible in that they can be erased by changes in social categorization.
                    • This change is known as recategorization.
                      Probably make a separate section for this and link or something.

              • Potential evolutionary explanation

                • In-group favoritism was likely selected for the advantages of coalition affiliation.

              • Related

                • Information bubbles

            • Suggested countermeasures
              In roughly descending order of effectiveness.

              • Learning (and practicing) to reason in a systematic way (at least for important decisions)

                • This minimizes the room for all biases (confirmation bias included) to creep in.

              • Reduce over-reliance on vague intuition

              • Learn about (and practice recognizing) cognitive biases and other common flaws in reasoning to be able to better recognize when it seems that others are using flawed reasoning to arrive at their conclusions.

                • Other flaws (fallacies?)

              • Facilitate group critical thinking

              • Getting better at measuring how much "soft" reasoning is involved in a topic.

              • Practice recognizing when groupthink is likely occurring.

                • Limitations

                  • This likely won't be very effective because it's difficult to practice this in a reliable way.

              • Common links

            • Potential evolutionary explanations

              • The importance of social cohesion for our ancestors.
              • The tendency for flaws in reasoning to sort of "even out" for most hypotheses, making the majority's opinion more reliable usually.

            • Related

              • Overton Window

              • Isolating yourself from unreasonable people
                • Reasonable people are much less likely to apply many of the kinds of social pressures that encourage groupthink.
                  Relevance
                  • Therefore complete isolation would likely not be necessary to be able to greatly reduce groupthink via isolating from unreasonble people.
                    Relevance

          • Anchoring/framing effects

            • Definition

              • The bias to consider values in the context of other presented values.

              • Example

                • Today only, you can get a new Toyota for 20% off the sticker price! So act now and drive your new car away today!

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Especially impactful when it biases us to consider an overly-limited range of potential values.

              • Common

                • Incredibly commonly abused by marketers and salespeople.

              • Improvable

                • We can learn to consider unpresented values to compare something with.
                  • Many of the most common and important unpresented values are fundamentally pretty easy to account for.
                    • Example: prior probability

            • Failure to (sufficiently) account for unknowns

              • Definition

                • Unknowns: Unknown factors relevant to the claim(s) you're considering.

              • Importance

              • Causes

                • Reasoning short-cuts

                  • Typically we simply do not have the time to account for all the biggest potential unknowns. We instead heavily rely on unreliable reasoning short-cuts and engage in heavy heuristic usage, which tends not to be the best way to consistently account for the biggest potential unknowns.

                • Unsystematic reasoning

                  • We typically lack the time to investigate arguments in a systematic fashion, which would typically involve (or at least help with) accounting for all the biggest potential unknowns.

                  • Often when considering other peoples' claims (particularly those from those we are close with), we will be less critical and more agreeable.

              • Solutions

                • Many specific strategies to account for unknowns are in accounting for unknowns

                • Systematic reasoning

              • Related

                • Accounting for unknowns

            • Less-is-better effect

              • Definition

                • The cognitive bias to favor a lesser option when it is presented separately, but to prefer the better option when both are presented together.

                • Examples

                  • A person giving a $45 scarf (from scarves ranging from $5-$50) as a gift was perceived to be more generous than one giving a $55 coat (from coats ranging from $50-$500);
                  • An overfilled ice cream serving with 7 oz of ice cream was valued more than an underfilled serving with 8 oz of ice cream;
                  • A dinnerware set with 24 intact pieces was judged more favourably than one with 31 intact pieces (including the same 24) plus a few broken ones.
                • Wikipedia

            • The availability heuristic

              • Definition

                • The bias to assume that the likelihood of something is more heavily correlated with how readily it comes to mind (its “salience”) than it actually is, or with more confidence than is justified.

              • Bias towards how well we can initially imagine something

                • Causes

                  • When we can better initially imagine something, it typically feels more real, which makes it feel more likely to be true.

                • Related

                  • Bias towards the specific/"grounded"

              • Bias towards the stated

                • Related

                  • False dilemma

              • Causes

                • Often an over-reliance on our system 1 thinking to provide good and relevant evidence, when it merely provides what we've formed the strongest relevant associations about.
                  PC copy/move to subconscious and link here

              • Related

                • Heuristics

            • Related

              • Assuming that others feel the same as us

                • Likely often caused by an anchoring/framing effect.
              • False dilemma

          • Wishful thinking

            • Definition

              • Bias towards emotionally appealing people/ideas/etc. and bias against emotionally unappealing people/ideas/etc.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

              • Importance of "defending sub/un-conscious"

            • Forms

              • Defending sub/un-conscious

                • Definition

                  • Bias towards believing (and especially defending) what we unconsciously or subconsciously did.

                • Importance

                  • Common

                    • Virtually everyone does it.
                    • ^

                  • Impactful

                • How to abuse this

                  • Make one feel like the belief you want them to have is their own idea (i.e. embed it in their un/sub-conscious). Once they feel this way, they will be much more likely to adopt/defend it themselves, possibly even if it goes against their previous belief(s).

              • Projecting desires
                This seems very similar to the parent.

                • Definition

                  • The bias to project our own desires onto reality and fool ourselves into thinking that reality conforms to how we would like it to be.

            • Potential evolutionary explanation

              • The conscious as our "PR team"

                • Humanity was (and still is) an irrational species, in a way that gradient decent promoted.
                  • A more rational species would require very specific moral/axiological axioms in order to consistently act in advantageous ways, and due to the way that our brains evolved, there's no easy way to evolve such axioms, only emotional instincts towards simplistic patterns roughly representing those axioms.
                • In order to motivate humans towards certain ideal actions (eg. mating more capable people), they developed metrics for judging features like capability.
                • However since humans were so irrational, their judgement was highly susceptible to trickery.
                • So natural selection supported the evolution of a mechanism that convinces people to view us in a better light than they otherwise would.
                • Our bias to defend our actions, even when they were our un/sub-conscious actions and there's no justifiable defense, is plausibly a result of this.

            • Related

          • Bias towards the specific/"grounded"
            ~~Amateur

            • Definition

              • We feel like things presented in a more specific/"grounded" way are more likely to be accurate.

              • i.e.

                • We are biased against vague/"ungrounded" things.

              • Examples

                • One presents us with a realistic hypothetical and all the necessary reasoning to support it, but we still treat it like it is "just a hypothetical/conceptual thing" and consider it substantially less likely to be truthful.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Theoretical reasoning importance

              • Common

            • Reducible

              • Heavy heuristic usage

                • We often develop the heuristic that more vague/abstract evidence is less reliable.
                • ^ Partially because they are more prone to being interpreted in a biased manner.
                  • And this is generally accurate, vague/abstract things allow for more possibilities, thus allowing us to fill those gaps with our biases.
                  • However there are major exceptions to this heuristic, such that relying on it to give more than a minor indication of how to treat vague/abstract evidence is sure to lead to major mistakes.

              • Bias towards how well we can initially imagine something

                • When we have an explicit example (especially when it is tied to an emotion that compels us to accept the fact), we can typically imagine it more clearly.
                  Relevance
                • Our imagination often has an easier time with more explicit/detailed descriptions.

              • Over-reliance on System 1 thinking

                • The bias towards the specific/"grounded" would not be very influential, except that we tend to trust our system 1 thinking (especially our initial intuition) far too much.
                  Relevance

          • Attribution errors
            Fix terms of sections under this and refine

            • Definitions
              Fix

              • 1. The underemphasis of situational and environmental factors and overemphasis on dispositional or personality factors for the behavior of other(s).
              • 2. The overemphasis of dispositional or personality factors for the behavior of one's self.

              • i.e.

                • An error where one tends to overattribute the behaviors of others to their personality (e.g., he is late because he's selfish) and underattribute them to the situation or context (e.g., he is late because he got stuck in traffic).

            • Importance

              • Impactful

              • Common?

              • Self-environment overemphasis

          • Mere exposure effect
            ~~Adept

            • Definition

              • The phenomenon whereby individuals develop a preference for things merely due to repeated exposure.

            • Resource

              • https://www.ebsco.com/research-starters/psychology/mere-exposure-effect

          • Distinction bias
            ~~Intermediate

            • Definition

              • The tendency to view two options as more distinctive when evaluating them simultaneously than when evaluating them separately.

              • Examples

              • Wikipedia

            • Importance

              • Common

            • Countermeasures

              • Independent evaluation

                • Definition

                  • An evaluation which avoids comparing two options directly and instead considers each option individually and makes an overall assessment of each one on its own, and then compares the assessments.

                • Limitation

                  • Can easily involve us accidentally imagining differences that don't actually exist.
                    • For example by thinking of a new way to utilize a common factor present in both options only while considering the second option. We may fail to account for how this factor would also improve the experience of the first option, if we do not keep the first option in mind.

              • Systematic reasoning

                • By being systematic, we can largely avoid relying on heuristics from which distinction bias emerges.

          • Gambler's fallacy
            ~Intermediate

            • Definition

              • The belief that, if an event (whose occurrences are independent and identically distributed) has occurred less frequently than expected, it is more likely to happen again in the future (or vice versa).
              • Synonyms
                • Monte Carlo fallacy
                • Fallacy of the maturity of chances

              • Distinctions

                • Events whose probabilities are explicitly not independent.
                  • The gambler's fallacy does not apply when the probability of different events is not independent.
                  • In such cases, the probability of future events can change based on the outcome of past events, such as the statistical permutation of events.

                  • Example

                    • When cards are drawn from a deck without replacement. If an ace is drawn from a deck and not reinserted, the next card drawn is less likely to be an ace and more likely to be of another rank. The probability of drawing another ace, assuming that it was the first card drawn and that there are no jokers, has decreased from ⁠4/52 (7.69%) to 3/51⁠ (5.88%), while the probability for each other rank has increased from 4/52 (7.69%) to 4/51⁠ (7.84%).
                • Events whose probabilities are not independent due to hidden factors.
                  • For example, if a coin is flipped 21 times, the probability of 21 heads with a fair coin is 1 in 2,097,152. Since this probability is so small, if it happens, it may well be that the coin is somehow biased towards landing on heads, or that it is being controlled by hidden magnets, or similar. In this case, the smart bet is "heads" because Bayesian inference from the empirical evidence — 21 heads in a row — suggests that the coin is likely to be biased toward heads. Bayesian inference can be used to show that when the long-run proportion of different outcomes is unknown but exchangeable (meaning that the random process from which the outcomes are generated may be biased but is equally likely to be biased in any direction) and that previous observations demonstrate the likely direction of the bias, the outcome which has occurred the most in the observed data is the most likely to occur again.
                • Events whose probabilities change
                  • If external factors are allowed to change the probability of the events, the gambler's fallacy may not hold. For example, a change in the game rules might favour one player over the other, improving their win percentage. Similarly, an inexperienced player's success may decrease after opposing teams learn about and play against their weaknesses. This is another example of bias.

              • Example

                • The belief that the next dice roll is more likely to be six than is usually the case because there have recently been fewer than the expected number of sixes.
              • Wikipedia
                Pull more from this starting with origins

            • Importance

              • Common

                • It is a major contributing factor to the amount of people who gamble.

            • Limitation

              • In most illustrations of the gambler's fallacy and the reverse gambler's fallacy, the trial (e.g. flipping a coin) is assumed to be fair. In practice, this assumption may not hold.

            • Reverse position

              • After a consistent tendency towards tails, a gambler may also decide that tails has become a more likely outcome. This is a rational and Bayesian conclusion, bearing in mind the possibility that the coin may not be fair; it is not a fallacy. Believing the odds to favor tails, the gambler sees no reason to change to heads. However, it is a fallacy that a sequence of trials carries a memory of past results which tend to favor or disfavor future outcomes.

              • Inverse gambler's fallacy

                • Examples

                  • A situation where a gambler entering a room and seeing a person rolling a double six on a pair of dice may erroneously conclude that the person must have been rolling the dice for quite a while, as they would be unlikely to get a double six on their first attempt.
                  • A game of roulette at the Monte Carlo Casino on August 18, 1913, when the ball fell in black 26 times in a row. This was an extremely unlikely occurrence: for any given sequence of 26 spins, the probability of either red or black occurring 26 times in a row on a single zero roulette wheel is around 1 in 68.4 million, assuming the mechanism is unbiased. Gamblers lost millions of francs betting against black, reasoning incorrectly that the streak was causing an imbalance in the randomness of the wheel, and that it had to be followed by a long streak of red.

            • Retrospective gambler's fallacy

              • Definition

                • A similar bias for inferences about unknown past events based upon known subsequent events.

                • Examples

                  • To observe multiple successive "heads" on a coin toss and conclude from this that the previously unknown flip was "tails".

            • Causes

              • Hasty generalization

              • Representativeness heuristic

          • Low-ball technique
            ~Advanced

            • Importance

              • It can allow people to manipulate you or people you are about.

              • You can learn to be harder to manipulate this way.

              • It can be used to manipulate other people for your benefit.

          • Foot-in-the-door technique
            ~Expert

            • Definition

              • A two-stage compliance technique in which the influencer sets the stage for the real request by first getting the person to comply with a much smaller request.

              • Example and source

            • Importance

              • It can allow people to manipulate you.

                • This is a technique that works on a lot of people.

              • You can learn to be harder to manipulate this way.

                • Learning patterns to watch out for and calculating important decisions consciously.
                  Adept

              • It can be used to manipulate other people for your benefit.

            • Indicators

              • A small request is quickly followed up by a much larger one.

            • Suggested countermeasures
              In roughly descending order of importance.

              • Learning (and practicing) to reason in a systematic way (at least for important decisions)

                • This minimizes the room for all biases (confirmation bias included) to creep in.

              • Practice recognizing when this technique is likely being used.

            • Potential evolutionary explanation
