            Move to "understanding others" probably.

            • Definition

            • Importance

            • Related

              • Lacking context

          • Understand before dismiss

            • Definition

              • Thoroughly understand an idea (usually important) before dismissing it.

            • Importance

              • Without following this, it is virtually guaranteed that out of all the important ideas you will investigate, there will be some that are prematurely rejected.

          • Avoiding missing exceptions

            • Importance

              • We often fail to account for certain broad possibilities and as a result spend all our calculation in areas that don't matter.

                • Examples of failing to account for all possibilities

                  • Classical theism vs atheism

                    • Definitions

                      • Atheism

                      • Deism

                      • Theism

                        • Classical theism

                        • Non-classical theism

                    • A common false dichotomy is between classical theism and atheism. But there are other possibilities:

                    • Deism

                    • Non-classical theism

            • Deductively categorizing possibilities

              • Definition

                • Starting with all possibilities and creating categories for mutually exclusive groups of possibilities to check if you've missed anything.

              • Importance

                • It's a good way to make sure that we've accounted for all possibilities.

            • Involving person(s) with different plausible opinion(s)

              • Definitions

                • Getting other person(s) with different plausible opinion(s) to look at the topic and see if they can spot any important things that you would miss.
                  • Opinion

                • Synonym

                  • Getting fresh eyes on the topic.

              • Importance

                • Impactful

                  • Particularly useful when you're too exhausted/bored but you want the topic to be considered more.

                • Common

              • Finding people with different plausible opinion(s)

                • Contacting fitting people you know

                • Finding people with high criticalness

          • Other-shoe reviews

            • Definition

              • Checking how your statement looks from the perspective of the primary side(s) your statement contests.
              • Distinction

              • Synonyms

                • Placing yourself in the other person's shoes.
                  • This can have other connotations like emotionally empathizing with others, which can also be useful but isn't necessarily meant by "other-shoe reviews".

            • Importance

              • This is an incredibly useful technique for catching things you missed and properly accounting for obvious counterarguments before they are given.

            • Always assume you're wrong

              • Definition (it's hyperbole)

                • The idea is to temporarily think almost entirely as though you know you are wrong but don't know how.

              • Importance

                • Our brain evolved to argue for one perspective and not adequately account for the other side. This tricks our brain into focusing on treating one side to that luxury, then the other.

                  • <broken link>

                  • It encourages you to try to look for all the ways in which you could be wrong and give all the promising counterarguments you can find adequate attention.

              • Applied to cooperative reasoning

                • If you disagree
                  • Almost always assume that the other(s) are right at first.
                    • Because you should (temporarily) assume that you're wrong about them being wrong.
                • If you agree
                  • Almost always assume that the other(s) are wrong at first.
                    • Because you should (temporarily) assume that you're wrong about them being right.

              • Back and forth

                • It's good to switch from acting as though one side is right and then acting like another side is right and so on.
                  • But when you do this, you should be careful to account for all possibilities:

                  • Avoid missing exceptions

          • Preemptive steelman counter check

            • Definition

              • The process of steelmanning your opponent and checking if they've already (implicitly or explicitly) accounted for your counterargument(s), concern(s) or similar.
              • Connotation
                • Typically the order of events is:
                • 1. You see a statement/argument.
                • 2. You think about it.
                • 3. You write counterargument(s).
                • 4. You carefully Steelman the statement/argument and re-read it, checking for implicit or explicit counter(s) to your counterargument(s).
              • Distinction
                • Other-shoe reviews are about looking for counterarguments that were neither implicitly nor explicitly given. Whereas PSCCs are about looking for counterarguments that were implicitly or explicitly given. However "implicitly" is defined pretty vaguely, so it's fair to regard these topics as having some overlap.

          • Multiple checks

            • Definition

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Consequences of not finding flaws

                  • Lacking evidence ≠ false

              • Common

                • A lot of people perform multiple checks in a flawed way.

                  • A lot of people use an unreliable but consistent method, so the multiple checks give the same wrong answer multiple times.
                  • A lot of people overestimate the thoroughness of correctly performed multiple checks.
                    • It is often decently effective, but usually far from definitive.

            • Limitations

              • Potential systematic issue

                • If the potential uncaught error emerged from a systematic error (eg. the relevant intuition(s) failing to account for a very important variable), then it is likely that the systematic error will be repeated if the same person(s) analyze(s) the same thing again.
                • This can be somewhat mitigated by using (at least slightly) different techniques/people for the checks.
                  • For example doing an ordinary check, coming up with counterargument(s), then doing a preemptive steelman counter check

            • Cooperative multiple checks

              • Definition

              • Importance

              • Ordinary peer-review

                • Importance

              • Conceptual replication

                • Definition

                  • A research strategy in which the same underlying hypothesis or theoretical concept is tested using different methods, operationalizations, samples, or experimental conditions compared to the original study.
                  • Connotation
                    • Has professional and academic connotations, but does not necessarily need to be professional or academic.

                • Importance

            • Related

              • Preemptive steelman counter check

              • Other-shoe reviews

          • Lacking evidence ≠ false

            • Definition

              • Lacking evidence in favor of a proposition is not necessarily substantial evidence of that proposition being false.

              • Synonym

                • Absence of evidence is not evidence of absence.

            • Importance

              • Lacking evidence against a hypothesis is often not great evidence for that hypothesis.
                • Even if you've done multiple checks, depending on the subject it could have a feature that makes it very easy for there to be many checks that would simultaneously fail.
              • This is often particularly relevant when applied to topics where people frequently reason badly.
                • The arguments for/against things that you are aware of are likely not the only possible ones.
                • As such, if people are not good at finding good arguments, you addressing all the bad ones likely doesn't tell you much about the reasonable position to take.

          • Unexpected obstacles

            • Causes

              • With a sufficiently complex task, it is impossible to account for all the challenges that will be involved.

            • Hofstadter's law

              • Definition

                • "It always takes longer than you expect, even when you take into account Hofstadter's law."
                • A law usually used to describe the widely experienced difficulty of accurately estimating the time it will take to complete tasks of substantial complexity.
                • https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hofstadter's_law
              • We have a tendency to focus only on the things that we can explicitly account for.
                Link to bias(es)

            • Plan for failure

              • Definition

                • Planning for things to go wrong in unexpected ways.
                  • Unexpected: Not specifically predicted.

                    • Example

                      • You might expect that trying to build your own submarine would go poorly, but it might be difficult to predict exactly which part(s) of your design would fail.
                        • After all, it's your design, so you would ostensibly prevent any sufficiently specific failures that you could successfully predict.

              • Importance

                • Depending on the importance, it may be worth coming up with contingencies for if things go wrong in unexpected ways.

              • Systematically planning contingencies

                • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=e-rx5GVRcMwUlauim9BgIzme

                • Assigning time usage in reasoning

                • Find semi-plausible failures

                  • Experiment method: Try to break the components in all the semi-realistic ways you can think to.

                • Make contingencies

                  • Multiple contingencies

                    • Importance

                      • Oftentimes you'll want multiple contingencies if it's very important not to fail.

                    • Independence

                      • Importance

                        • It's very important to keep the contingencies very independent, so that a common failure of one thing doesn't cause a failure of multiple contingencies.

                      • Design independence

                        • Different designs so that if one design is faulty (or faultily implemented, etc.) then that's ok.

                      • Source independence

                        • Different people, manufacturers, companies, etc.

                      • Implementation independence?

                      • System independence

                        • Contingencies that are meant to be independent should not be reliant on a common system.

              • Commonly useful contingencies

                • Backup time

                • Backup money

              • Commonly useful areas to plan for failure

                • Reasoning

                  • Importance

                  • Contingencies

          • Learning from others

            • Definition (unusual)

              • Learning about things other than how to reason better, from others.
                Here.

            • Importance

              • There are some very important details in most areas that you're not realistically going to discover on your own.
                • Even if you research the topic somewhat on your own, oftentimes there's not much info about it on the Internet, or a lot of misleading info.
              • As such, learning from others is really important to get a good understanding of many important subjects.

            • Limitations

              • Human biases

                • Humans (at least currently) have <broken link> that can cause many seemingly quite independent humans to perform the same flawed reasoning.
                  • This is especially likely to occur when using "soft" reasoning

            • Find good counterarguments

              • Importance

                • To verify that you're not missing something, you should argue against the best arguments (often given by the strongest advocates).
                  • Arguing against weaker advocates usually proves little, because even the strongest idea will attract weak advocates.
                  • Arguing against weaker arguments usually proves little, because even the strongest idea will have weak arguments used for it.

              • Find people who disagree

                • Definition (specific)

                  • The practice of finding someone who disagrees and learning why they disagree.

                • Importance

                  • In order to learn about promising counterarguments/details that you didn't consider before discussing it with them.
                    • This does not require that the people you discuss the subject with are very reasonable.
                      • They could have learned promising knowledge about the subject from other people.
                        • They could be biased towards the other position and accept any reasoning they find, and happen to have found pretty good reasoning.
                      • They could have stumbled across a decent argument by chance.
                      • Confirmation bias tends to cause people to calculate in favor of their own position quite a lot, so if there weren't any subtle counterarguments along the way, they could have a deep line that's good for justifying their position even if they were heavily subject to confirmation bias, close-mindedness, etc.

                        • <broken link>

                • Limitations

                  • Oftentimes, the people regarded as the best to consult on the topic will not (at least outside of a paper or book) immediately use many of the best counterarguments or mention many of the important nuances relevant to the topic.
                  • Oftentimes the best people to talk to for a given subject will not be available.

                • Finding good disagreers

                  • Measuring criticalness

                  • Knowledge of the subject

                  • Measuring open-mindednese

                    • Open mindedness generally does not matter as much for finding good disagreers.

                • Learning from counterarguments

                  • It's important to focus on understanding and adequately representing their position. The point is not to argue with them or prove them wrong, the point is to learn from them.
                    Link goal and strawmanning here

                  • Directly

                    • Definition

                      • Learning from them directly, like being in a chat or call with them, or talking with them in-person.
                    • You might also want to ask them to focus on giving you the best arguments that they're aware of, if you suspect that they might otherwise give you worse ones that don't matter as much.

              • Search for counterarguments themselves

                • Definition

                  • Search for counterarguments using methods other than looking through the people who make them.

                • Importance

                  • Limitations of finding people who disagree

            • Facilitate group critical thinking

              • Definition(s?)

              • Importance

              • Questioning others

              • Avoiding openly contesting others

                • Avoid openly contradicting others in situations where the social consequences of openly contesting the common opinion would likely end unideally.

              • Handling people contesting each other

              • Other ways to facilitate objective means of evaluation and critical thinking practices as a group activity.

          • Surrounding yourself with reasonable people

            • Definition (specific)

              • Surrounding yourself with people who have the best effect on your reasoning possible.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Reasoning feedback

                  • Surrounding yourself with reasonable people is likely to increase the average amount of good feedback on how you reason that you get, which would likely help you learn how to reason better.
                    Relevance

                • You reason like those around you

                  • So surrounding yourself with more reasonable people will generally make you more reasonable.
                    Relevance

                • Learning

                  • Being surrounded by reasonable people likely means having easy access to getting their help with reasoning about important things.
                    Relevance
                  • ^ This can help you learn the truth about various important topics.
                    Relevance
                  • ^ ^ Virtually any disagreement with sufficiently reasonable people (who hold the same relevant axioms) can be resolved.
                    Likelihood | Probably link in eg. importance of reasoning?
                    • Zipf minimizes required things to consider to resolve a disagreement
                      Likelihood
                    • If people agree on the relevant axioms, then they can theoretically exchange all the substantially relevant information required to have all the same knowledge, thus precluding them from realistically disagreeing after that point.
                      Likelihood
                    • All disagreements about a particular belief must be entirely caused through disagreements about other beliefs.
                      Likelihood

                      • Distinction

                        • This isn't to say that things like biases don't play a role, just that the way in which they play a role must work through other beliefs. Eg. A bias might make someone hold absurd epistemic beliefs for a time, or manifest as an absurd epistemic belief.
                      • All beliefs can be expressed in terms of reasoning pyramids, which is to say that all beliefs are entirely supported by other beliefs, or unsupported by anything (non-circular axioms).
                      • Axioms are beliefs.
                      • Supposing people hold the same axioms, differences in beliefs can only arise due to making mistakes or having different knowledge.
                        • All mistakes would be resolvable if the one making the mistake had the knowledge that it is a mistake, by getting the relevant knowledge required to come to that conclusion.
                    • Likely resulting in one or both sides changing their mind in a way that more likely corresponds to reality.
                      Relevance
                  • ^ ^ Many disagreements with sufficiently reasonable people (who don't hold the same relevant axioms) can still be discussed in a manner that results in substantial learning.
                    Likelihood
                    • Oftentimes differences in relevant axioms is only part of the reason for the disagreement.
                      Likelihood

                • Scrutiny-driven reasonableness

                  • When one is (or believes they will be) under scrutiny from people they perceive to be well-informed and interested in accuracy, they tend to be more reasonable.
                    Relevance
                    • Perceived well-informedness is correlated with perceived reasonableness.
                      Relevance
                    • Perceived interest in accuracy is correlated with perceived reasonableness.
                      Relevance
                    • Perceived likelihood of being under scrutiny is correlated with perceived reasonableness.
                      Relevance

                    • Related

                      • Common requirement: audience scrutiny attributes

              • Difficult

                • Unintuitive

                  • We tend to [believe / feel like] our reasoning is ultimately our resonponsibility, and that we are independent of effects like thsoe that we are surrounded with.
                    • Particularly because there are many cases where we feel we are forced to be surrounded by much less reasonable people, and yet feel they do not have a substantial negative effect on our reasoning abilities.
                      • This feeling is unjustified.
                      • ^ We are almost always very bad at measuring our own reasoning abilities.
                        Likelihood
                        • Difficulty of measuring reasonableness
                          Likelihood

            • How to do

              • Do not trust your system 1 thinking for deciding who to surround yourself with.
                • Very unintuitive

            • Related

          • Isolating yourself from unreasonable people

            • Definition (relatively)

              • Isolating yourself from relatively (compared to how reasonable you want to become) unreasonable people.
              • Isolation from them both in real life and online. Not only standard interactions but also things like eg. watching videos / reading books hosting/made by unreasonable people.

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Impactful

                • Can heavily reduce the influence of unreasonable people on one's reasoning.
                  • Would likely greatly reduce groupthink.
                    Likelihood
                  • Would likely reduce our picking up bad reasoning habits from unreasonable people.
                    Likelihood
                • Likely to lead to (near-)complete social isolation.
                  • There likely aren't many people at all who are relatively reasonable.
                    Likelihood | Link to section about lack of reasonable people
                    • Relatively reasonable compared to how reasonable you want to become.
                  • (Near-)complete social isolation is likely bad for reasoning.
                    Relevance
                    • Likely to reduce one's self-awareness.
                      • Less opportunity to get feedback from others, to better know of one's own flaws.
                        Likelihood
                      • Self-awareness is very important for reasoning
                        Relevance
                      • Self-awareness is already intrinsically difficult to get right
                        Relevance
                    • If one surrounds themselves with reasonable people then it wouldn't be detrimental to reasoning.
                      • It's very difficult to properly measure reasoning
                        Relevance
                        • Therefore it's likely that in attempting to surround oneself with reasonable people, they would surround themselves with relatively unreasonable people.
                          Relevance
                    • Information bubbles may tend to be detrimental to reasoning.
                • Can be detrimental to one's social abilities.

                  • Charisma is very important

                  • If one already has a decent amount of social experience, this should just manifest at becoming temporarily rusty. It shouldn't be too difficult to get back into "social shape" if/when the need arises, so long as there is some warning ahead of time.

              • Difficult

                • To do practically anything, we generally must at least frequently briefly interact with many unreasonable people.
                  • This plausibly isn't too much interaction to reap many of the benefits from otherwise isolating from unreasonable people.
                    Relevance
                • Most people become attached to friends/family/a partner/social interaction in general, such that separating would be a very painful process.

            • The implications of quick conclusions

              • Very high cognitive speed

                • Definition

                  • The idea that when someone comes to a quick conclusion, it is due to very quickly consciously calculating many things.
                • This typically isn't true. Usually the person just had previous thoughts to work with.

              • Previous thoughts

              • Presumptuous reasoning

            • Societal counterfactual thinking

              • Definition

                • The idea that on a large scale, we work out the truth via a kind of societal counterfactual thinking, whereby groups/individuals with different beliefs debate until the society is won over by the victors.

              • Importance / Limitation

                • Impactful

                  • This kind of counterfactual thinking is quite limited by the general open mindedness of the society.
                    • Sufficient open-mindedness in a given area is often required to adequately judge the victor of such debates, due to our https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=W7HyxLRMyYcuGQTUpyV2gLDq

        • Systematic reasoning
          Amateur

          • Definitions

            • Thorough, precise reasoning, similar to formal reasoning.

          • Importance

            • Impactful

              • Systematic reasoning (done right) can be very reliable.
              • Unsystematic reasoning is typically very unreliable.

                • Heavy heuristic usage is always substantially involved in unsystematic reasoning.

                  • And it's almost unavoidably quite unreliable.

                • Unsystematic reasoning typically involves a lot of vague definitions.

                  • Vague definitions are problematic because they cause:

                  • Conflations

                  • Definitional differences

                • Unsystematic reasoning typically involves ignoring the structure of arguments.

                  • Unstructured reasoning typically leads to lots of/major blunders and mistakes.
                • Even slight unreliability in a large reasoning pyramid can often compound.
                  • Propogation of uncertainty
              • Unsystematic reasoning makes it much harder to realize when we're wrong.

            • Common

              • Our reasoning is almost always very unsystematic.
                • Our reasoning is almost always Informal
                  Likelihood
                  • Informal reasoning is almost always very unsystematic
                    Relevance

            • Improvable

              • Similar to chess, systematic reasoning is made up of patterns that can be memorized.
                • These patterns can be memorized in such a way that we can perform many subconscious calculations with them very quickly.
                  Relevance

          • Precision

            • Definition

              • Distinction

                • Different from specificity.
                  • It is possible for something to be broad but precisely defined/understood.
                  • It is possible for something to be specific but not precisely defined/understood.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Reasoning systematically well requires a significant amount of precision.

            • Related

              • Heavy heuristic usage

          • Thoroughness

            • Importance

              • If you are not thorough, you can easily fail to address impactful parts of important contrary positions.

                • "Address" (distinction)

                  • Here defined to mean addressing all the reasoning down to (or very near to) axioms common between the relevant party(s).
                  • "Relevant party(s)"
                    • Usually including you and one other person you're mainly discussing the matter with.
                    • Can also include the audience.
                      • Eg. In a debate and trying to convince the audience, you may want to account for the axioms that the audience are likely to be using, therefore they may be a relevant party as well.

                  • Distinction (important)

                    • Distinct from addressing every argument other(s) give.
                      • If you've already addressed their reasoning down to common axioms and they fail to

                • "Important"

                  • Important to your goals specifically.
                  • ^ "Your goals" as in the goals that you hold, not necessarily selfish/self-interested goals.
                    Maybe create a definition for this, since this is 2nd copy of this?
                • This tends to greatly reduce your ability to critical piggyback and learn many important things.
                  Relevance
                  • It can easily lead you to underestimate counterarguments against your position, because of not fully understanding them due to being insufficiently thorough.
                    Likelihood
                  • If you fail to address impactful parts of important contrary positions, then you typically fail to fully understand those positions.
                    Relevance
                    • Usually, if you don't understand important positions, you cannot accurately determine how probable they are.
                      Relevance
                      • Therefore you cannot change your mind to accept them.
                        Relevance
                      • ^ Therefore you're often left with inaccurate beliefs about important topics.
                        Relevance
                    • Because if you do understand them, it's typically easy to address them.
                      Likelihood
                      • And when it's easy to address them, we typically would address them.
                        Relevance
                      • ^ instinctually, for satisfaction's sake.
                        Likelihood
                • There are many exceptions to this.
                  Likelihood | Create a section in "thoroughness" about when not to be thorough, likely linking this to those sections' importance sections.
                  • When the reasoning presented is confirmed to consistently ignore relevant axioms, it's unlikely to be accurate.
                    Likelihood & Relevance
                    • Relevant axioms: Axioms common between you and the relevant party(s).
                    • Therefore it would typically be justified to skip addressing the specific arguments/people that consistently ignore the relevant axioms.
                      Relevance
                    • This typically provides very little evidence against [the position the reasoning is advocating for].
                      Relevance
                      • People can (and frequently do) provide horrible reasoning for true claims.
                        Likelihood | Likely link to how bad people are about "soft" reasoning in "frequently do".
                        • Reversed stupidity is not intelligence
                          Likelihood
                      • So this does not justify skipping addressing any reasoning about the position in question.
                        Relevance

            • Per-claim

                • Answer each part one at a time.*

        • Reasoning mediums

          • Definition

            • The non-hardware medium used to read/write the ideas.
              Make this definition more specific.

          • Importance

            • Impactful

              • Avoiding repetition

                • Because the same reasoning often repeats in multiple places, with multiple overlapping, incomplete sets of information at varying levels of specificity, accuracy and truth, most of the effort in debate is wasted in repetition.

                • How some reasoning mediums can help avoid repetition
                  There should be more added to this.

                  • 1. Canonicality
                  • 2. If you pick a structured reasoning medium (eg. argument mapping), you can store past points and link it to people who make arguments you've already addressed.

              • Memory: Typically harder to know what exactly was previously stated.

                • Typically vocal reasoning can't consistently be reused in future discussions.

            • Reply chain
              ~~Amateur

              • Definition

                • Chain of messages replying to other messages.

              • Importance

              • New reply

                • Definition

                  • Adding a new reply to a reply chain.

                • Reviewing context

                  • Definition

                    • Look at far back in the reply chain as seems necessary to understand the context thoroughly.

                  • Importance

                    • This tends to be important to do before adding to a reply chain.
                      • Otherwise, it is quite easy to forget (and then fail to account for) important details in previous replies.

                • Checking for other arguments

                  • Definition

                    • Checking that the other person(s) haven't addressed [what you would add to the chain] in other messages/reply chains.

                  • Importance

                    • This tends to be important to do before adding to a reply chain.
                      • Otherwise, you might miss that they already addressed your counterargument.
                        • This might mean wasting time writing your reply.
                        • This might mean not at all realizing that they already addressed your argument until they point it out.
                          • If this repeatedly happens, it wastes a lot of time by forcing them to have to point it out many times for the discussion to constructively progress in those areas.
                            • This would likely result in the discussion being dropped before it is sufficiently explored for the mistaken person(s) to change their mind(s).
                          • This is typically embarrassing.

            • Check for later context

              • Definition (specific)

                • When it's important, respond after reading through it all to make sure that there isn't important context to what's said earlier that you're missing later on in the text.

              • Importance / Limitations

                • Prevents missing how later context addresses your counters to earlier text.

                  • Important to avoid embarrassing yourself.
                  • Important to be quicker.
                  • One can learn to be decently consistent at recognizing when later context addresses their counters.
                    • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=KVIlDwqG0oJsf9U7kwCk8GwM

                • Avoids wasting time

                  • If you have to wait until later to find out that what you wrote was addressed, you'll likely end up writing counter(s) that are doomed to be useless.

            • Related

              • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=lj9jejrk2s5bCXgZLKLHwWkZ

                  • Argument mapping encourages us to reason more consciously and thoroughly.

                      Likelihood

                    • This wastes time, which is impactful

            • General considerations

              • Canonicality

                • Definition
                  Probably pull from CDL

                • Importance

                  • Impactful

                    • In typical debates and debate societies/platforms, past work is not often systematically saved and reused in future works, leading to lots of pointless repetition. In reasoning mapping tools with canonicality, a lot of work can be reused that wouldn't normally be reusable.
                    • ^ This can (depending on the size of the userbase) greatly increase the amount of knowledge/human computation available.
                      Relevance

              • Clear organization

                • Definition

                  • The intuitive categorization of reasoning, especially propositions and logic (eg. arguments).
                  • In this context: Reasoning mapping tools tend to allow for much more clear information organization than typical methods.

                • Importance

                  • This allows us to more frequently identify parts that we can likely skip without issue.
                    Relevance
                    • This saves time, which is substantially important
                      Relevance
                  • This allows us to more often quickly find reasoning that we've seen before.
                    Relevance
                    • Our brain has an easier time remembering what branches things fall under, than remembering where exactly things are in a linear text thread.
                      Likelihood
                    • This saves time, which is substantially important
                      Relevance

              • Scores

                • Definition

                • Importance

                  • The importance of multi-user scores
                  • The importance of roll-up scores

                • Roll-up scores

                  • Definition
                    Probably pull for CDL

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                      • In typical debates, unreliable intuitions must be relied on to calculate and combine confidences, giving our cognitive biases a huge opportunity to influence our thinking. But in reasoning mapping tools, there are sometimes roll-up systems that can be used to combine scores in a reliable, mathematical way.

                • Multi-user scores

                  • Importance

                    • Prioritization: Multi-user scores can substantially improve prioritization of where to reason.
                      • Multi-user scores make it much easier to know where people disagree, which makes it easier to know where to prioritize reasoning.
                        Likelihood
                      • This makes it easier to avoid providing reasoning in cases where it isn't needed.
                        Relevance
                        • This allows you to skip writing this reasoning out.
                          • This saves time, which is important
                            Relevance
                        • This usually allows other(s) to skip reading that reasoning.
                          • This saves time, which is important
                            Relevance
                        • This usually prevents other(s) from adding to that reasoning.
                          • This saves time, which is important
                            Relevance

              • Saving time

                • Importance

                  • That time can be spent expanding the reasoning.
                  • More time as more users read the reasoning and as the reasoning grows larger.

              • Future potential

                • Importance

                  • If a tool will turn out to be incredibly useful for you in the future, it may be best to start learning it now even if it isn't in the ideal state currently.

          • Inferences

            • Definition

              • A conclusion reached by way of evidence and reasoning.

              • Examples

                • If you notice someone making a disgusted face after they've taken a bite of their lunch, you can probably infer that they do not like it.
                • If a friend walks by with a graded test in her hand and a smile on her face, you can probably infer that she got a good grade on the test.
              • Wikipedia

            • Deductive inference
              Consider moving to definitions

              • Definition

                • A deductive inference is an inference that is valid from its premises, meaning that it is impossible for the premises to be true and the conclusion to be false.

          • Circularity

            • Definition

              • Circular reasoning.
              • Reasoning in which the conclusion is present in one of the (stated or implied) premises.

              • Synonyms

                • Begging the question
                • Vicious circle fallacy

              • Distinction

                • Distinct from non-loop circles.

                  • Definition

                    • Circles of reasoning that don't form a loop, so there's no justification chain that supports itself.

                  • Example

                    • A -> B -> D
                    • A -> C -> D
                    • When you graph this out, it forms a kind of circle of connections, but the connections don't run in a "loop" such that the justification chain supports itself.
                • Distinct from relation/category loops (partial or otherwise).
                  • Total relation/category loops
                  • Partial relation/category loops

                    • Example

                      • A supports B, B is in the A category.
                      • This isn't necessarily problematic because non-B things in the A category can support B.
                        • i.e. One member of a set can support another member of that same set.

            • Importance

              • Common

              • Impactful

            • Recognizing circularity

            • Vicious vs non-vicious circularity

              • Vicious circulatory

              • Non-vicious circulatory

            • Non-axiomatic circularity

              • Definition

                • Examples

              • Inductive counterarguments

                • Observed unreliability of circular reasoning

                  • It seems like circular reasoning does no better than chance at deriving truths.
                    • "Truths" defined broadly to include avoided falsehoods.

                • Intuition pull

                  • Energy counterargument to circular reasoning

                    • Justification functions sort of like energy. You can't use energy to increase itself without drawing from other energy.

              • Lawless circulatory

                • If circular reasoning can be used to deduce a proposition and its negation, then a contradiction is entailed.
                • True logical contradictions are impossible, so circular reasoning must be limited in such a manner that it cannot be used for any pair of proposition and its negation.
                  • Supposing that circular reasoning with only this detail is accurate requires a more specific hypothesis.

                    • Occam's razor

            • Axiomatic circularity

              • Definition

              • Importance

                • Many people (at least claim to) use circularity at the foundation of their epistemology.

              • Intuition pull

                • There's no inherent inconsistency in presupposing the validity of certain limited forms of circular reasoning.
                • It's common for people to unknowingly use axioms to justify circular reasoning to justify axioms.

            • Arguments for/against circulatory

              • Premise-circularity is vicious but rule-circularity is not

                • It is not necessary to know or even justifiably believe that rule R is reliable in order to move to a justified conclusion using the rule.
                  • As long as R is in fact reliable, one can form a justified belief in the conclusion of an argument relying on R, as long as one has justified belief in the premises.
                    • If one does not have justified belief in the rule of inference, then it is not possible to have justified belief in a conclusion that requires that rule of inference.
                  • This doesn't work without its support, which doesn't work due to the support's counter.
                • Rule-circularity is sufficient to demonstrate the reliability very fundamental rules.
                  • It is unreasonable to require an uncircular demonstration of a very fundamental rule's reliability.
                    • There is nowhere more basic to turn for such fundamental rules, so all that we can reasonably demand of a fundamental form of reasoning is that it endorse itself.
                      • Even deductive inference cannot be justified deductively.
                      • There is somewhere more basic we can turn.
                      • We do not a priori owe fundamental forms of reasoning a realistic chance to defend themselves.
                • This is odd (and thus analogically unlikely), because there appears to be an easy interchange between rules and premises.
                  • A rule can always be added as a premise to the argument.
                    • There is a fundamental difference between being prepared to accept a premise stating a rule and being prepared to use that rule.
                • A circular rule argument does not provide a full justification of the rule, so it is pretty much useless.
                  • A circular rule argument does not provide a full justification of the rule.
                    • Less sane inference rules such as counterinduction can support themselves in a similar fashion. The counterinductive rule is CI:Most observed As are Bs.Therefore, it is not the case that most As are Bs.Consider then the following argument CI*:Most CI arguments have been unsuccessfulTherefore, it is not the case that most CI arguments are unsuccessful, i.e., many CI arguments are successful.This argument therefore establishes the reliability of CI in a rule-circular fashion.
                  • [...] so it is pretty much useless.
                    • It's useful for showing people who accept inductive inference that it is reliable.
                      • There is some added value in showing that inductive inferences are reliable, even when we already accept that there is nothing problematic about them.
                        • If "there is nothing problematic about them" is defined to preclude inconsistency, then this is equivalent to saying "there is some value in showing x even when we already know x", and it's false.
                      • The inductive justification of induction provides a kind of important consistency check on our existing beliefs.
                        • It doesn't provide this consistency check because of ovserved unreliablility of circular reasoning
                        • It doesn't provide this consistency check because of energy counterargument to circular reasoning

              • Circular reasoning using a sufficiently large circle can provide useful information

              • It is generally unclear (given the limited reliability of circular reasoning) what forms of circular reasoning should be considered ok and what forms should not.
                • eg. Why is circular reasoning not allowed for establishing the existence of unicorns?
                  • Premise 1. Unicorns exist (assumption)
                  • Premise 2. If unicorns exist then unicorns exist (law of identity)
                  • Premise 3. Unicorns exist (Modus ponens 1, 2)

            Maybe combine with reasoning pyramids?

              • Other beliefs are the branches and leaves.

                • Creating/maintaining dependency maps between beliefs.

                • Impactful

                  • Very helpful for more systematically reasoning.

            • Pyramid of practical reasoning

              • Definition

                • Quicker but less reliable reasoning methods built on shower and more reliable methods.

              • Importance

                • It allows us to know to (and to some degree how to) default to more reliable methods when the less reliable ones come into sufficient question.

          • Reasoning pyramids

            • Definition

              • An epistemic structure where a proposition is only directly justified via a combination of other propositions, which are in turn likely the same, and likely so on.
                • Technically virtually everything is only justified via a combination of other propositions if we allow for indirect justification.
                  • Virtually all proposition justifications at least involve the law of identity and some other proposition.
              • From now on in this section, "pyramid" will be used as a short-hand for this.

            • Importance

              • Common

                • We use reasoning pyramids to justify a vast amount of our beliefs.

            • The instability of large reasoning pyramids

              • Importance

                • We frequently use large reasoning pyramids for important reasoning

                  • Examples

              • The difficulty of mapping pyramids

                • Definition

                  • Mapping
                    • Properly learning what justifies what for a sufficiently large portion of the pyramid.

                • Importance

                • The lack of already-present maps

                  • We lack many easily-accessible well-structured maps for even much of the most common reasoning we use.

                • Blind to large portions of pyramids

                  • Definition

                    • Not consciously knowing about large portions of pyramids.

                  • Causes

                    • Unstructured reasoning

                • We typically have no idea how to

                • Related

                  • Argument mapping

              • The difficulty of stabilizing known large pyramids

                • Definition

                  • The difficulty of determining when all known crucial things are justified in a large reasoning pyramid.

                • Propagation of uncertainty

                  • Definition

                    • In statistics, propagation of uncertainty is the effect of variables' uncertainties on the uncertainty of a function based on them.

                  • Examples

                    • 62 crucial justifying propositions that, if true, are all 98% likely to justify their parent.
                      • Only ~28.6% likely for the main proposition to be justified, despite all its supporting propositions having such a high likelihood.

                • Error cascade

                  • Definition

                    • A single important error upon which many things are built causing a major collapse.

                  • Importance

                    • Common

                    • Impactful

                  • Examples

                • Examples

                  • NASA failure because of metric vs imperial confusion.
                    • In retrospect this was a very obvious thing to check for and avoid. But when dealing with the massive pyramid of reasoning involved in making sure everything in the rocket will work, there's so many things that could go wrong that it's very hard to thoroughly check everything.
                • Technicality
                  • This is technically slightly misleading since there are situations where there are multiple options for how to justify something and only one is required.
                    • None are individually crucial but having at least one is crucial.

            • Related

              • Fitting things together

              • Consistency in reasoning

          • Gödel's incompleteness theorems

            • Definition

          • Reductio ad absurdum

            • Definition

              • The form of argument that attempts to establish a claim by showing that following the logic of a proposition or argument would lead to absurdity or contradiction.

              • Synonyms

                • Argumentum ad absurdum
                • Apagogical argument
              • Wikipedia

            • Importance

              • Frequently an effective form of persuasion

            • Limitations

              • It's very easy to strawman others using this technique.
                • In order to make a reductio ad absurdum, we often have to perform a few steps of reasoning from the other person's perspective.
                • And if our understanding of the person's perspective is wrong such that any of those steps don't work, we would likely be creating a strawman argument.
                  • "Likely" because we could be doing something like using multiple lines of reasoning to justify our ad absurdum, so that if one line doesn't work, the other could still work.
                • Taking care to avoid strawmanning others is important.
                  • People frequently strawman eachother and strawmans often cause major and quite impactful misunderstandings when people misunderstand each other.

            • Types

              • Proof by contradiction

                • The deductive form.

              • Inductive ad absurdum

                • Definition

                  • An argument that following the logic of a proposition or argument leads to an overly unlikely outcome, and thus that the proposition/argument is more likely to be flawed than it other would be.

                  • Distinction

                    • Emotional ad absurdum

                • Limitation

                  • Often largely based on an emotional ad absurdum

              • Emotional ad absurdum

                • Definition

                  • An argument intended to appeal to our subconscious/intuition about how "absurd" something is.

                  • Distinction

                    • Different from inductive ad absurdum

                • Importance

                  • Learning to recognize and avoid this is very important.

                • Limitations

                  • The unreliability of emotions.
                    Link

          • Likelihood and relevance
            Move or link to induction

            • Distinguishing likelihood and relevance
              Link(s) to likelihood/relevance, link to semantics somewhere probably

              • Importance

                • Impactful

                  • Failing to distinguish between likelihood and relevance can easily lead to substantial misunderstandings.

                • Improvable

                  • It's common for people to assume that likelihood quantifiers lose almost all weight when combined with a relevance quantifier of sufficiently high or low strength.
                    • The norm is for relevance quantifiers to supercede likelihood quantifiers

              • The norm is for relevance quantifiers to supercede likelihood quantifiers

              • Related

                • Quantification

            • Likelihood is often considered more important than value, likely due to our inability to intuit very large or very small things, and the regular upper and lower bounds typically subconsciously placed on likelihood and value.
              Link the cognitive bias about very large/small values.

        • Common errors

          • Heavy heuristic usage
            Move the "largely based on stupid patterns" kinda stuff into the places dedicated to saying why they're good/bad, rather than being in the definitions, to make it feel less controversial and more understandable. Also perhaps change the name to "topic-specific heuristics" or create a section for that as well.

            • Definitions and examples

              • Reasoning that's based on a large collection of heuristics specific to various topics in which the reasoning is applied.
                Write this (and child) in a better way.
                • Thinking of a subject (or collection of subjects) with intuitive pattern-reaction thinking based on patterns specific to the topic (or collection of topics) for the most part, instead of mostly applying a general reasoning system to all subjects.

              • Heavy heuristic usage

                • People who use different collections of patterns for different kinds of reasoning.
                • They don't super generally [apply concepts or learn things] in [abstract and broadly-applicable] ways.

              • "Act-based reasoning" here refers to reasoning that's largely based on applying fundamental rules, habits and processes to all subjects.

              • Examples

                • Social rules
                  • Family
                  • Traditions
                  • Relationships

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Almost always heavily contributes to uncriticalness
                • The prevalence of Heavy heuristic usage is one of the biggest contributors to unsystematic reasoning, which is very important to avoid

              • Common

                • Failure to broadly apply good reasoning

                  • People are often very reasonable in some areas while being very unreasonable in others, in ways that can only be explained by the failure to apply the reasoning typically used in the first area for the second area.
                  • ^ In other words, a heavy reliance on topic-specific heuristics.

            • How to view it

              • Something to learn to avoid, since it's much easier to learn good act-based reasoning than to refine rule-based reasoning enough to be as good.

            • Forms of rule-based reasoning

              • Causes for different forms

                • Environment

                  • Definition

                    • We often learn patterns for rule-based reasoning based on what we feel to be best given our environments.

                  • Examples

                    • Conspiracy theorist

                      • In a conspiracy theorist environment, people are much more likely to form positive patterns about claims like the earth being flat, and reasoning common among claims such as that. And negative claims about experts, so-called unquestionable facts, etc.

                    • Good at critical thinking

                      • In an environment that involves good critical thinking, people are much more likely to have positive associations about good critical thinking practices and negative associations with things like fallacious reasoning.

              • Consequences of the diversity of forms

                • Rule-based reasoning doesn't necessarily preclude good reasoning

                  • Rule-based reasoning can in some cases come pretty close to typical act-based reasoning on how well the reasoning is performed for some (or occasionally many) particular topics.

                • Rule-based reasoning can make intelligent people very bad at reasoning

                  • 1. People can learn good patterns for some particular areas but not for others.
                  • 2. People can learn terrible patterns in general but be highly genetically gifted to be intelligent.

            • Convincing rule-based reasoners

              • Suggested method
                Define positive/negative patterns.

                • Definition

                  • "good"/"bad" patterns

                    • Patterns that a person has associations with that make them want to agree ("good") or disagree ("bad").
                    • This definition is only meant for this context.

                • Isn't this manipulation?

                  • Sort of, but:
                  • 1. If using a sufficiently broad definition of "manipulation", we must conclude that basically any interaction with anyone/anything is a form of manipulation on such people, because they're usually being irrationally influenced (if influenced at all) by virtually everything due to how flawed rule-based reasoning is. So it isn't as though leaving them alone would make a substantial difference to how much they get manipulated.
                  • 2. You can manipulate people in ways that benefit them. For example, it should theoretically be possible (albeit most likely quite difficult) to manipulate a rule-based reasoner to become an act-based reasoner.

                • 1. Learn the "good"/"bad" patterns

                • 2. Minimize "bad" patterns

                  • Hide the "bad" patterns until near the end of your reasoning

                    • If you're trying to convince them of a point, usually they've learned "bad" patterns around the common arguments against their position. So if you're going to present one of those arguments (or a form of them), it's usually best to build up most of the reasoning behind your argument in a way that isn't close enough for them to feel like it's the same counterargument they're familiar with. At the end you will usually be forced to use at least some of the "bad" patterns. But you can use the reasoning that they agreed with to minimize the distance you have to take them after revealing the "bad" patterns. Alongside this, you should of course maximize good patterns

                  • Words/phrases

                    • Words/phrases are a very common source of "good"/"bad" patterns.

                • 3. Maximize "good" patterns

                  • Characterization

                  • Words/phrases

            • Act-based reasoning

              • Definition

                • "Act-based reasoning" here refers to reasoning that's largely based on applying fundamental rules, habits and processes to all subjects.

                  • Distinction

                    • This still involves learning patterns and doesn't mean the absence of sbuconscious/intuition or the learning/application of irrational patterns for certain subjects.

              • Limitations

                • Act-based reasoning can still be irrational if the fundamental rules being applied to all subjects are flawed.
                  • The set of fundamental rules doesn't necessarily have to be the same as the set of axioms that the person accepts.

              • Indications of act-based reasoning
                ~Expert

                • Asks why a lot maybe? Debates when disagrees even as a child? Maybe caused by parents entertaining questions?

              • Systematic reasoning

          • Confirmatory thought

            • Definition

              • A reasoning process focused on justifying a pre-existing belief, where the mind selectively searches for, interprets, and remembers information that supports what it already thinks is true.
              • Distinction
                • In some cases it can appear quite similar to someone who isn't doing this kind of reasoning, but who's focused on representing their position with all the arguments they think of first, and who doesn't acknowledge when they agree or think of counterarguments against their arguments.

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Common

                • Confirmation bias is common.
              • Importance/limitations of starting with conclusion
              • Importance/limitations of assuming that you're right
              • Importance/limitations of assuming that others are wrong
              • Importance/limitations of reasoning to convince others

            • 1. Starting with conclusion

              • Definition

                • Typically

                  • In large part directed by system 1 thinking
                  • Involving a strong feeling that the conclusion is true.

              • Importance / Limitations

                • Common

                  • Confirmation bias contributes to how common it is.
                  • The way that system 1 thinking works contributes to how common it is.
                  • You will find plenty of mention of how common it is in the relevant literature.

                • Impactful

                  • High propensity for bad thinking

                    • Confirmation bias
                      Likelihood
                    • Heavy heuristic usage
                      Likelihood
                      • Because this often feels like stronger evidence than it is, and when under the influence of confirmation bias, one will accept this evidence without thinking further to consider the possible counters.
                    • Cherry-picking
                      Likelihood
                    • Often causes: assuming that you're right
                      Relevance
                  • Can sometimes be temporarily helpful
                    • Eg. When considering hypotheticals

                      • Eg. When Steelmanning

                • Assuming that you're right

                • Evolutionary social instincts to defend opinion

                • Definition

                • Causes

                  • Assuming that other(s) are wrong

                • Often partially caused by lacking context
