          • Fallacies
            Show how almost all of these are reducible to more fundamental mistakes.

            • Definition

              • Specific instances of flawed (fallacious) reasoning in arguments*.
                • The arguments can be either formal or informal.

            • Limitations

              • Most can be avoided using systematic reasoning

                • Most fallacies are implicitly avoided if you use systematic reasoning, which you should be doing anyway. So most are not very important to learn about specifically.

              • Learning isn't enough

                • Merely learning about the fallacies will not make you much better at accounting for them. For that, you have to form habits and practice processes that avoid falling into fallacious reasoning

            • How to view them

              • Mostly common mistakes that people make. It is much less common for people to intentionally employ fallacies, though it does of course happen.
              • Most of these fallacies are best understood in terms of other, more fundamental reasoning practices, mistakes, etc.
                • They implicitly include most of these fallacies with most of their nuances.
                • We mostly list all these fallacies so that you can easily find a good definition for them if someone mentions them.

            • Formal fallacies

              • Definition

                • Formal fallacies occur when there is a problem with the form, or structure, of the argument, such that the argument is invalid.
                • Etymplogy: The word "formal" refers to this link to the form of the argument.

              • Fallacies of presumption
                Maybe should be in informal fallacies?

                • Definition

                  • Fallacies that make presumptions, often in a hidden way. That is, they rely on hidden and unstated assumptions written, as it were, between the lines.

              • Fallacies of ambiguity

                • Definition

                  • Fallacies that involve one switching between meanings without acknowledging that they are doing so.

            • Informal fallacies

              • Definition

                • An informal fallacy is an error in reasoning that occurs due to a problem with the content, rather than the form or structure, of the argument.

              • Irrelevance

                • Definition

                  • Fallacies that employ irrelevant claims that typically feel relevant.

                  • Synonyms

                    • Fallacies of irrelevance
                    • Fallacies of relevance
                    • Relevance fallacies

                • General countermeasures

                  • Causes

                    • These fallacies depend on the use of information that may seem relevant to establishing the conclusion but isn’t really relevant after all.

                • The genetic fallacy

                  • Definition

                    • The fallacy in which arguments or information are dismissed or validated based solely on someone's or something's history, origin, or source rather than its current meaning or context.

                    • Example

                      • Wedding ring

                        • You're not going to wear a wedding ring, are you? Don't you know that the wedding ring originally symbolized ankle chains worn by women to prevent them from running away from their husbands? I would not have thought you would be a party to such a sexist practice.

                  • Nuances

                    • Common misconception

                      • We should focus on what was said rather than the who said it.

                        • Definition

                          • The idea that it's always more reasonable to figure out if something is true on its own merit rather than to assume one way or the other based on where the information comes from.

                        • Limitations

                          • Difficulty to investigate

                            • It's realistically impossible learn whether all important things are true on their own merit.
                            • Focusing on who said it is in many cases a good way to decide whether it's true or not.
                              • If it's a relevant expert who has a near-perfect track record about hundreds of similar claims, it seems likely that it will be true.

                        • How to view it

                          • An oversimplification that shouldn't be heavily relied on, but not a horrible rule of thumb.

                  • Reducible

                    • 1. We subconsciously associate things with their histories/origins to enough of an extent that during recollection, we have a very strong intuition that it is best to emphasize feature(s) of the associated history/origin in a way that is irrelevant to the current context.
                    • 2. We decide to follow and defend that intuition without sufficiently questioning it.

                    • Heavy heuristic usage

                    • <broken link>

                  • Appeal to authority

                    • Definition

                      • An appeal to an inappropriate or clearly inadequate authority.
                        • Inadequate after taking into account the other evidence presented.

                      • Example

                        • My friend who is a scientist insists that global warming is not cause for alarm, and for me that is a good enough reason to accept her conclusion.

                      • Synonym

                        • Appeal to inappropriate authority.

                    • Nuances

                      • Common misconceptions

                        • Any appeal to any authority would be an appeal to authority fallacy.

                        • We should focus on what was said rather than the who said it

                      • The issue that the "appeal to authority" fallacy is meant to demonstrate is the overconfidence people often have for particular authorities.

                  • Ad hominem

                  • Naturalistic fallacy

                • Ad hominem

                  • Definition

                    • An argumentative strategy that attacks the character, motive etc. of the person(s) associated with the argument, in a way that provides no sufficiently substantive evidence to be plausibly indicative of a relevant and important truth.

                    • Synonyms

                      • Personal attack
                      • Abusive fallacy

                  • Importance

                    • Common

                      • This fallacy is very common.

                    • Impactful

                  • Nuances

                    • An argument against one's character can be given for a constructive purpose outside of furthering the primary topic.

                      • Examples

                        • Constructive criticism given mid-discussion.
                    • An argument against one's character can be genuinely relevant and impactful to the discussion.
                      Maybe move this limitations into above?

                      • Explanation

                        • While we should not care intrinsically who gives an argument and should typically focus on the argument's validity and soundness, oftentimes who gave the argument will be extrinsically substantially relevant to the trustworthiness of the argument.

                      • Limitations

                        • Mid-discussion character criticism often causes people to get more defensive.
                        • Mid-discussion character criticism is often seen as a bad faith technique.

                      • Example

                        • If one person states that they witnessed God, and the other person argues that the supposed witness often takes drugs that cause hallucinations, this attacks the reliability of the witness (at least insofar as the particular claim they made goes) in a relevant and impactful way.

                    • We should focus on what was said rather than the who said it

                  • <broken link>

                • Red herring

                  • Definition

                    • The red herring is a seemingly plausible, though ultimately irrelevant, diversionary tactic.

                    • Examples

                    • Distinction

                      • This is distinct from red herrings as literary devices for eg. mystery novels.

                  • Limitations

                    • Easily misattributable to genuinely important and relevant deep dives into important and relevant details primarily involving other topics.

                  • Nuances

                    • A red herring may be intentional or unintentional; it is not necessarily a conscious intent to mislead.

                • Appeal to popularity

                  • Definition

                    • Synonym

                      • Bandwagon fallacy

                  • Nuances

                    • We should focus on what was said rather than the who said it

                • Appeal to consequences

                  • Definition

                    • Rejecting some particular viewpoint, theory or idea based on the consequences to which it leads, typically because of a negative emotional reaction to those consequences.

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                      • It is usually irrelevant whether or not we like or want to believe something, truth is indifferent to feelings.

                    • Common

                • Appeal to emotion

                  • Definition

                    • The fallacy that intends to manipulate the recipient's emotions in order to win an argument.
                      • Source: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Appeal_to_emotion
                    • Often: In the absence of factual evidence.

                    • Examples

                      • Charity advertisement

                        • A charity advertisement showing a single suffering child, eyes wide, belly descended. Statistics about millions in need remain abstract, but this one face breaks through our defenses. Our wallets open not because we've evaluated the charity's effectiveness or administrative overhead, but because we cannot bear the weight of those eyes. The emotion becomes the argument.

                      • Crime statistics

                        • Crime statistics might show historic lows, but one brutal incident (properly packaged) overrides decades of data. The politician who practices safety from the manufactured threat wins over the one citing improvement trends. Parents clutch their children tighter, vote for harsher measures, support policies they would reject in calmer moments. Fear doesn't require facts.

                      • Product commercials

                        • A lot of advertising is created and followed not because of a fallacy, but because of social expression. i.e. Socially expressing certain characteristics about one's self by buying products with advertisements associated with those characteristics.

                          • Source

                        • The car commercial doesn't list specifications or safety ratings, instead, it shows a father driving his daughter to her wedding, both of them crying.
                        • The watch advertisement ignores precision engineering, focusing on a grandfather passing his timepiece to his grandson. We buy the emotion, not the product.

                      • Climate change

                        • Climate change discussions devolve into pictures of polar bears on shrinking ice vs images of unemployed coal miners.

                      • Healthcare reform

                        • Healthcare reform gets buried under individual tragedies from both sides. The emotional ammunition flies, while substantive policy discussion dies in the crossfire.

                      • Source

                        • Taken virtually verbatim from the linked video.
                        • Seems AI genned, but I think the examples still largely work.

                    • Distinction

                      • It is only fallacious when the emotions that are elicited are irrelevant to evaluating the truth of the conclusion and serve to distract from rational consideration of relevant premises or information.
                      • Merely including emotions in some premises in an argument is not sufficient to count as a form of appealing to emotions.
                        • Eg. If I feel frustrated, then I will likely yell. I feel frustrated, therefore I will likely yell.
                          This is not a fallacious argument, unless perhaps it is implied that the listener(s) ought to do something as a result of this argument.

                  • Types

                    • Appeal to fear

                    • Appeal to flattery

                    • Appeal to pity

                    • Appeal to ridicule

                    • Appeal to spite

                  • Related

                    • Appeal to consequences

                      • Often has overlap with this.

                    • Wishful thinking

                      • A common bias preyed on by this fallacy.

                • Misc

                  • Unimportance

                  • Naturalistic fallacy

                    • Definition

                      • Assuming that something is better because it is natural, without anywhere near sufficient justification for the assumption that natural things are more likely to be better.

                    • Nuance

                      • Some people hold beliefs that (at least in their opinion) justify the conclusion that natural things are typically better. This is not an example of the naturalistic fallacy since it is not assuming that natural things are better without any reason.

                  • Appeal to force

                    • Definition

                  • Weak analogy

                    • Definition

                      • An insufficient analogy.

                      • Example

                        • Galileo and freemasons

                          • Galileo was ridiculed because of his views, and these views later proved to be correct.
                          • I too am ridiculed for believing that the Pope is a reptilian alien in league with the Freemasons.
                          • Thus I too will have my day and my views will be accepted.

                          • The issue

                            • The main issue is the "thus", since that implies that the preceding statements should substantially support the ending.

                    • Limitations

                      • It's easy to mistakenly attribute the fallacy to sufficiently strong analogies.

                    • Analogical reasoning

              • Ambiguity

                • Definition

                  • Fallacies involving switching between meanings without acknowledging that one is doing so.

                • Strawman

                  • Definition

                    • A statement that misrepresents (intentionally or unintentionally) other statement(s).

                    • Memorable

                      • Prop up a weak argument that's more easy to knock down, like propping up a person made of straw.

                  • Importance

                    • Common

                      • People frequently strawman each other

                    • Impactful

                      • Strawmans often cause major and quite impactful misunderstandings when people misunderstand each other.

                  • Related

                    • Steelmanning

                • Equivocation

                  • Definition

                    • Equivocation is using multiple meanings of a word as if they were the same.
                      • Specifically multiple non-synonymous meanings.

                    • Example

                      • Prisoners are free

                        • Prisoners are really free. This is because if you can think whatever you want, then you are free and prisoners can certainly think whatever they want.

                  • Reversed stupidity is not intelligence

                    • Definition

                      • "Reversed stupidity" here means the "opposite" of "stupidity".
                        • "Opposite" being defined vaguely in one of several ways

                    • Very broken ≠ capable at "opposite"

                      • Definitions

                        • That something is very bad at doing one thing doesn't mean it's very good at doing the "opposite".
                        • "Opposite" being defined vaguely in one of several ways.
                          • It might mean the inverse / converse of the belief(s) that were stupidly derived.
                          • It might mean the logical negation of the belief(s) that were stupidly derived.

                        • Example

                          • A car with a broken engine cannot drive backward at 200 mph, even if the engine is really really broken.

                      • Related

                        • Superintelligent stupidity

                    • Superintelligent stupidity

                      • If you knew someone who was wrong 99.99% of the time on yes-or-no questions, you could obtain 99.99% accuracy just by reversing their answers. They would need to do all the work of obtaining good evidence entangled with reality, and processing that evidence coherently, just to anticorrelate that reliably. They would have to be superintelligent to be that stupid.

                      • Related

                        • Very broken ≠ capable at "opposite"

                    • Example

                      • “The world’s greatest fool may say the Sun is shining, but that doesn’t make it dark out.”

                  • Associating with believers/disbelievers

                    • Negative association

                      • Definition

                        • Examples

                          • Some people may be reluctant to believe that God doesn't exist, not because there is evidence that God does exist, but rather because they are reluctant to affiliate with Richard Dawkins or those darned “strident” atheists who go around publicly saying “God does not exist.”

                    • Positive association

                • Cherry-picking
                  Link in induction

                  • Definition

                    • Selectively reading, noting or citing the evidence in favor of your own hypothesis, or “cherry picking” the data to get the parts that support your hypothesis.
                    • Typically: often ignoring things that contradict it.

                  • Related

                    • Confirmatory thought

                      • Essentially cherry-picking what you think about, rather than what you read/note/cite.

                • Texas sharp-shooter
                  Link in induction

                  • Definition

                • Composition

                  • Definition

                    • The inference that something is true of the whole merely because it is true of some part of the whole.

                    • Examples

                      • Tire, vehicle, rubber

                        • This tire is made of rubber; therefore, the vehicle of which it is a part is also made of rubber.
                          • This is fallacious, because vehicles are made with a variety of parts, most of which are not made of rubber.

                      • Atoms, sets of atoms, life

                        • No atoms are alive. Therefore, nothing made of atoms is alive.
                          • This is a statement most people would consider incorrect, due to emergence, where the whole possesses properties not present in any of the parts.

                    • The converse of division

                  • Nuance

                    • The fallacy of composition can apply even when a fact is true of every proper part of a greater entity.

                      • Example

                        • Atoms, sets of atoms, life

                • Division

              • Presumption

                • Mere assertion

                  • Definition

                    • An assertion made without any evidence.

                    • Example

                      • Abortion is just wrong, and that is all there is to it.

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                      • Mere assertions are often very confidently asserted, which can make them feel like much stronger evidence than they really are.

                  • Reducible

                    • Partially reducible to faulty heavy heuristic reasoning
                    • Partially reducible to a lack of systematic reasoning
                      • If systematic reasoning was employed, it would likely be quite clear that the statement is unsupported.
                    • Partially reducible to a lack of other-shoe reviews

                  • Slippery slope

                    • Definition

                • Appeal to ignorance

                  • Definition

                    • A direct appeal to the lack of evidence in favor of something, with the intent of definitively proving that the something is false.

                  • Nuance

                    • It's very easy to mistake this with the appeal to the lack of evidence without the intent to definitively disprove it, particularly when they are implicitly relying on occam's razor or something.

                  • reducible

                    • Partially reducible to faulty probabilistic reasoning

                • False dilemma

                  • Definition

                    • A presented dilemma between an incomplete set of options, without the remaining members being sufficiently implied.

                    • Synonyms

                      • False dichotomy/trichotomy/etc.
                      • Black or white fallacy

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                      • We are typically biased to fall for false dilemmas.

                        • <broken link>

                  • Reducible

                    • Partially reducible to the failure of sufficiently accounting for unknowns

                  • <broken link>

                • Hasty generalization

                  • Definition

                    • Generalizations made with pretty obviously too little justification.

                    • Synonym

                      • Law of small numbers

                    • Example

                      • All three of my ex-wives always told me what to do. This clearly shows that all women want to control us men all the time.
                    • Wikipedia
                      Pull a bunch from this

                  • Reducible

                    • Partially reducible to faulty probabilistic reasoning

                • False cause

                  • Definition

                    • Assuming that because two events are correlated and/or related and/or one precedes the other by a relevant amount of time, that means one causes the other.

                    • Example

                      • My alarm goes off every morning before sunrise, therefore my alarm causes the sun to rise.

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                    • Common

                  • Reducible

                    • Partially reducible to faulty heavy heuristic reasoning

                  • Correlation ≠ causation

                • Secundum quid

                  • Definition

                    • The fallacy of failing to recognize the difference between rules that are generally true (but still leave room for exceptions) and rules that are always true.
                    • Synonyms
                      • Since it ignores the limits/qualifications of general rules, this fallacy is also named:
                      • Ignoring qualifications
                      • Sweeping generalizations
                      • The expression misuse of a principle can be used as well.

                    • Examples

                      • Accident fallacy example
                      • Converse accident fallacy example

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                    • Common

                      • Despite being lesser known than many other fallacies, this is still a very common fallacy.

                      • Examples

                  • Reducible

                    • Substantially reducible to faulty heavy heuristic reasoning

                  • Accident fallacy

                    • Definition

                      • The fallacy of applying a general rule to an exceptional case (one in which some special circumstance (“accident”) makes the rule inapplicable).

                      • Synonyms

                        • Destroying the exception
                        • A dicto simpliciter ad dictum secundum quid

                      • Example

                        • Blind people

                          • The fact that “people are capable of seeing” is insufficient for the conclusion that “blind people are capable of seeing.”
                            • In this context, "people are capable of seeing" is defined as something like "in general, people are capable of seeing", which is insufficient (in light of our evidence that blind people exist) to establish that there are no blind people.

                    • Importance

                      • Common

                    • Source

                      • https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Accident_(fallacy)

                  • Converse accident fallacy

                    • Definition

                      • The fallacy of applying a rule (that only applies to some exceptional cases) to all cases in general.

                      • Example

                        • 1. The Tunguska explosion happened in Siberia.
                        • 2. Magnitogorsk Iron and Steel Works is in Siberia.
                        • 3. This is a bad company to invest in because it's just going to be destroyed by a meteorite.
                        • Here, a general rule is mistakenly made (that places in Siberia are likely to be destroyed by meteors in the foreseeable future) based on one exceptional case.

                    • Importance

                    • Source

                      • https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Converse_accident

                    • Related

                      • Slippery slope

                • Circularity

              • <broken link>

          • Presumptuous reasoning

            • Definition

            • Recognizing presumptuous reasoning

              • Common forms

                • Definition

                  • Common forms of presumptuous reasoning.

                • Presumption

                • Confirmatory thought

              • Commonly confused non-forms

                • Definition

                  • Forms/types/cases of reasoning that are frequently mistakenly considered to be presumptuous.

                • Quick conclusions

                  • Definition

                    • The idea that when someone comes to a quick conclusion, it is due to inadequate thinking about the subject.

                  • Explanations

                    • People often have sufficient previous thoughts that they used to reach their conclusion.
                    • Some seemingly complex matters are much less complex than they seem.
                      • So it is possible to arrive at a conclusion much quicker than might otherwise be expected.

        • Reasoning self-improvement

          • Definition

            • Literal self-improvement of reasoning. Without some of the usual connotations of "self-improvement".

          • Acknowledging mistakes

            • Definition

              • Admitting that you made mistake(s).

            • Importance

              • Long-term open mindedness

                • Often helps form long-term habits that are good for self-awareness and open mindedness.

              • Immediate open mindedness

                • Often helps with immediate self-awareness and open mindedness.

            • Internal and external acknowledgement

              • Internal acknowledgement

                • Definition

                  • Acknowledging one's mistake(s) to themselves.

                • Importance

                  • Consciously improving from mistakes requires acknowledging them at least internally.
                    • Oftentimes there's a little feeling/intuition that something might be a mistake, but we haven't really consciously acknowledged it as such.
                    • If we don't consciously acknowledge it, the feeling/intuition will typically fade away and leave us with no way to improve.

                  • Long-term open-mindedness

                  • Immediate open-mindedness

                • Limitations

                  • This typically doesn't make the mistake(s) feel as real/significant as external acknowledgement

              • External acknowledgement

                • Definition

                  • Acknowledging one's mistake(s) to other(s).

                • Importance

                  • This makes it much harder to delude yourself about how many mistakes you actually make.
                    • This typically makes it much harder to forget about mistakes.

                  • Long-term open-mindedness

                  • Immediate open-mindedness

            • Consistency

              • It's often easy to acknowledge small mistakes, or even major ones (in terms of how bad the reasoning was) if the impact was relatively insignificant.
              • But it's often significantly harder for us to acknowledge (or even recognize) bigger mistakes, so learning to acknowledge those is really the main importance of acknowledging mistakes in general. Though getting into the habit of acknowledging small mistakes probably helps with acknowledging bigger ones.

            • Related

              • (Un)conservative claims

              • Inflammatory statements

          • Analyzing your own discussions

            • Definition

            • Importance

              • There ordinarily isn't a clear feedback mechanism that works well for reasoning, so analyzing your own discussions can be a very helpful process.

            • Limitations

            • Analyzing a past discussion

              • Importance

                • We typically miss a lot of our own mistakes in the midst of our discussions, looking back at them (especially with the context of the things that are said later on) can sometimes help us see where our reasoning was flawed, which is useful to learn from our mistakes and change our minds.
                • It can often be relatively stress-free, since it's after the discussion is already over. And you can do it alone so that there's no one to judge you except yourself.

            • Analyzing a current discussion

              • Limitations

                • This often doesn't give you the full picture of a lot of things that become clearer after a discussion has finished.
                • You're mind is typically pretty occupied with the discussion you're having. Trying to analyze your discussion at the same time is likely going to involve making significant mistakes in one or both.
                  • An exception possibly being when responses are on a very large delay or something.

              • How to view it

                • Typically a much worse way to do it.

          • Solo analysis
            ~~Mostly expert, some parts belong in lower levels

            • Definition

              • Analyzing things on your own.

            • Limitations

            • Talking to yourself

              • Plausibly has benefits with remembering.
              • Plausibly has benefits with getting into character for multiple sides.

          • Questioning old beliefs
            ~~Advanced, link elsewhere in misc probably.

            • Definition

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Even if you're perfect at reasoning right now, you've surely formed many fundamental beliefs while you were previously not great at reasoning. So revisiting and updating your confidence for those beliefs is very important.

            • What beliefs to look for

              • Old

                • Especially from when you were not very good at reasoning.

              • Impactful

                • Justifying some very impactful belief(s) of yours.

            • How to question them

              • Look for hidden premises

                • Systematically map out your reasoning for the belief, ideally formally, and don't settle until the conclusion deductively follows from the premises (though including induction in premise(s) and the conclusion is fine).

              • Look for unsupported premises

                • Look for weaknesses in the premises.
                  Link some stuff here.

          • Finding weaknesses

            • Definition

              • Finding your own weaknesses.

            • Importance

              • Often we are not aware of many of our weaknesses, because the weaknesses we are not aware of are much more likely to remain, whereas we usually solve many that we are aware of.

            • Strategies

              • Due to this, getting independent https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=W7VkGKlZfqI3D-chGqzjjpwn is often very important to recognize your weaknesses.

              • Analyzing specific past errors/biases, particularly immediately after they occur.

        • Reasoning short-cuts

          • Definition

            • Practices that are intended to skip large portions of reasoning to make the process faster.

          • Importance

            • Using the wrong short-cuts can often be disastrous for good reasoning on the topic

            • It's common to use the wrong short-cuts

            • Using the right short-cuts consistently can save a ton of time

          • Heuristics

            • Definition

              • Heuristics is the process by which humans use mental shortcuts to arrive at decisions. Heuristics are simple strategies that humans, animals, organizations, and even machines use to quickly form judgments, make decisions, and find solutions to complex problems.
              • Often this involves focusing on the most relevant aspects of a problem or situation to formulate a solution.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Uncertain judgments and decisions based on heuristics are often good enough to satisfy a pressing need in situations of uncertainty, where information is incomplete.

              • Common

                • Everybody relies on heuristics practically all the time.

            • Limitations

              • It's very easy to rely too heavily on or overly trust heuristics.
              • Heuristics can differ from answers given by logic and probability.
                • While heuristic processes are used to find the answers and solutions that are most likely to work or be correct, they are not always right or the most accurate.
                  • Heuristics rely on simplificatied rules that try to cut likely unimpactful nuances to more quickly come up with an approach to a problem.
                  • Sometimes impactful nuances are accidentally cut.

            • Evolutionary cause

              • To solve problems, our brains have evolved to employ cognitive heuristics— rough shortcuts that get the right answer often, but not all the time.

            • Representativeness heuristic

              • Definition

                • Our tendency to assess phenomena by how representative they seem of various categories.
                • Wikipedia
                  Pull more from this.

              • UNORGANIZED

                • The representativeness heuristic is used when making judgments about the probability of an event being representational in character and essence of a known prototypical event.[1] It is one of a group of heuristics (simple rules governing judgment or decision-making) proposed by psychologists Amos Tversky and Daniel Kahneman in the early 1970s as "the degree to which [an event] (i) is similar in essential characteristics to its parent population, and (ii) reflects the salient features of the process by which it is generated".[1] The representativeness heuristic works by comparing an event to a prototype or stereotype that we already have in mind. For example, if we see a person who is dressed in eccentric clothes and reading a poetry book, we might be more likely to think that they are a poet than an accountant. This is because the person's appearance and behavior are more representative of the stereotype of a poet than an accountant.
                • The representativeness heuristic can be a useful shortcut in some cases, but it can also lead to errors in judgment. For example, if we only see a small sample of people from a particular group, we might overestimate the degree to which they are representative of the entire group. Heuristics are described as "judgmental shortcuts that generally get us where we need to go – and quickly – but at the cost of occasionally sending us off course."2] Heuristics are useful because they use effort-reduction and simplification in decision-making.[3]When people rely on representativeness to make judgments, they are likely to judge wrongly because the fact that something is more representative does not actually make it more likely.[4] The representativeness heuristic is simply described as assessing similarity of objects and organizing them based around the category prototype (e.g., like goes with like, and causes and effects should resemble each other). This heuristic is used because it is an easy computation. The problem is that people overestimate its ability to accurately predict the likelihood of an event. Thus, it can result in [neglect of relevant base rates and other cognitive biases.
                  Link to neglect of relevant base rates

              • Common resulting biases

                • Conjunction fallacy
                  Create section for it and link.

                  • Example: Tversky and Kahneman found that experimental subjects considered it less likely that a strong tennis player would “lose the first set” than that he would “lose the first set but win the match.”
                  • ^ A comeback seems more typical of a strong player, so we overestimate the probability of this complicated-but-sensible-sounding narrative compared to the probability of a superset (and thus more/equally likely) scenario.

                • Base rate fallacy

              • Source

                • Much of this was pulled from https://www.readthesequences.com/Biases-An-Introduction

        • Theoretical reasoning
          ~~Amateur

          • Definition

          • Importance / Limitations

            • Impactful

              • It is often overlooked because of a mistaken notion that theoretical reasoning is internal and (in itself) unverifiable, in contrast to empirical evidence.
                Important, should maybe be linked elsewhere. And/or theoretical reasoning should be linked in more places.
                • Supposing that we accept axioms like those recommended in axioms, we can decompose the theoretical reasoning and verify it through fundamental operations. This is clear via the chain if reducibility you'll find throughout the fundamental sections like standard deduction and induction
                  Likelihood (about the "mistaken")
                • Empirical evidence is evidence only because we trust things like induction, the semi-reliability of our mental faculties, etc., and reasoning for the trusthworthiness of empirical evidence from those things.
                  Likelihood (about the "mistaken")
                  • Thus, the interpretation of empirical evidence is also internal and (without accounting for specifics) just as (un)verifiable.
                    Relevance
                  • ^ Our theoretical reasoning is typically unsystematic
                    Relevance
              • Often the best/only method for finding a good answer
                • Many important hypotheses cannot be empirically tested but can be rationally investigated to a sufficient degree to come to a reasonable conclusion.

              • Inventions / Innovations

                • Virtually all inventions/innovations were based on a good amount of theoretical reasoning, especially the more significant ones.
                  • Eg. Computers
              • Easy to overlook practical obstacles
                • We often fail to properly account for unknown practical challenges.
                  • <broken link>
                    Likelihood
                  • Hofstadter's law
                    Likelihood
                • It is one of the easiest areas for confirmatory reasoning to creep in [...]
                • We are often biased to assume that the practical obstacles cannot be overcame.
                  • We are often biased to reject some ideas which we cannot understand the full reasoning for.

                • Related

              • Our theoretical reasoning is typically unsystematic
                • There is a general trend for applications of theoretical reasoning to be less systematic and thus less reliable.
                  Likelihood + relevance | Probably split
                • This is mainly due to our cultural propensity to perform theoretical reasoning in a more vague and unsystematic manner than how we typically deal with empirical evidence.
                  Relevance
                  • Thus one should not typically heavily rely on heuristics about the reliability of theoretical reasoning vs empirical reasoning, it is better to consider the reliability of theoretical/empirical reasoning on a case-by-case basis.
                    Relevance

            • Difficult

              • It is one of the easiest areas for confirmatory reasoning to creep in (both for and against some theoretical reasoning), because it implicitly involves starting with a conclusion.
                • Against because: If one sees theoretical reasoning for a claim they disagree with, they will (typically subconsciously) begin with the conclusion that it is wrong.
                • ^ confirmatory thought

          • How to view it

            • A very important but limited kind of reasoning that must be dealt with carefully, both when using and contesting it.
            • It is one of the easiest areas for confirmatory thought to creep in [...]

          • Immediate correctness

            • Definition

              • Being correct about the immediate thing.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Immediate correctness doesn't matter as much as correctness in general.

                  • Correctness in general is dependent on overall reasoning performance rather than immediate correctness.
                    • The way to improve overall reasoning performance is by looking at how you reason in the short-term.
                • Correctness ≠ good reasoning

              • Common

                • People tend to think being correct entails that they used good reasoning to get there
                • Immediate correctness is usually less important than the reasoning used to get there.
                  • The reasoning used to get there: How well you reasoned to arrive at your conclusion.

            • Correctness ≠ good reasoning

              • Importance

                • Impactful

                • Common

                  • People tend to think being correct entails that they used good reasoning to get there

              • People tend to think being correct entails that they used good reasoning to get there.

                • People give themselves feedback in accordance with how close/far they believe they are from the truth, rather than how well they reasoned.
                  • This usually leads them to do behaviors that make them feel closer to the truth, which often don't actually get them closer to the truth.

                  • Types

                    • Reason well but still far from truth

                      • Definition

                        • We sometimes go "ah, I was wrong" and think of it as a mistake, when really it was just bad luck or lacking important evidence.

                      • Limitations

                        • This is much less common than reasoning badly but still close to the truth because we tend to be biased towards <broken link>, at least intellectually.

                    • Reason badly but still close to truth

                      • We often go "ah, so I was right!" and focus on that, regardless of whether we reasoned well to get there.

            • <broken link>

          • "Winning"

            • Definition (broad)

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Common

                • People often focus on "beating the opponent", "winning the debate", etc.

              • Impactful

                • Prioritizes bad reasoning and deprioritizes good reasoning: "Winning" debates is typically measured in terms of metrics that significantly differ from good reasoning. So focusing on "winning" typically entails deprioritizing good reasoning to some extent, and prioritizing those other metrics.

                  • How convinced the audience is

                    • Persuasion strategies that don't prioritize reasoning tend to convince people more
                      • Most debates involve a lot of "soft" reasoning
                        • Therefore people are much more likely to be irrational about them.
                          Relevance | Link to a section about how people tend to be more irrational about soft reasoning, once it's available.
                      • Therefore we're encouraged to use such strategies, especially subconsciously from past experiences of them working better.
                      • We're often taught to use these strategies in thinks like debate classes.
                      • Eg. Characterization

                  • How stumped the opponent is

                    • Stumping the opponent is much easier with rhetoric and fallacies than with good reasoning/solid sources.
                      This has to do with how unreasonable opponents and the audience tend to be.

                • Encourages confirmatory thought

                  • "Winning" in this context means successfully defending a certain conclusion, so the natural instinct is to start with that conclusion in one's thinking and try to justify/defend it.

            • Related (many very related sections)

              • Persuasion

                • Limitations

                  • Not a good indication of truth

                    • Most people (probably even among groups who you would expect to be exceptions) are pretty uncritical, so convincing them is not a good indication of your point being correct.

              • Competition

              • Refutation

              • Correctness ≠ good reasoning

          • Refutation

              • Prioritizing refuting others' points.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

              • Common

                • Often we get into a mindset of prioritizing finding what's wrong with opposing arguments and counterarguments.
                  • This isn't necessarily a bad thing.

            • How to view it
              Depends on rank.

              • A mindset to largely avoid for now.
                ≤ ~Adept
              • A useful tool that should usually be used when there are important ideas that aren't being questioned much.
                ≥ ~Advanced

            • Pros / Cons

              • Prioritizing refutation tends to reduce the amount of effort we put into understanding and being open to opposing ideas.
                • It is crucial for good reasoning to often heavily prioritize <broken link> such claims.
              • Can help a lot with finding hard-to-find flaws or counterarguments.
                ~Advanced

        • Time usage in reasoning

          • Definition

          • Importance

            • We're often bad at time usage in reasoning

            • It's quite consequential

            • We can get better at time usage

          • How our emotions often affect our time usage

            • Social pressure

              • We tend to feel pressured to finish our reasoning in a very short amount of time in live discussions, especially when face-to-face with people. One of the biggest advantages of text conversations is that it's much more socially acceptable to take much longer to think things through properly.

            • Impatience

              • Types of impatience

                • Fatigue

                • Overwhelm

                • Frustration

              • We often start using a lot of dubious reasoning short-cuts

              • We don't perform a lot of the checks or double-checks that we would normally perform when we're at our peak. Time usage can be a decent way to measure this, as typically when we're thinking things through properly on a remotely complex topic, it takes a while for us to properly consider all the important relevant points and overall reason well*. When we get into an emotional state where we by default stop performing a lot of the checks that are necessary for good reasoning, it will take much shorter amounts of time for us to come up with a response that we feel substantially compelled to send instead of thinking further upon.
                • * Assuming we aren't pulling from our memories of our conclusions from when we previously did the same process on the topic or something.

          • Proper time usage

            • Strategies for going slower in general

              • Reasoning alone

                • Importance

                • ^ Impactful

                • ^ It often makes it easier to take more time with one's reasoning.
                  • ^ It removes social pressure to reason quickly.
                    Perhaps link sections about how unconfidence lowers social value in many cases. Probably do the same in other places in time usage as well.

            • Pausing briefly to override system 1 thinking

              • Importance: Impactful

              • ^ Taking more time often results in our initial intuitive inclination fading enough for our more reliable reasoning processes to have a fair chance to overrule it.
                • There is a tendency for us to trust our initial intuitive inclination far too much, so this is quite important.
                  Relevance
              • As a result of how our emotions often affect our time usage, it's important to make yourself take the necessary time and think things through more than you're tempted to in cases where you know it's likely necessary, even if you're very emotionally compelled to take various short-cuts instead.
                Organize this a bit better.

            • Ideal time usage in text

              • Rereading

              • ^ It's often ideal to reread some key sections a few times in a text conversation.
                • There's often going to be a lot of useful relevant information that you'll otherwise forget or fail to notice/deeply understand.
                  Link to consequences of misunderstandings.

            • Definition

            • Importance

              • It's usually easy to blunder in reasoning

                • The instability of large reasoning pyramids

                • Analogies

                  • Chess is like reasoning, and it's easy to blunder in chess.

              • It's often very consequential
                Should include more or something

                • Reasoning blunders in crucial areas are common

              • Feedback is not guaranteed

                • It's quite possible (and common) for people to use reasoning built on a blunder for important decisions all throughout their entire life without ever realizing it.

                  • Examples

                • So it is incredibly important to do many thorough and varied checks for blunders in your own reasoning.

        • Misc

          • Enthymemes

            • Definition: Arguments with hidden premise(s).

              • Hidden premises:

              • ^ Unstated propositions that are required for an argument to be valid and/or sound.
                • The unstated propositions can be ordinary premises, or inference rules (because an inference rule can be thought of as a type of premise).
                  Explain why in the section for inference rules, which will likely be in Axioms, and then link that explanation here.
              • ^ Synonym: Unstated assumptions

            • Example

              • "Socrates is mortal because he's human."
              • ^ The complete formal syllogism would be:
              • ^ ^ All humans are mortal. (major premise – unstated)
              • ^ ^ Socrates is human. (minor premise – stated)
              • ^ ^ Therefore, Socrates is mortal. (conclusion – stated)

      • Expression/interpretation

        • Importance

          • Impactful

          • ^ Reduce misunderstandings
            Both your own and others'

            • Relevance of the interpretation/expression section: Misunderstandings are caused by lacking expression/interpretation abilities. This section is about expression and interpretation.
              Relevance

            • Cost of misunderstandings

              • Exponential misunderstandings: While trying to resolve a misunderstanding, more misunderstanding(s) can arise, at a rate faster than they are resolved.
                Relevance
                • When this happens, it's easy for the amount of required work to resolve the original misunderstanding to grow exponentially until a massive amount of terms have had to be defined, a massive amount of ideas have had to be clarified, etc.
                  Relevance
                  • Where there is one misunderstanding, there is often ample room for many more.
                    Likelihood
                • Learning interpretation/expression can greatly reduce the rate of misunderstandings arising, and the speed at which you can resolve them.
                  Relevance

              • Cost of unresolved misunderstandings (high)

                • Often, some misunderstandings are crucial to resolve in order to efficiently continue with the reasoning interaction where the misunderstandings arose.

                  • Knowledge piggybacking
                    Create and link

                    • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=ab0k5w-abYxOLCZM9aOYOhgR

                  • Critical piggybacking

                    • In order to consistently and effectively perform this piggybacking, you cannot back out of discussions where misunderstandings arise.
                      • Often even if there are lots of them, and lots are primarily caused by other party(s).
                        Relevance
                      • Because most people are not very good at interpretation/expression, even people who have great counterarguments that you haven't heard before.
                        Likelihood
                        • So it's important to learn to be the primary driver of resolving misunderstandings.

                • "Or similar" definitions

                  • Definition

                    • Definitions in which (a) major component(s) are paired with a qualifier such as "such as", "or similar", "or something like that", etc. without adequate clarification on which concepts would be regarded as "similar"/etc.
                      • "Adequate clarification" meaning sufficient clarification that it can be reasonably expected (from the perspective of the definition-giver, with >75% confidence) that the definition-receiver will adequately understand which concepts would be regarded as "similar"/etc.
                        • "Adequately understand" meaning understand well enough to avoid significant misunderstanding(s).

                • Recognizing vague wording

                • Dealing with vague wording

                  • Asking what they mean

                    • Importance

                    • ^ Impactful

                    • ^ Good reading comprehension by accounting for different possibilities when other people use vague wording.

                • Avoiding vague wording

                  • Use the longer clearly-spelled-out version

                    • Importance / Limitations

                    • ^ Impactful

                    • ^ Not always practical.
                      • Spelled-out version is sometimes much longer.
                      • The same idea is sometimes used many times.

                • Examples

                  • That, this, it, them, those, etc.

                  • Example free speech definition misunderstanding

                    • Two statements using different definitions:
                    • ^ "Free speech is sacred" meaning "Government censorship should be minimal".
                    • ^ "Free speech has limits" meaning "Private platforms can moderate content".
                    • Here it appears that either "free speech" or "sacred/limits" is being defined differently between the two parties, likely causing them to talk past each other.

                  • Definition via analogy

              • Specific definitions

                • Note

                  • Unless otherwise stated, each definition also applies (although usually with slight modifications) to very similar words.

                    • Example

                      • The definitions for "know" apply (except for the obvious change about how the word would be used) to "known".

                • Importance

                • Formal and informal

                  • Reasoning

                    • Informal reasoning

                    • Formal reasoning
                      Move, probably to informal reasoning. Make sure that backlinks are preserved in the process.

                      • Definition

                        • The process of drawing valid inferences in a well-structured and irrefutable (given certain axioms) manner.

                        • Distinctions

                          • This is not to suggest that the premises we begin with in this process necessarily need to be irrefutable.
                          • Nor is it to suggest that we cannot make mistakes in determining whether an argument is valid or not while using formal reasoning, just that under normal conditions validity is technically ensured.

                      • Importance

                        • Very important for systematic reasoning.
                          • Helps a lot with precision.
                            Likelihood
                            • Representing ideas formally tends to require representing them precisely, without ambiguity.
                              Likelihood
                              • Formalisms tend to be completely unambiguous.
                              • It's possible (and common) to represent the informal aspect as eg. a premise in a formal argument.
                                • By having the knowledge to represent the rest of it in a formal manner, one would likely have a much easier time representing or supporting the informal parts in a more formal manner upon request.
                                  Relevance
                                  • Therefore the knowledge of formal reasoning could still help a lot with precision.
                                    Relevance
                                • Even in this case, much of the reasoning is likely made formal in transforming the processes from the premises to the conclusion into a formal argument.
                                  Relevance
                          • Helps a lot with thoroughness.
                            Likelihood
                            • If one skips over an important step of the formal process, it tends be obvious and able to be pointed out easily.
                              Likelihood
                              • Therefore it would often help a lot with being thorough, by making it easy to see where one is not thorough and where one must be more thorough to completely represent their position formally.
                                Relevance
                          • Importance of systematic reasoning
                            Relevance

                    • Related

                      • Epistemic rationality

                  • Debate

                    • Formal debates often involve specific preparation, timers and rounds, a moderator, etc.
                    • Informal debate is typically spontaneous, unmoderated, untimed and unstructured.

                  • Colloquial

                    • This typically has to do with something like social atmosphere.

                  • Argument

                    • A process of reasoning consisting of one or more premises (statements providing evidence or support) that lead to a conclusion (the claim being proven).
                    • In formal contexts, "argument" is usually used to specify a structured version of the above, where the premises, conclusion and inferences are all explicitly represented as such.

                  • Rationality

                    • Epistemic rationality

                      • The practice of systematically aligning your beliefs with the truth and updating your worldview based on objective evidence.

                      • Distinction

                        • Instrumental rationality

                          • Epistemic rationality is often quite useful for instrumental rationality, but some specific aspects of epistemic rationality don't improve instrumental rationality much.
                            • Eg. In some cases one knows what would be the best way to act to achieve their goals, but they don't actually do it.
                              • Eg. One might know that the best way to convince another person is by speaking calmly with them, but then still end up shouting at them.
                          • Sometimes it even reduces it.
                            • Eg. Deluding yourself into thinking that you're more attractive than you are might make you feel more confident, which might make you more effective at achieving your goals.
                            • However this is likely quite rare.
                              • Long-term, there are typically other strategies for achieving emotional effects like confidence, without the potentially huge cost of getting into the habit of deluding yourself in an emotionally pleasant manner.
                              • ^ The habit of emotionally deluding one's self in a pleasant manner tends to frequently cause of a ton of cognitive biases.
                                • Wishful thinking
                                • Confirmation bias
                                • Groupthink
                                • Rationalization
                                • Sunk cost fallacy
                                • Gambler's fallacy

                    • Instrumental rationality

                      • Acting in accordance with your fundamental goals, whatever those are.

                      • Distinction

                        • "your [...] goals" as in the goals that you hold, not necessarily selfish/self-interested goals.

                    • Emotionless rationality

                      • Lacking emotion or passion.

                      • Distinction

                        • Epistemic rationality

                          • Trying to repress one's emotions tends to lead to lots of issues that get in the way of epistemic rationality, and it tends to be correlated with a conflation of epistemic rationality with emotionless rationality, which would of course be correlated with a lack of epistemic rationality.
                            Link to section about repression

                    • Source

                      • What is rationality? Short video

                • Logic

                  • 0 order (prop)

                    • True and false (standard)

                    • Inverse

                      • Adds a "NOT" to both sides of a syllogism.
                        Check if it actually is a syllogism, link whatever it really is, and link NOT.

                      • Example

                        • The inverse of P→Q is ¬P→¬Q.

                      • Notes

                        • The inverse of a statement is logically equivalent to the converse of that statement.
                      • Wikipedia)

                    • Converse

                      • Parent definition

                      • The result of reversing the two variables.
                        Maybe change the term "variables" to wherever the standard term is in prop log.

                      • Example

                        • For the implication P → Q, the converse is Q → P.

                      • Nuance

                      • Wikipedia)

                    • Contrapositive

                      • The inference of going from a conditional statement into its logically equivalent contrapositive.
                      • The contrapositive of a statement has its antecedent and consequent negated and swapped.
                        • The contrapositive of P→Q is ¬Q→¬P.
                          • If P, Then Q. — If not Q, Then not P.

                      • Example

                        • "If it is raining, then I wear my coat." — "If I don't wear my coat, then it isn't raining."

                      • The law of contraposition

                        • The law of contraposition says that a conditional statement is true if, and only if, its contrapositive is true.

                        • Proof by contrapositive

                      • Synonym

                        • Transposition
                      • https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Contraposition

                    • Well-defined proposition
                      Reconcile with well-defined propositions

                      • Definition

                        • A proposition whose definition is assigns it a single correct truth value.
                        • Synonym: Well-formed formula

                        • Related

                          • Well-defined expression

                    • Inference rule

                      • Definition: A logical pattern that allows one to deduce a valid conclusion from a set of given premise(s).

                  • 1 order (pred)

                    • Proper part

                      • A part of something that is not the whole of that thing.

                    • Converse

                      • The result of reversing its two constituent statements.

                      • Examples

                        • Prop example
                        • For the categorical proposition "All S are P", the converse is "All P are S".

                      • Nuance

                        • The truth of the converse is generally independent from that of the original statement.
                      • Wikipedia)

                • Probabilities

                  • Probability

                    • Importance

                      • Difficulty: It's very easy to conflate many of these definitions of probability.
                        • This can result in making bad decisions based on conflating the definitions.
                          Relevance

                    • Plausibility

                      • A quantification of how plausible an event or proposition is, given our state of knowledge/ignorance.

                      • Example

                        • Fair coin toss

                          • Given the plausibility definition, the coin toss has a 50% probability of landing on each side because we don't have any reason to pick one over the other. i.e. The principle of indifference combined with the knowledge that there are two possible outcomes.

                          • Distinction

                            • We're talking about an idealized scenario where you have absolutely no evidence for the coin tending to land on one side or the other.
                            • In reality, coins are slightly more likely to land on the side that starts facing up.

                      • Related

                        • Plausibility school of thought
                          Maybe create a section for this?

                          • This definitions falls under the plausibility school of thought.

                        • Bayesian inference

                          • It's the definition typically used in bayesian reasoning.
                            • Although it's possible to use bayesian reasoning with propensity as well.

                    • Credence

                      • The subjective probability we assign to things based (in part) on the evidence available to us as individuals.

                      • Example

                        • Dice
                          • Typically has an equal subjective probability (or credence) of landing on any particular side.
                          • While the physics likely objectively at-least-virtually determine the result once the dice is thrown, given our human limitations, we do not typically have the means to predict the result with more-than-extremely-slightly-better-than-random accuracy.

                      • Synonyms

                        • Confidence

                          • Confidence in a proposition/event X.

                          • Distinctions

                            • Different from one's "confidence" in propositions/events in general like in epistemic self-confidence.
                            • Different from one's "confidence", as in self-esteem.
                            • Different from one's "confidence", as in how confident they appear, such as during some kind of persuasion.
                        • Subjective probability
                        • Epistemic probability

                    • Frequentist probability

                      • Defined in terms of the limit of the frequency of an event occurring.

                      • Example

                        • Fair coin toss

                          • Given the frequentist definition, the coin would have a 50% probability of landing on each side because if we would keep flipping it, the ratio of the two outcomes would approach 1/1, so the fraction of either one happening takes up half the total possibility space.

                          • Distinction

                    • Propensity
                      Consider combining this with frequentist probability. I'm unsure about it because the definition of "frequentist probability" I came across (in 3swap video) would seem to consider a coinflip in terms of repeated very similar events, whereas the definition I have here seems to only be about events/propositions that are exactly the same. Should probably look into it deeper at some point.

                      • The actual, real probability of events occurring / propositions being true.

                      • Examples

                        • Dice
                          • Typically has a ~100% propensity to land on the side it lands on, at least once it's thrown. Because:
                            • ("At least once it's thrown", because we don't intend to make claims against free will in this example.)
                          • Dice are typically atleast nearly entirely determined by the laws of physics while falling through the air, in such a way that the final result is typically at least virtually guaranteed, given the exact same starting conditions.
                        • Determinism
                          • If determinism is true, everything is entirely determined to occur from previous events and there is no randomness, so each event objectively either has 0% or 100% propensity to occur.
                        • Quantum mechanics
                          • Might involve interactions that follow genuinely random probability distributions, so the propensity of different quantum events may be quite varied.

                      • Synonyms

                        • Objective probability
                        • Chance

                • Informal

                  • True and false (standard)

                  • True and false (probability)

                    • True: Usually high-probability beliefs, occasionally axioms.
                    • False: Low-probability beliefs.
                    • These definitions are uncommon.
                      • Typically the uncertainty in statements like "I know it's true that we have bread." lies in the word "know" rather than the word "true".

                        • Know (probability)

                  • "Soft" reasoning

                    • Definition

                      • Reasoning that cannot easily be broken down to a formal process using any typical methods, and that thus requires an unorthodox and/or informal process.

                    • Importance

                      • Impactful

                        • "Soft" reasoning makes up a large portion of important reasoning.

                  • Hot/cold biases
                    Improve

                    • Hot biases
                      Link a bunch of biases here to categorize them, and maybe add this to related under cognitive biases.

                      • Motivated irrationality where our interests, emotional responses or visceral reactions to things influences our thinking process.

                      • Related: Cognitive biases

                    • Cold biases
                      Link a bunch of biases here to categorize them, and maybe add this to related under cognitive biases.

                      • Unmotivated irrationality which are a result of certain mental shortcuts and routines we rely on even in situations where they do not really apply.

                      • Related: Cognitive biases

                  • Free speech

                    • "Free speech" is a vague term that should ideally be defined very explicitly before engaging in a serious discussion that uses it.

                      • Example misunderstanding

                  • Ad hoc

                    • Science and philosophy definition

                      • The addition of extraneous hypotheses to save another hypothesis from being falsified.
                        Link to falsification
                      • Ad hoc hypotheses compensate for anomalies not anticipated by the theory in its unmodified form.

                    • Source

                  • Informal operations

                    • OR

                      • Depending on the context, it can mean https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=n7OMAZ2eLgm2YIZTBh-L2db8 or standard OR.

                    • IF-THEN

                      • Definition

                        • Usually a material conditional with the added requirement that there be some causal or epistemic relation between the condition and consequence.
                        • Sometimes instead a IF-AND-ONLY-IF (material biconditional).
                          • Example: "I'll buy you a new wallet if you need one" may be interpreted as a biconditional ("if and only if"), since the speaker doesn't intend a valid outcome to be buying the wallet whether or not the wallet is needed (as in a conditional).

                • Misc

                  • Know

                    • Know (certainty)

                    • Know (probability)

                      • Know: Have more than C credence in something being true.
                      • Typically C is more than 85% and less than 100%.

                      • Distinction

                        • Distinct from know (certainty)
                          • Less than 100%.

                  • Good/bad/etc.

                    • Moral/axiological

                    • For reasoning

                      • Good for reasoning, bad for reasoning, etc.
                      • This definition is uncommon, but for this particular context (a reasoning guide) we use it for convenience.

                  • Philosophical logic

                    • The mathematical investigation of basic notions of logic.
                    • The deployment of logic to help with mathematical problems, so ‘philosophical logic’ means both (a) the philosophical investigation of the basic notions of logic and (b) the deployment of logic to help with philosophical problems.
                    • In the context of this guide, in largely includes these sections:

                      • Axioms

                      • Standard deduction

                      • Probabilistic reasoning

                      • Expression/interpretation
