                  • Made-up terms

                    • Definition

                      • Some relevant terms that we've made up, to make it easier to talk about certain concepts.
                      • Note that this probably doesn't include all the made-up terms used throughout the guide, but hopefully most of them.

                    • Importance / Limitations

                      • Probably not very useful since nobody uses these terms.

                      • Could maybe be nice for communities wanting to adopt some misc handy short-cut terms.

                    • Critical piggybacking

                    • Other-shoe reviews

                    • RAC

                      • Definition

                        • RAC is an acronym for "rationally action-compelling", which means:
                        • Truly better to have/do (all else being equal) in a sense that is action-compelling to a perfectly rational agent, without needing any moral/axiological axioms/presuppositions.

                        • i.e. / expounding

                          • In other words, a proposed kind of ought that can be derived epistemically from non-oughts, and which would, if its potential existence is known by a perfectly rational agent, compel that perfectly rational agent to "act in accordance with" it, for rational reasons."
                          • "Act in accordance with" being defined as "attempt to (in the absence of sufficient competing RAC or potentially RAC things) maximize the average* (or mean, etc.) completion of". "Action-compelling" being defined similarly.
                          • `*` "Average completion" or alternatives typically being defined in terms of subjective probability, rather than objective probability. However this might change depending on the context.
                    • Literaloo
                      • Definition: Literally literally

                      • Importance

                        • There are a decent number of situations where you want to clarify that you mean something literally, but there isn't an easy way to do so because the normal explicit methods have other connotations.
                          • For example it might be interpreted as rude, and prefacing what you say with "literally" is likely just going to sound like emphasis of the rude thing.

              • Definitional options

                • Recommended options

                  • Most common definition

                    • Definition

                      • The current most common definition in the world.
                    • Should be the default choice in most situations.

                  • Most common definition (given broad context)

                    • Definition

                      • The current most common definition given the broad context.

                        • Broad context

                      • Examples

                  • Other's definition

                    • Definition

                      • The definition that the person(s) you're having the discussion with appear(s) to be using.

                    • Limitations

                      • Difficulty of guessing definitions

                      • Potential conflations

                        • The other person may be conflating multiple definitions, meaning using their definition may cause more confusion.

                  • Making one up

                    • Limitations

                      • If there is an already-present definition, it can cause a lot of confusion to make one up instead.

                    • How to view it

                      • If no definition fitting one of the other options works well, writing your own may be the most efficient option despite the potential confusion.

                  • How to choose

                • Unrecommended options

                  • Vague definitions

                    • Why to avoid

                      • To avoid conflating ideas
                        • Vague definitions frequently cause conflations.
                      • To avoid confusing them

                  • Etymology-based definition

                    • Definition

                      • Definition based primarily on the etymology of the word.

                    • Why to avoid

                      • Confusion

                      • Reasons to adopt

                        • "Deeper truths"

                          • Definition

                            • The view that the etymology of words hide deeper truths about those words.
                            • This is a semi-common view, particularly among conspiracy theorists.

                  • How to avoid

              • Definitional consistency

                • Definition

                • Importance

                  • Changing a definition mid-discussion often causes insane amounts of confusion (usually for both parties).

                    • Exceptions

                      • If you make it very clear when you're using a different definition and what that other definition is, most confusion can usually be avoided.
                  • Unknowingly using multiple definitions is called "conflation" and is one of the biggest causes for faulty reasoning ever.

                • Methods

                  • Pick a definition and stick with it

                    • This is the most reliable way to avoid conflations and semantic confusion.

              • Definitional differences

                • Importance

                  • When you and the other person are using different definitions, it's very important to recognize and fix this, otherwise you could end up talking past each other for a long time.

                • Types of definitional differences

                  • Different definitions

                    • Definition

                      • This is the typical case where people have different definitions for the same word.

                    • Examples

                      • The hot dog ran
                        • The dog (animal) that was hot (temperature) ran.
                        • The dog (animal) that was hot (attractive) ran.
                        • The "hot dog" (food) ran.

                  • Different sub-definitions

                    • Definition

                      • Sub-definition

                      • ^ A definition for a (word that is inside of a definition for the main word in question).

                      • Example

                        • Two people may appear to be using the same standard definition for a classical God, but define one of the relevant properties (eg. omnipotence) differently. One might define it as maximally powerful (as powerful as possible within logical constraints), while another may define it as having power over absolutely everything, including logic.
                        • ^ The definition for classical theism just mentions "omnipotence", so they can both point to the same definition and think they're talking about the same concept, but due to the different definition of the term *inside* the definition for classical theism, they are actually talking about different concepts.

                    • Importance

                      • Impactful

                      • ^ People can seem to agree on the main definition due to using the same words, but they can actually mean separate things due to defining part(s) of the definition for the main word differently.
                        • This can lead to a significant amount of time wasted from misunderstandings, perhaps making the difference between changing someone's mind / understanding someone, and not.

                        • Example
                          Likelihood

                  • Different "orders of operations"

                    • Definition

                      • Abiguity due to not knowing which parts to read as connected to which other parts.

                    • Examples

                      • A definition for a word that is inside of a definition for the main word in question.
                        • A definition for a (word that is inside of a definition for the main word in question).
                        • (A definition for a word) that is inside of a definition for the main word in question.

                  • Assumed details

                    • People sometimes assume details for a term that weren't explicitly given.
                    • These assumed details can often differ from the intended details, if there were even intended details in the first place.

                • Recognizing when definitional differences occur

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                    • ^ When people say things that seem very obviously wrong, it is very often due to some definitional difference.

                  • Checking for possible interpretations
                    Reorganize or split this into other sections here? Not sure

                    • Definition

                      • Checking for different ways that a person could be using terminology.
                      • Example: Using a different definition.

                    • Importance

                      • Impactful

                      • ^ Likely to save a substantial amount of time to catch misunderstandings sooner rather than later.
                        Link to section on the cost of misunderstandings.

                    • Definitions

                      • Maybe try to think of all the common definitions that could plausibly be used for the circumstance.

                  • Topics with a lot of confusing/alternative definitions

                    • Example

                    • "Soft" reasoning topics

                      • Tentative explanation

                        • People tend to reason about these topics in a vibes-based way, so they tend to talk about the topics in a vibes-based way, so the definitions common within these topics primarily developed to capture those vibes, rather than the logic content.
                          • This is why for example "morality" can mean a million different things to different people, but the vast majority of those meanings have a similar vibe.

                      • Specific topics

                        • Philosophy

                      • How to deal with this

                    • The topics you specialize in

                      • Importance

                        • Expert terminology tends to differ from colloquial terminology, so you can expect that laypersons will often not be able to understand you very well if you start using a topic's expert terminology to talk about the topic.
                          • Misc note: Sometimes this expert terminology is to make it easier for experts to speak with each other, and sometimes just to sound more knowledgable/intelligent or because of tradition.

                      • How to deal with this

                  • People likely to have definitional differences

                    • People who use a lot of niche definitions

                      • Importance

                        • When people use niche definitions in many other areas, there's a good chance they'll use niche definitions in the area you're talking about.

                      • How to deal with this

                    • People who likely are(n't) very familiar with relevant niche definitons

                      • Example

                        • One philosopher and one layperson talking about ethics. The philosopher uses terminology that's standard among philosophers, but non-standard to lay-people, or has a different meaning. For example a philosopher may use the word "utilitarianism" to refer to the ethical framework, while the layperson might interpret this to be referring to the advocacy of "utility" as in "practical usefulness", rather than pleasure minus suffering or similar.

                      • Importance

                      • How to deal with this

                        • Internalizing that some groups will be (un)familiar with some terms

                          • Unfamiliar: It's recommended to internalize that certain groups will be unfamiliar with certain types of terminology and likely to use other terminology to talk about the same concepts.
                            • You: Highly recommended to account for the fact that you are likely in many groups that are unfamiliar with certain types of terms.
                            • ^ And as such, in contexts that you are unfamiliar with, you should account for the fact that you likely do not know many of the definitions being used.
                            • ^ Even (and especially!) for words that you otherwise believe yourself to be familiar with.
                          • Familiar: Recommended to internalize that certain groups will be familiar with certain types of terminology that you are unfamiliar with.

                        • Memorizing specific groups' definitional behavior

                          • Recommended (and most crucial) to internalize what many of these specfic groups and types of terminology are (within reason).

                  • Seemingly absurd statements

                    • Definition

                      • Statements that on the surface seem absurd, based on the way you are interpreting them.

                    • Importance

                      • Oftentimes, statements will seem absurd because they are absurd given the interpretation we have of them, but not the interpretation that the author intended.
                        • One might be tempted to argue that "they said something absurd either way", and that the statement should be counterargued regardless of the intention. Depending on the context, this may make sense. But I would argue that more often than not, it is more productive to ask for clarification/definitions in order to try to understand what the author meant to say, to be able to address their actual reasoning.
                          Probably move this somewhere else and link it here, like a section about debate habits or something.

                    • How to deal with this

                      • Rewrite in simpler words

                        • Definition: Asking them to rewrite the seemingly absurd statement in simpler words.

                        • Importance / limitations

                          • This often makes it clear what they were trying to say.
                          • It often isn't sufficient to make it clear what they meant to say, in which case this can be a waste of time.

                • Identifying specific definitional differences

                  • Definition

                    • Identifying exactly a specific definitional difference is, often after you start to suspect that one might be at play.

                  • Source-specific strategies

                    • "Soft" reasoning topics
                    • The topics you specialize in
                    • Their irrelevant niche definitions
                    • Your/their relevant niche definitions
                    • Seemingly absurd statements
                  • Check/think about the context to see if you can figure out where they're coming from.
                  • Check for different definition(s) for key term(s).
                    • Unlearn the habit of assuming what is meant for key terms when it isn't obvious.
                  • Check for insinuated details that weren't technically included.

                • Dealing with definitional differences

                  • Adopt their definition(s) for the purposes of the discussion, if it wouldn't be too confusing.
                  • Ask them to use different definition(s) if it would otherwise be too confusing.
                  • Try to adapt to their contextual style of speaking.

              • Related

                • Ostensive definition

            • Conflations

              • Definition

                • When a term is used or interpreted to mean two incompatible concepts without the user/interpreter in question realizing.

              • Importance

                • Common

                  • Conflations are one of the most common causes of reasoning errors.
                  • It's so easy to conflate very similar concepts.
                  • Multiple very similar concepts often arise.
                    • It's common for multiple very similar concepts to (at least on the surface) fit a situation well.

              • How conflations arise

                • <Link starting with conclusion section>

                • Vague definitions

                • Complex reasoning

              • Others' conflations

                • Make sure a key definition doesn't shift during the discussion.
                • If something doesn't seem to add up, you may want to check to see if they used a key term differently in different places.

              • Related

            • Being clear

              • Stating the obvious

                • Definition

                  • Stating what you believe the other person(s) present likely know(s).

                • Importance

                  • Useful for making sure that others understand.
                    • Especially when you're only like 60% confident that they know it.
                  • Useful for persuasion
                    ~~Advanced Link, support

                • Limitations

                  • Often wastes time.

              • Quantification

                • Definition

                  • Providing a specific quantity of relevant values/variables.

                • Importance

                  • Impactful

                    • Helps with being clear

                • Quantifying confidence

                  • Definition

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                      • Helpful for applying more systematic inductive reasoning.

                • Quantifying error margin

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                      • Helpful for applying more systematic inductive reasoning.

                • Quantifying relevance

                  • Importance

                    • Helpful for more systematic decision-making
                  • Reducing usage of very high/low relevance quantifiers

                  • (Un)conservative claims

                  • Distinguishing likelihood and relevance

              • Reducing usage of very high/low relevance quantifiers
                ~~expert

                • Norm: relevance > likelihood

              • Slowly walking people through reasoning

                • Importance

                  • Impactful

                    • People tend to skim reasoning, especially if there's a lot of it given at once.

                  • Improvable

                    • People tend to go away too fast for other people to properly, systematically understand.

              • Parentheses/brackets

                • Definition

                  • Using () and [] to make the logical structure of claims more clear.

                • Examples

                  • We can learn much more consistent means of accounting for [past reasoning that may have been forgotten].

            • Sentences

              • Declarative vs interrogative vs imperative sentences

                • Declarative sentences

                  • Declarative sentences express what is the case.
                  • "The door is open."
                  • Different declarative sentences can express the same idea.
                    • The English sentence "Snow is white" and the German sentence "Schnee ist weiß" express the same idea.

                • Interrogative sentences

                  • Interrogative sentences request information.
                  • "Is the door open?"

                • Imperative sentences

                  • Imperative sentences issue command(s).
                  • "Open the door!"

            • Related

          • Requesting info

            • Definition

            • Importance / limitations

              • Common

                • It's very common for people to request info in one way or another.

              • Impactful

                • When used correctly, asking for info can be very useful for understanding what others mean.

                • People often give vague/inadequate info

                  • Because of attempted anticipation

                    • Often very hard to solve.

                  • Often relatively easy to get clearer info

                    • It's often relatively simple to see where the info was inadequate and ask for more clear/specific info about a particular part.
                    • When attempted anticipation is involved, the clearer info is often not clear enough.

                  • Can easily waste time

                    • If people cannot give sufficiently clear info sufficiently quickly, then often very little progress is made per info request.
                      • In such cases it's typically better to either try to work with the vague info or abandon the matter altogether.
                • Slow: It's often a little slow for people to give the info.

            • Requesting definition(s) / meaning(s)
              Possibly move importance/limitations from here to [definitions and/or definitional differences] or vise versa. Or maybe just move this under definitions.

              • Importance / Limitations

                • Impactful

                • ^ Definitions are usually not as confusing as examples or elaboration, so it's often easier to ask for clarification on part(s) of a definition to get a clearer definition.
                • ^ People often don't provide a definition.
                  • They often perform attempted anticipation

              • Reflecting for feedback

                • Definition

                  • Restating what someone says and asking if you've correctly understood what they said.

                  • Distinction

                    • "Reflecting for feedback" is distinct from reflecting for other reasons. Sometimes people will often restate things just because it feels natural to them to converse that way. This tends to be a form of useless fluff, and as a result we don't recommend it.

                • Importance / Limitations

                  • When used correctly, this can be a useful technique for understand what other people mean.

                  • Slow (major)

                    • This tends to slow down the conversation a lot, since you have to think about and rewrite what they said using different wording each time you perform this technique.
                    • Asking for definition(s), elaboration or an example is often quicker.

                  • Unreliable feedback (major)

                    • They might answer "yes" even when you haven't quite correctly understood what they meant.
                      Likelihood
                      • Especially if they don't have the best reading comprehension or if they aren't accounting for some subtle detail(s).
                    • They might answer "no" even if you have correctly understood what they meant.
                      Likelihood
                      • Because they might not understand how what you said is ultimately synonymous with what they said, because they don't have the best reading comprehension or they aren't accounting for some inbetween steps or something.
                    • This is especially problematic because it can give a false sense of understanding, which can lead to much worse misunderstandings down the road.
                      Relevance

              • Related

                • Definitions

                • Definitional differences

            • Requesting elaboration

              • Definition (broad)

              • Importance / limitations

                • Difficult

                  • We sometimes have habits to ask for elaboration broadly, without specifying what should be elaborated on.
                    • Problematic because [...]
                      Relevance

              • Directed questions

                • It's usually best to ask for elaboration on some specific element, rather than with no specification of what should be elaborated.
                • ^ Because asking for elaboration with no specification of what should be elaborated on usually requires the other person(s) to spend longer coming up with said elaboration, and makes their elaboration much more likely to be inadequate.

            • Requesting example(s)/analogies

              • Importance / limitations

                • Common

                  • It's very common for people to ask for examples in order to better understand something.

                • Impactful

                  • It's very easy to accidentally use analogies that are compatible with multiple mutually exclusive plausibly-intended definitions

            • Related

              • Checking for different axioms

          • Implications

            • Definition

            • Importance

            • Heavily relying on implied premises

              • Definition

                • Avoiding explicitly mentioning many premises that one's relying on.
                  • "Many" relative to how many premises others tend to mention.
                • Especially premises that one is unsure that they'll need to mention, but that could plausibly be useful to mention.

              • Impactful

                • Common

                  • Many people do it without even realizing it, so it's likely more common than it seems.
                  • People often forget the reasons for their opinions and work their way back to them in real time as the discussion progresses.

                • Impactful

                  • Feels like bad reasoning: This often results in others feeling like the reasoning is bad.

                  • ^ It feels ad hoc
                    Likelihood

                    • It seems as though much of the reasoning is created on the spot.
                      Likelihood
                      • When using this approach, a lot of reasoning is often not given until requested or a new counter needs addressed.
                        Likelihood
                      • During discussions, people often fail to account for the possibility that other person(s) are heavily relying on implied premises.
                        Likelihood
                        • i.e. Not revealing or recalling all their points immediately.
                    • It being ad hoc makes it seem as though the reasoning is much less reliable than it could otherwise seem.
                      Relevance
                      • If it was created on the spot, it couldn't have been thought through very much.
                        Likelihood
                      • When one is using ad hoc reasoning, their confidence often feels disproportionate to their evidence, given the limited support provided for their claims.
                        Relevance | Maybe create an ad hoc section and put this there, and link it here.
                        • This often makes it seem as though the person using this technique is more over-confident and close-minded than they are.
                          Relevance
                          • This often causes others to grow over-confident that the person using this technique isn't worth taking seriously on the discussion topic.
                            • This causes others to be much more close-minded about it.
                          • This seeming often doesn't go away even once sufficient evidence is given.
                            • Meant to be about the parent claim, even though the link leads elsewhere. Link given because of its children.
                      • The feeling often doesn't go away even once sufficient evidence is given.
                        Relevance

                        • Confirmation bias

                          • Defensiveness

                            • "If someone is using ad hoc reasoning, they shouldn't be able to beat me!"

                        • Myside bias

                          • Defensiveness

                            • "If someone is using ad hoc reasoning, they shouldn't be able to beat me!"

              • How to view / strategies

                • Doing it yourself

                  • State that you're using it: You can explicitly state that you have a lot of reasoning that you'll present only after the relevant counters have been given.
                    • This is likely going to be difficult to remember to do, so you might want to practice it and ensure that it will become a habit.
                  • State why: To make it more memorable that you're doing it, and to somewhat avoid them feeling like you're stupid for doing it, you can explain why you're doing it.
                    • Eg. To save time by efficiently give only the relevant info to the person you're discussing the topic with.
                  • Act less confident: By acting less confident than you actually are while using this technique, you can avoid these issues.
                  • Don't do it: Without accounting for others assuming the worst when you use this technique, it's generally more efficient to use it. But at the cost of that efficiency, you can avoid problems caused by it by simply not using it.
                    • To avoid using this technique, do more argument premoves and go a layer or so deeper in your reasoning, even if it involves accounting for responses than the opponent might not give.
                    • ^ Just be careful not to account for things that you know none of the other person(s) present would argue/think, as this is likely addressing irrelevant reasoning.
                      • This is bad for reasons that might not be immediately apparent if you haven't visited the link.
                        Relevance

                • Others doing it

                  • Due to the propensity for people to use this technique, it is very important to account for this by keeping in mind that people very often have deeper reasoning that they haven't presented yet, which may justify their position in a way we hadn't considered.

                  • Related

                    • Steelmanning

          • Analogies

            • Definition

              • Figures of speech that compare two different things to highlight a shared relationship or principle.
              • Its purpose is usually to explain a complex or unfamiliar concept by comparing it to something simple and well-known.

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Impactful

                • Limitations of analogical reasoning

                • Limitations of [definitions via analogies]

            • Limitations

            • Examples

              • Definition

                • A specific instance or case that is used to illustrate a general rule or clarify an idea.

                • Type of analogy?

                  • While examples are not typically regarded as a type of analogy, they always implicitly involve a comparison of two different things (the specific case with the general principle) to highlight a shared relationship (that the specific case belongs to the general rule's domain).
                  • Therefore, at least for the purposes of this guide with the definitions provided, examples are considered a type of analogy.

              • Importance / Limitations

                • Impactful

                  • Examples tend to be a very good way to get a broad intuition of roughly what something means / how something works.
                  • Examples tend to leave a concept somewhat vaguely defined in a manner that can easily result in misinterpretations.

              • Detailed examples

                • Definition

                  • Examples that are detailed, typically enough so to make it clear what the relevant attributes of the specific case are, and what general rule it's being claimed to be a part of.

                • Importance / Limitations

                  • Impactful

                    • Good for catching/conveying details that wouldn't otherwise be thought of even with vague examples
                      • For very important issues, taking the extra time to carefully account for things like this is often worthwhile.
                      • More often than not, vague examples or no example whatsoever is sufficient to catch/convey all the important details.
                    • Often quite time consuming to produce.
                      • It depends on the circumstance how much time is required to come up with a detailed example, there are exceptions.

                • Techniques

                  • Anticipating misinterpretations

                    • In order to give a sufficiently detailed example to reliably demonstrate a target general principle, it's typically ideal to try to anticipate possible ways in which an example could be misinterpreted to be about a different relevant general principle.

              • Vague examples

                • Definition

                  • Examples that are vague, typically enough so to leave some room for plausibly misinterpretation of either some relevant properties of the specific case, or what general rule/category the specific case is meant to fall under.
                  • Example
                    • Trolley problem
                      • It's not very realistic or detailed, but it's an example scenario that's (typically) targetted at comparing consequentialism and deontology.

                • Importance / Limitations

                  • Common

                    • It's very common for people to give vague examples.

                  • Impactful

                    • Vague examples can usually be thought up pretty quickly.
                      • Vague examples are usually easy to come up with.
                    • Vague examples tend to be very easily misinterpreted.
                      Link a section about the consequences of misunderstandings.
                    • Some people refuse to accept vague hypotheticals.
                      Link to a section on accepting the hypothetical in hypothetical thinking or something.

            • Ostensive definitions

              • Definition

                • Definitions which convey the meaning of a term by pointing out examples.
                • They assume the questioner has sufficient understanding to recognize the type of information being given.
                • They are usually accompanied with a gesture pointing to the object serving as an example, and for this reason is also often referred to as "definition by pointing".

              • Importance / Limitations

                • Common

                  • Common when a term is difficult to define verbally.
                    • Very common when teaching someone a language.
                      • eg. a child or a non-native speaker.
                    • Common when the nature of the term intrinsically makes it very difficult to define straightforwardly.
                      • Eg. colors, sensations.

                • Impactful

                  • Useful when the nature of the thing being described makes it very difficult to describe with other terms.
                    • A color itself.

                  • Often leads to misunderstandings

                    • It's very easy to accidentally use analogies that are compatible with multiple mutually exclusive plausibly-intended definitions.
                      • Especially when the concept is very abstract, like in philosophy, and cannot simply be pointed to.
                    • The risk of misunderstandings can be somewhat mitigated by using it *alongside* a more explicit definition.
                      • Although this often wastes time if the explicit definition was sufficient.

              • You should probably try to avoid it where possible

                • Most people interested enough in reasoning to be reading this guide probably intend to have a lot of atypical abstract conversations.
                  • It's often a bad technique to make a habit of using in such conversations.
                  • ^ It often leads to misunderstandings in such conversations

              • Related

                • Vague definitions

                  • Using analogies to indicate a definition tends to result in the definition being left vague.
                    • Using analogies to indicate a definition tends to allow for multiple possible interpretations by defualt, which means that the definition is left vague.

              • Source

                • Mostly taken from the wikipedia page.

            • Related

              • Analogical reasoning

              • Definition via analogy

          • Lacking context

            • Definition

              • Lacking important information for how to interpret thing(s).

            • Importance

                • Definition

                  • When short messages/posts are used, some important context is almost bound to be omitted.

                • Harder to be thorough

          • Steelmanning

            • Definition

              • The dialectical technique of restating an opponent's argument in its strongest, most charitable, and/or most convincing form before offering a counterargument.

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Common

                • People frequently make major mistakes due to using an unideal version of it.
                • People frequently strawman eachother and this is a technique that (if used properly) helps with avoiding strawmanning.

              • Impactful

                • If used well, it can be very handy for properly understanding other people.
                  • Being able to properly understand others is very important for finding the truth
                • Often involves confirmatory thought

            • What to avoid doing

              • Overly literal interpretations

                • Importance

                  • Impactful

                    • Often when taking into account the context, a clear line of reasoning can be followed and you can see where people are making small mistakes in expressing what they mean, which helps you understand their position, avoid misunderstanding key aspects of it, and makes the discussion more productive and makes it more likely that you'll reach a consensus with them on the matter.
                      Link this to understanding and maybe some other places.

                  • Common

                    • Very often, people will interpret the arguments that other people make too literally.

              • Strength over all else

                • Definition

                  • Representing others' statement(s) in the strongest possible way (in the opinion of the representer).

                  • Distinction

                    • This conceptually differs from representing others' statements in the strongest plausibly intended way, or anything similar.
                    • ^ It is thus typically not the most convincing interpretation.

                • Importance / Limitations

                  • Impactful

                    • This kind of interpretation can run completely obviously contrary to what the statement maker could have plausibly intended, or what they said.
                      • The motivation for this often comes from a thought process like "I addressed an even stronger version of your position than you gave".
                      • Oftentimes we do not know whether the opponent's position is stronger before we argue against it and see what their counterarguments are.
                        Relevance
                        • So by trying to form a stronger position for them before we've tried to address their stated one, we can easily fail to address the actual strongest position.
                          Relevance

            • Related

              • Reading between the lines

              • Expression/interpretation

                • One of the most important parts of adequately representing (and thus one of the most important parts of actually addressing) others' positions is in having good expression/interpretation techniques/knowledge.
                  • So incredibly often, disagreements and misunderstandings arise simply because of people using subtly different definitions and can't resolve it (at least in a timely manner)

              • Always assume you're wrong

              • https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=QLfbU4dDp6Me-Vt_xT5qvkwk

                • Argument premoves are a very common technique used when attempting to steelman others.

          • Checking for different axioms

            • Definition

              • Checking (usually by asking) if other person(s) have different axiom(s).

            • Importance

              • It often takes a while before a disagreement from differing axioms can be recognized as such naturally.
                • Axioms are typically at the end of the argument lines and are often not brought up even if you try to dig all the way down into someone else (s)' epistemology.

          • Attempted anticipation

            • Definition

              • The process of attempting to anticipate what other people mean, how other people are getting confused, etc. and address that directly.

            • Importance / Limitations
              ~~Advanced

              • Common

                • People very often try to anticipate what other people are saying in some form or another.

              • Impactful

                • Can save time if done well.
                • Often involves prioritizing anticipation over providing clear definitions, examples, etc.
                  • Very often wastes a lot of time because they often can't properly anticipate it.
                    • The ideas of the other person(s) are often too different to easily anticipate.
                    • Often involves an over-reliance on heavy heuristic usage which makes it harder to anticipate unfamiliar ideas.
                  • Even when the person has explicitly asked for a clearer definition, example, etc.
                    • Often it's so deeply embedded in their habits that it's difficult to resist attempted anticipation
                    • Often they are so reliant on heavy heuristic usage that they don't really have a more fundamental form of reasoning they can default to in order to more easily avoid their flawed habits
                  • Even when the person has explicitly requested that they don't perform attempted anticipation.
                    • Often it's so deeply embedded in their habits that it's difficult to resist attempted anticipation.
                    • Often they are so reliant on heavy heuristic usage that they don't really have a more fundamental form of reasoning they can default to in order to more easily avoid their flawed habits.
                • Extremely prone to overconfident assumptions about what others mean.
                • It's very easy to go too far with this, resulting in strawmanning.
                • Extremely prone to confirmatory thought

            • How to view it

              • A technique to be very careful with.
              • Usually best to avoid prioritizing it over asking for clear definitions, examples, etc. when possible.
              • Typically best to only use it for situations where it's likely very easy and simple to anticipate what others mean.

          • Translating natural language to formal logic

            • Definition (broad)

              • This includes partial translations, such as merely mentally translating the rough structure.

              • Examples

                • Because of these, that has to be true.
                • If this then that, otherwise this.
                • All of the above is true so this means…
                • This is the only option that makes sense.
                • If we assume that this is true we get a ridiculous result so it can’t be true.

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Impactful

                • Being able to better understand the logical structure of natural language arguments is extremely useful.
                  • Being able to explicitly translate natural language into formal logic often allows for better understanding the logical structure of natural language.
                    Relevance
                • Typically very time consuming, so generally best to reserve this for more important problems.

            • Related

              • Standard form

        • Expression

          • Definition (broad)

            • The art and study of using language effectively and persuasively to inform, influence, or motivate.

          • Importance

            • Common

              • Rhetoric underlies virtually all communication.

            • Impactful

              • Being able to be properly understood by others is extremely important for finding the truth.

          • Semantics

          • Thinking before speaking

            • Definition (specific)

              • Thinking more thoroughly before expressing your thoughts.
              • Distinction: Doesn't necessarily have to be vocally, could be thinking before sending a message, etc.

            • Importance

            • Critical emotional mindset

              • Definition

                • An emotional mindset in which you're very skeptical of the statement(s) in question.

              • Importance

                • Impactful

                  • Our emotions typically aren't by default in an ideal state for critical analysis.

              • Techniques

                • Dissociating the statement from yourself

                  • Importance

                    • Helps with utilizing habits you formed for investigating other peoples' statements.
                      • Those habits tend to be more critical than the ones meant for investigating your own statements.
                  • Imagine that someone else (especially someone untrustworthy) said the statement.
                    • "Untrustworthy", to help with assuming it's wrong

                • Assuming it's wrong

                  • Definition (specific)

                    • Imagining that you know for certain that it's wrong, but don't know how.

                  • Importance

                    • This helps with really dedicating one's self to trying to figure out how it could be wrong.
                      • When we feel like we don't know if a statement is wrong, we typically don't check it very thoroughly.

            • Getting into the habit

              • Definition

                • Getting into the habit of thinking more thoroughly before expressing your thoughts.

              • Importance

                • This is an incredibly important habit to have for <broken link>

              • Related

                • <broken link>

          • Fluff

            • Definition

              • Material that has (almost) no epistemic impact on the relevant reasoning at hand.
                • I.e. It provides virtually no information about what's true in the relevant reasoning.

              • Distinction

                • Different from eg: person X says thing(s) to make person(s) Y feel better so that person(s) Y can reason better.
                • ^ While we would consider this to be a form of fluff, it is not the definition of fluff, since it is specifically a form of useful fluff.

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Common

                • It's extremely common for people to use at least a little of this in what they say.
                • It's virtually impossible to completely avoid.

              • Impactful

                • Typically for important reasoning, fluff should be kept close to a minimum.

            • How to view it

              • Typically for important reasoning, fluff should be kept close to a minimum.

            • Useless fluff

              • Definition

              • Importance / Limitations

                • Common

                  • Fluff tends to be almost if not entirely useless.

                • Impactful

                  • Wastes time: It usually wastes time that could be used on more important calculations.
                    • If the fluff is made optional to view with sufficient indication of its uselessness, it may only waste the writer's time.
                    • ^ In situations where the writer does not know if something is useless (eg. argument premoves), it may be beneficial to include it just in case. Though it's usually advisable in these situations to include some indication of what a section's relevance is conditional on.
                  • Contributes to overconfidence
                    • Useless fluff often gives the feeling of having addressed more points than it actually does.
                      Likelihood
                      • Thus often making one feel overconfident about rejecting what they believe they addressed.
                        Relevance

              • Primary causes

                • Unsystematic reasoning

              • Pointless repetition

                • Definition

                • Importance / Limitations

              • Addressing irrelevant reasoning

                • Definition

                  • Addressing reasoning that's irrelevant to the present person(s) reasoning.

                • Importance / Limitations

                  • Impactful

                    • It often feels like supporting one's perspective or justifying it to the other present person(s) when it actually isn't.
                      • Addressing positions that one feels the other person(s) might hold often feels like being thorough, which can easily make one feel like they addressed other person(s) reasoning better than they actually did.
                        Likelihood
                        • This can easily lead to overconfidence.
                          Relevance
                      • This often leads to believing that one has supported their perspective or justified it to the other present person(s) when they haven't, which is usually a form of strawmanning.
                        Relevance
                        • This can easily lead to overconfidence.
                          Relevance
                      • Addressing positions that one feels the other person(s) might hold often feels like addressing positions that they actually hold.
                        Likelihood | Unsure about this one
                    • It can often make it feel more like you're not properly listening to/remembering/understanding the person(s) who the reasoning is not relevant for.
                      • This can make the person feel like their reasoning isn't being accounted for, which can make them more close-minded about the matter.
                        • Feeling like the reasoning isn't being addressed can make the person feel like their emotions are being disregarded, which can make them more frustrated towards the one who's addressing irrelevant reasoning.
                          Link to frustration section
                          • Link to section of frustration about increasing short-term general close-mindedness.

                • Related

                  • Argument premoves

                    • The most common cause of "addressing irrelevant reasoning".

            • Useful fluff

              • Definition

              • Importance / Limitations

              • Emotional softening

                • Adding extra fluff can at times act as padding that makes the concepts more emotionally tenable.

              • Entertainment

                • Even purely from the perspective of getting people to do the best reasoning possible on a subject, presenting it in an entertaining manner is important. And inserting the right kind of fluff can contribute to that entertainment in many cases.

              • Over-explaining

                • Importance

                  • Can be useful to make it clearer for people who can't focus or think well.

                • Nuance

                  • In many cases it's better if this it's optional whether or not one gets the over-explaining.

            • Related

              • Monologuing for long periods at once

          • Changing the topic

            • Definition

              • Changing the focus of the discussion.

            • Deep lines

              • Definition

              • Importance

                • Impactful

                  • The topic seeming to shift in deep lines is common and not necessarily a bad thing, as oftentimes relevant and important details will be in deep lines that seem to run far into very different topic(s).

                • Common

                  • Oftentimes when exploring deep lines, the topic being focused on will seem to change because the important details being discussed will largely involve a different topic.

              • Limitations

                • The instability of large reasoning pyramids

              • How to view them

                • Often important to explore, even when it seems like the topic shifts.
                  • So allow others to change the topic, as long as they are still bringing up sufficiently relevant and important points.
                    • Even if the relevance involves a long line of reasoning that involves very different subject(s)).

            • Persuasion

          • Persuasion

            • Definition

              • Changing other people's minds.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Prioritizing or building habits around persuasion tends to reduce one's reasoning abilities.
                  • This section teaches how to avoid such pitfalls.
                  • Prioritizing persuasion tends to lead to prioritizing persuasion habits over reasoning habits.
                    • The best habits for persuasion are often mutually exclusive with the best habits for reasoning
                  • The best habits for persuasion are often mutually exclusive with the best habits for reasoning.
                • Being irrationally persuaded via these strategies can be quite bad.
                • Persuading people to do irrational things that are significantly beneficial for your goals is extremely useful.
                • <broken link>, so persuasion strategies separate from good reasoning practices tend to work best for persuading the vast majority of people.
                  • Especially rhetoric, stumping the opponent and charisma.
                    • Stumping the opponent has some indication of good reasoning, but not as much as you might think.
                      • Stumping the opponent is much easier with rhetoric and fallacies than with good reasoning/solid sources
                  • Avoiding risky opinions (even if they are more reasonable).
                • <broken link>, so persuasion is often difficult

              • Common

                • Marketing

                  • Advertising

                • Politics

            • Limitations

            • Broad

              • Acting confident
                Maybe change to "behaving confident", if so then change backlinks accordingly.

                • Definition ('acting' distinction)

                  • Behaving confidently, as though one is confident.

                  • Distinctions

                    • Acting: Not necessarily a form of faking confidence.
                      • One could be acting confident because of genuine confidence.
                    • Different from actually being confident.
                      • Although the two are correlated.

                • Importance

                  • Often effective in persuasion, by frequently making unsuspecting people feel like one must have very good info / reasoning to justify their confidence.
                    • Important to learn how to avoid being influenced by this.
                      Relevance
                  • Contributes to epistemic self-overconfidence.
                    • Acting confident is correlated with confident claims, which contributes to epistemic self-overconfidence.
                      Likelihood
                    • Likely important to learn to avoid, because of the issues with epistemic self-overconfidence.
                      Relevance

                • Related

                  • Confidence

                    • Acting confident is correlated with confidence for obvious reasons.

                  • Appeal to authority

                    • Somewhat similar and often paired with this.

              • Understand who you are persuading
                Link to relevant psychology sections

                • Understand their positions

                  • Definition

                    • Esure you understand their relevant positions.

                  • Importance

                    • Impactful

                      • Without doing this, it's extremely easy to use premises that they don't accept, or fail to use useful premises which they do accept.

                  • Understanding their values

                    • Definition

                      • Understand their relevant values.

                    • Importance

                      • Improvable

                        • It's very common for people to frequently presume (with inadequate evidence) that other people share their own values.

              • Avoiding pure reasoning

                • Definition

                  • Avoiding primarily relying on reasoning and counterarguments (however reasonable) to counter other(s) opinions.

                • Why?

                  • The reasons that people give for their positions (especially on matters of "soft" reasoning) are often rationalizations and the products of https://dynalist.io/d/nBfl0bh1yEuK5cktkexuu8Xd#z=OrIjqF9wWhAuAxUvnk078FOy
                    • Thus they will typically unconsciously assume that they are right and that you are wrong with a very high degree of confidence before even considering your reasoning.
                    • So when they investigate the counterarguments that you present, they will be looking for any ways in which they could be wrong, even the tiniest hints. Because they "know" that you're wrong and that they are right, they "just have to find out how".
                    • It's very rare for someone to run out of tiny hints to pursue, or to gain the self-awareness to realize how flawed their epistemic process is.
                      • So they will typically continue looking for things until they either:
                      • ^ Forget how their initial reasons were flawed (and thus start repeating arguments that you already addressed)
                      • ^ Or feel too stuck from not being able to justify themselves to continue (they'll get a very strong desire to resolve the tension and end the discussion, and since they can't address your reasoning, they'll end it another way).

              • Don't begin with the conclusion

                • Why?

                  • Starting with your conclusion makes it much easier for people to engage in confirmatory thought
                • Instead go as far as you can with reasoning, bringing them along on a journey they don't know the exact destination of.
                  Pull from/link to a section somewhere where I talked about this.

              • Intuition pull

                • Definition

                  • A statement made primarily for convincing intuition(s).

                  • Distinction

                    • It doesn't necessarily have to use epistemic justification for convincing the intuition(s).

                • Importance

                  • Importance of convincing your subconscious to agree with you conscious beliefs

              • Ambiguity

                • Definition

                  • Ambiguity as a persuasion technique.

                • Importance

                  • Impactful

                    • Allows for appealing to a larger subset of people.

              • Consistency

                • Definition

                  • Consistency of one's opinions.
                  • Consistency both across time and across different beliefs.

                • Importance

                  • Common

                    • Inconsistency is one of the most noticable things to most people.

                  • Impactful

                    • Inconsistency is one of the biggest deal-breakers for most people. Hypocrites are quite hated.

                • Ambiguity

                  • Expressing things in an ambiguous way allows one to remain consistent with their previous statements despite changing their mind.

              • Biased simplification

                • Definition

                  • When a subject is simplified in a way that is substantially biased towards particular option(s).

                • Importance

                  • Impactful

                    • Particularly impactful when misleading people to agree with detailed proposals based on the biased simplification(s).

              • Characterization

                • Definition

                  • Characterizing what something looks like by talking about a specific hypothetical or example that feels realistic and gets person(s) immersed in a scenario.
                  • Typically: To emotionally push a certain idea.

                • Importance

                  • Common

                  • Impactful

                    • This is something that most people are affected by.

              • Gish galloping

                • Related

                  • Monologuing for long periods of time

              • Eloquence

              • When things go wrong, wait a while before trying again

                • Emotional associations (like all associations) fade over time

              • Small talk -> Deep talk

                • Definition

                  • The transition from small talk to deep talk.

                • Importance

                  • Useful for more efficiently getting to know people better, which can allow for getting to know more people better, which can increase the odds of finding better people.

                • Ask about them

                  • Definition

                    • Ask about the other person(s) in a positive or neutral way.

                  • Importance

                    • People usually like to talk about themselves, so people will often be open to talking about themselves, which usually allows for getting the conversation into a deeper and more meaningful place.

          • Accounting for other(s)' abilities

            • Definition

              • Accounting for other(s) abilities when driving the discussion forward.

            • Giving more/less explanation

              • Probably largely based on knowledge/criticalness.

          • Following your thought associations

            • Definition

              • Following your thought associations while expressing your thoughts.

            • Limitations

              • This can easily lead to lots of useless tangents.

            • Types

              • Instead of structuring it and focusing on long-term conversation goals.
              • With the intent to try to bring it back to the main topic, but without a clear idea of how to do that at first.

          • Monologuing for long periods at once
            Specific to vocal.

            • Limitations

              • Can easily lead to not being able to hear clarification or nuance that the person(s) listening could give.

            • Related

              • Fluff

                • Speaking for long periods of time tends to be the result of adding a lot of fluff to what's being said.

              • Gish galloping
                Definitely not exactly the same, but loosely related.

          • Committing to falsification condition(s)
            Shorten the name

            • Definition

              • Explicitly committing to condition(s) under which the commiter would consider a hypothesis falsified.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • Helpful for making it easier to change your mind without losing face.
                • Helpful for turning the common desire for making "strong" statements (confident-sounding ones) into a boon by directing it towards making a commitment on what conditions would make you change your mind.

              • Definition

                • An attack on a person at the core of their character. In effect, dismantling their whole being with very insufficient (if any) guidance on how to improve.

          • (Un)conservative claims

            • Definition

              • Claims and how broad and/or confident they are.

            • Importance

              • Impactful

                • It's important to form good habits about what kind of claims to make, to be more open minded
                • Useful for persuasion
                  ~~Expert

            • Unconservative claims

              • Confident claims

                • Importance

                  • Typically very important to avoid due to limitations
                  • Can be useful for persuasion
                    ~~Adept, support

                • Limitations

                  • Even if we are justified in having more confident beliefs about more major claims, doing so is often not ideal because it "closes the doors behind us". If we're pushed back with evidence, we emotionally have nowhere to go and are often prone to get defensive and more likely to refuse the truth. (Source)
                    • Video chosen because it's the best quick and straightforward demonstration I'm aware of, not because of the atheist angle the video creator chooses to take with it.

              • Major claims

                • Definition

                  • Claims which are very broad.

                  • Example

                    • All squares have four 90° angles.
                    • All Nazis were 100% evil.
                    • 2 + 2 always equals 4.

                • Importance

                  • It's very important to be able to discriminate from justified major claims and unjustified major claims, as the can often appear far more or less justified than they really are.
                  • ^ Typically because of failing to adequately account for the prior probability

                • Limitations

                  • Often mistaken

                    • It's rare that practical things are entirely black and white / all or nothing, usually (unless dealing with purely abstract concepts or something) there is some nuance/exception.

                  • Encourages defensiveness

                    • Major claims are often made (or seem to be made) confidently.

                      • Confident claims

                      • ^ Made more damaging by the fact that major claims are often mistaken

                • How to view

                  • Aside from statements like this where you have a solid deductive proof to back the broad claims up, or in situations where the evidence is quite overwhelming, it is usually best to avoid making such major claims.
                  • And even in the exceptional cases where you're likely right, it is typically best to speak less confidently than you feel, so as to make the process of changing your mind more comfortable if the situation happens to call for it.

                • Justified major claims

                  • Deductive proofs

                  • Overwhelming evidence

                    • Overwhelming evidence for the generalization being very reliable

            • Related

              • Distinguishing likelihood and relevance

          • Argument premoves

            • Definition

              • Attempting to account for possible counterarguments before they are given.

            • Importance / limitations

              • Common

                • At least small examples of this are very common.

              • Impactful

                • It often feels like addressing relevant position(s) when it actually isn't.
                  • Limitations of addressing irrelevant reasoning
                • Can save time
                  • You really have to know what you're doing to consistently save time.
                  • It can also waste a lot of time from issues resulting from addressing irrelevant reasoning

            • Recommended alternative

              • It's usually better to ask what one believes and mostly only respond to what you can be very confident is their position.
              • ^ Limitations of addressing irrelevant reasoning

            • Related

              • Addressing irrelevant reasoning

                • A very common downside of argument premoves.

              • Steelmanning

                • Argument premoves are a very common technique used when attempting to steelman others.

          • Inflammatory statements

            • Definition

              • Provocative or emotionally charged remarks.
              • Often: Intended to provoke strong reactions (typically negative), often anger or outrage.

            • Importance / Limitations

              • Common

              • Impactful

                • Using inflammatory statements often causes people to launch inflammatory statements back, intended to make you feel a similarly negative way.
                  • If these attempts are even remotely successful, it's typically an epistemic problem because it could easily cause you to misunderstand and/or unintentionally strawman their position.
                    Link how mood (especially anger) effects our reasoning.
                    • We often believe that it wouldn't influence us when it actually would.
                      Try to find the study on how people cared about what other people thought of them even when they expected otherwise.
                  • This can lead you to further dehumanize them and take what they say even less seriously, often less than make sense.

          • Informal arguments

            • Definition

              • Arguments which are given in ordinary language.

            • Importance

              • Common

                • Most arguments are informal, as opposed to formal.

              • Difficult

                • In an informal argument, it is often difficult to see what the premises and the conclusion are.
                  • Informal arguments often contains material which is inessential to the argument.
                    • Much of what people say or write has a rhetorical purpose.

            • Related

              • As opposed to standard form arguments

          • Related

            • Conflations

            • Fitting things together

              • Mentally fitting concepts together in a logical way is crucial for being able to explain your position to people who are not accustomed to your mental map of concepts.
                • You may need to explain it in completely different terms and in relation to completely different concepts than you're accustomed to.
                • ^ Being able to follow connections to those alternative areas is very important for this.

            • Examples

            • Analogies

            • Heavily relying on implied premises
